[Title Page: Astrolabe Usage]
THE USAGE OF BOTH THE PARTICULAR AND UNIVERSAL ASTROLABE. Explained both in the declaration of their parts, and in a faithful and easy exposition of their practice in Astronomy and Geometry. And all accommodated to the small treatises of the Sphere, of the Astrolabe and of the Geometric Square by Dominique Jacquinot, Champenois, by the same means corrected, augmented, and restored to a better order. D. A. L. G. IN PARIS, At Jean Moreau's, Saint Jacques street, at the White Cross. M. DC. XXV. WITH ROYAL PRIVILEGE.

[To the Reader]
TO THE READER. My Reader, Having recognized for a long time that curiosity led many people not only to seek a great quantity of diverse mathematical instruments, but also to wish among several of the same construction and manufacture to be able to find different sizes to have some more portable than others, I have always striven, as far as possible, to satisfy the curiosity of both, and with much effort I have sought out all sorts of instruments to retrieve them from places where they lay useless, and to give them to the public. But recognizing that the number of those made of solid material such as brass, copper, silver, and others is very small, and the price excessive, and furthermore that it is difficult to obtain them, and that they can only be communicated to a few people, I have sought a way to at least partially satisfy all those who would ask me for them, and on this occasion I have gathered what I could find of engraved plates of diverse figures of Mathematical Instruments, such as the Astrolabe, Cylinder, Radiometer, Graphometer, Trigonometer, Horologes, and others, so that by having a large number of copies printed, the thing could be common to all. And with the same intention, seeing that many employed me to procure for them Spheres whose composition and division were not as readily available as they might have desired, to remedy this, I had some engraved on copper plates of different sizes to offer a choice to everyone according to their curiosity. This convenience pleased them so much that they have since wished for similar ones for Astrolabes, which are recognized among mathematical instruments, especially in Astronomy, to easily hold one of the foremost places. But the matter being long and costly, I have for a long time deferred the undertaking until, as if by importunity one had wished to engage me, several asking me for them, finally the desire to serve the public led me to seek the means to be able to give them satisfaction on this subject. And seeing that most of the Astrolabes we have had until now, even those that the late Sieur Danfrie engraved, of which I have all the plates in my possession, are beginning to deviate from the concordance and relation they previously had with the celestial movements, I have decided to have two engraved that are more portable, indeed I dare say that they will be found more accurate and precise in their divisions than many others larger, which will also have this advantage (besides being according to the reformation of the Calendar) that, as I have communicated the calculation to some of my friends sufficiently versed in this science, they will agree precisely with the celestial movements of the present year 1615. So that where most of the others are presently different from the said movements by long years, these two of ours will not differ in anything, and will be able to serve for a number of subsequent years without sensible difference. Which I also hope to finally procure for the said Astrolabes of Danfrie, by augmenting them with some plates, and having a part of them re-engraved anew to make them, at the choice and will of the curious, conform to our two aforesaid ones. But as I desire to serve all, so I have not had the said Astrolabes engraved only for those who already have knowledge of them, but also, and principally, for those who seek and desire to have it. And for this purpose, I had compiled a summary of the particular Usage of the said Astrolabes, which desiring to communicate to them, I judged it appropriate, so as not to oblige them to seek other more ample treatises, to add it to the treatise that Dominique Jacquinot had previously had printed, which having been, by a second impression, although after a review of the whole work, as much soiled and tarnished by new errors, as little clarified in many places, it seemed to me that it could be given back to the public more corrected and more ample: more corrected, especially by removing what the authors of the said second impression had improperly added: and more ample, by explaining what is found most obscure there, and by adding what can serve the subject, and has been omitted by the Author. But it happened to me that, being on the verge of sending it to press, a third impression fell into my hands, corrected and augmented by a certain David Robert, which nevertheless, upon examination, I found in many places similar to the second, and still having many things to reform, of which I judged it appropriate to warn you, as I have done, and you will be able to recognize it in its place, not that it is my aim to simply give you, by this fourth impression, Jacquinot's book either more corrected or more ample: but inasmuch as I will choose it as the briefest on this subject, to add to it and distribute more conveniently what concerns the particular Usage of our small Astrolabes both for Astronomy and Geometry, I believed that it was in your interest, and mine, to note in passing both the old errors followed by the last two impressions, and those that each of them contributed. Besides this, you will find some difference in the order of the propositions that I have arranged in such a way that the subsequent ones are always dependent on the preceding ones, so that, following the natural and necessary order of doctrine, the passage is always made and given from known things to unknown ones. And in the explanation of the said propositions, I have dwelt a little longer than usual, I hope that this length will not be found annoying to those who, little versed in the practice of this noble instrument, will undoubtedly be very pleased that I entertain them a little longer on the operations, to instruct them, and to form them (as this has been my principal aim) by familiar and intelligible discourses, leading them by a smooth and easy path, and guiding them as by the hand to turn them away from the impediments and obstacles they might otherwise often encounter, showing them the inconveniences that often arise, and the means to avoid them. In short, in expounding this double treatise of Astronomy and Geometry (namely of the Astrolabe considered simply as a Planisphere, and of the Geometric Square) I have endeavored to note all the precautions of which I could think, and which must be taken and observed in the practice of both: which no one, that I know of, has yet done, and which I could not do to make myself intelligible to the less advanced, for whom I principally work, except by speaking to them with a somewhat extended discourse, clarified and confirmed by examples. Receive all this in as good a part as you have passionately desired it, and (to my great regret, by the idleness of the engravers, too long awaited) and that with heart and affection it has been offered by me. Jean Moreau, Bookseller.

[Treatise on the Material Sphere]
TREATISE ON THE MATERIAL SPHERE, Containing a brief and succinct declaration of the principal circles comprised therein. BECAUSE the Astrolabe, or Planisphere, of which we wish to treat, is nothing other than the celestial sphere placed and described in a flat figure. It is therefore appropriate and necessary in this place to declare and demonstrate the circles described on its surface, together with the distinction of their names and parts: in order to have an easier understanding of what will be said in our Astrolabe. Such is the text of the first impression of D. Jacquinot that the Auditors of the second (which is false and changed inappropriately at the very beginning, as a sample of their ignorance) have altered in many places, and for the most part in the third impression of David Robert. There are three kinds of Planispheres, namely two general or universal ones, and a third particular one of universal planispheres: one is called that of Gemma Frison, the other of Jean de Rojas, not because they were the first Authors or inventors, the thing being much older, but because each of them made a particular treatise of their composition and their use. Such universal Planispheres are always similar, and serve generally for all kinds of places. As for the particular one, it varies as much as one can imagine different horizons in the Sphere, and according to what one may need, because each particular horizon must have its particular Planisphere, which cannot serve other different horizons; And it is of this last kind of Planispheres that the said Astrolabe of Jacquinot is composed, and most of the others such as those of Stöffler, Bassantin, Roebius, Danfrie, and our small ones, to which, to further extend their use, several particular planispheric plates are added to serve several and different horizons. It must therefore be understood that the Heavens have two principal motions: one which is from West through South to East, contrary to the primum mobile, and comprises in itself the motions of both the fixed stars and the seven planets, for this contains eight different motions, which occur in various spaces of time: for the firmament, according to Ptolemy, completes its revolution in 36,000 years, which is one degree in 100 years. And the Sphere of Saturn, which is the next planet to the said firmament, completes its revolution in 30 years, that of Jupiter in 12 years, that of Mars in 1 year, the Sun, Venus, and Mercury, each in 1 year. The Sphere of the Moon in 27 days and 8 hours. This period of Ptolemy concerning the first motion of the fixed stars has since been changed. First reduced by Albategnius to 25,760 years, giving to each degree 66 years. Afterwards, Alphonse increased it to 49,000 years, which some have inappropriately called the great period, or the great Platonic year: But the last and most truthful period is that of Tycho Brahe who limited it to 1541 years and 7/8 of a year, having observed that every 72 years the fixed stars advance according to the order of the signs precisely by 31 seconds, which would be in 100 years 1 degree 15 minutes, and one degree in 70 years 7 months: The periods of the other inferior motions, except that of the Sun, are not considerable either in the composition or in the use of the Astrolabe, and deserve another place than this treatise, as well as the question, whether these two motions of which Jacquinot speaks, are acceptable and admissible in the celestial Spheres, against the reason of Philosophy, which does not admit at the same time two contrary motions in the same subject; therefore we will content ourselves with saying here only, that it being impossible to recognize in the same subject two appearances of contrary motions equal in periods, in time, and in place, it is absolutely necessary in case of inequality, to give to the faster the name and quality of motion; and to the slower the name and quality of simple resistance; if a full bowl of water agitated and turned quickly, is carried at the beginning of a revolution faster than the interior water, which although agitated and as if carried by the motion of the bowl, nevertheless by its resistance seems to take a slower contrary motion, and thus makes appear two contrary motions of different periods, which we note in passing without changing anything of Jacquinot's text, and of the common way of speaking concerning these two appearances of contrary motions. The other motion, which is first, simple, and regular, makes its revolution contrary to the aforesaid, namely from East through South to West: which, although it is proper to the primum mobile, is nevertheless common by accident to all the inferior orbs, notwithstanding their own motion: for they are conducted and revolved each natural day around the world, as is sensibly perceived by the courses of the Sun, and of the Material.

[Celestial Movement and Poles]
The Moon, and other stars, which by this movement rise and set, and make day and night. This movement is universal, and turns the entire celestial machine around two opposite points, which, for this reason, are called the poles of the world. One is named Arctic, or Northern, which is situated in the Septentrion, near the tail of the Little Bear, of which the star closest to us that we see at the end of the two last stars of the Great Bear is commonly called the Pole Star, and by some mariners the North Star, distant however from this Pole by 3 degrees and 14 minutes. The other Pole, opposite to the aforesaid, is named Antarctic or Southern, as much depressed below the Horizon towards the Antipodes as our own is elevated above. Near this, one sees a star of great light and clarity, named Canopus. Between these Poles, the line extended from one to the other, passing through the center of the earth, is called the Axis of the world.

[Treatise on the Sphere]
Treatise on the Sphere. Jacquinot wrote this treatise around 1615, at which time, according to the most accurate calculation of Tycho Brahe, the said Pole Star was at the 11th degree and 17 minutes of Gemini, and therefore the complement of its declination, that is to say, its distance from the Arctic Pole, was then 3 degrees and 43 minutes. And since then, we have observed this distance in the fourth and fifth minute of 1621 to be precisely 2 degrees and 45 minutes. Among other stars closer to the said Antarctic Pole, there is one of similar magnitude and clarity called Achernar at the end of the River Eridanus, which is closer to the said Pole than the said Canopus by about 7 degrees. Or rather, the axle, by analogy to the efficacy of a wheel, which turns around it. This word 'essieu' is French, and the word 'Axe' is Greek, nevertheless both signify the same thing. One must then conceive a circle equidistant from these two Poles, exactly in the middle of the Heavens, which will divide them precisely into two hemispheres. This is the circle described by the Sun when it enters the equinoxes, namely at the first point of Aries or Libra, of the Ecliptic of the first mobile, which will be called the equinoctial circle or equator.

[Equinoctial Circle and Day/Night Equality]
The equinoctial circle is well described by the circumduction of one or the other of the equinoctial points, namely the first points of Aries or Libra of the first mobile, around the aforesaid axis of the world; but not by the circumduction of the Sun, being in one or the other of the said points: inasmuch as, just as the said circle has no width, and is cut in two opposite points by the ecliptic or path of the Sun, of which the declination, near the said intersection, is greatly precipitous and rapidly changing, so also the Sun cannot remain there long enough to describe it visibly, but passes through it in an instant, describing in an entire day a portion of a spiral, and not a circle. Furthermore, the equinoctial is so called because, supposing the Sun makes some stay in it, the nights are made equal to the days, and this throughout the entire earth. The reason for this is that, in whatever situation of the Sphere, if the horizon cuts the said circle, the common section of the two is the diameter of both, passing through the center of the earth, and therefore dividing both into two equal parts, so that the measurement of hours being taken on the said equinoctial, of which half always appears above the horizon, and the other is below, it is necessary that the Sun, being in it, appears describing this upper half for the day in 12 hours, and the other lower half for the night in another 12 equal hours: and from this it follows that, except at the two Poles where their Horizon is parallel to the said equinoctial, this equality of day and night cannot be received, inasmuch as there is no common section that divides the said equinoctial into two equal parts, one for the upper Hemisphere, the other for the lower: But rather the Horizon dividing the ecliptic or circle of the Sun's path into two equal parts, so that for the space of six months they see the Sun above their Horizon in the upper part of the ecliptic, for another six months they lose its light while it is in the lower part of the said ecliptic, except always what the refractions determine otherwise. The equinoctial circle was called by the Greeks 'Isimerinos'.

[Horizon and Terrestrial Divisions]
Furthermore, it is to be considered that some people inhabit under the equinoctial, and others here and there, both towards the Northern and Southern parts. However, in whatever part they inhabit, they always see half of the sky, divided all around the earth by a circle called the horizon, or terminator of sight, of which the upper part that we see is called the superior hemisphere, and the other lower part towards the Antipodes is called the inferior. The said horizon is found in two different ways: namely, the right horizon for those who dwell under the equinoctial; and the oblique for those who inhabit the Septentrional or Austral parts, in which places one of the poles will always be elevated above our horizon, and the other in similar depression in the opposite. These elevations or depressions increase or decrease according to the diverse distance of the regions from the equinoctial, under which there is no latitude, nor elevation of poles, as you will be able to see and understand by the following figure.

[Types of Horizon]
Besides these two differences, a third can be added, and just as the two mentioned derive their denomination from the diverse situation of the Sphere, namely from the right Sphere the right horizon, and from the oblique Sphere the oblique horizon: so since among these two differences of Sphere there is still a third which is called parallel, we can with reason add a third difference of horizon not remarked by the ancients, which we will call the parallel horizon for those who have one or the other of the two poles for their Zenith, and it is called parallel for the same reason that the other two are called right or oblique, namely with regard to the equinoctial: For the right horizon is that whose plane cuts the plane of the equinoctial at right angles, for those who dwell under the said equinoctial, and for whom all the stars rise and set directly: The oblique, that whose plane cuts the plane of the equinoctial obliquely for those who inhabit between the said equinoctial and one or the other of the two poles, for whom the stars rise and set obliquely. Also, the parallel horizon is that whose plane is parallel to the plane of the equinoctial, for those, as has been said, who would be under one or the other of the poles, for with such a horizon the stars move and turn parallelly.

[Sensible and Rational Horizon]
There is another difference or division that the equinoctial and others have not touched upon, namely the sensible or apparent horizon, and the rational or natural horizon; the first is understood to be that space on land or sea that our sight can encompass, circumscribed around the point of our station, which space can be different according to the inequality of the terrestrial surface and according to the diverse height of the eye above it. The second, called the rational or natural horizon, is that which, although it passes through the surface of the earth, and therefore outside the said center, nevertheless, inasmuch as the earth, with respect to most celestial movements, is reputed as a point, it is also estimated to pass through this point as the center of the Sphere, and to cut it into two equal hemispheres.

[Meridian Circle and Zodiac]
Next, the circle passing through the poles of the world, and the zenith (which is the vertical point in the sky situated directly above our head) will be the meridian circle, which in two opposite places intersects the horizon exactly at right angles, and is called the meridian, because it divides each of the hemispheres into two parts, of which the upper part divides the day equally into two, just as the lower part divides the night. Then there is the Zodiac containing the twelve signs of the heavens, namely Aries, Taurus, Gemini, Cancer, Leo, Virgo, Libra, Scorpio, Sagittarius, Capricorn, Aquarius, Pisces. And each of these is divided into 30 degrees, supposing that a degree is nothing other than one part of the 360 into which the Ecliptic, like all other circles, is commonly divided. Each of these degrees can be subdivided into 60 minutes and other Astronomical fractions. Beneath this Zodiac, the Sun and the other planets continually move contrary to the first mobile, and this Zodiac is cut by the Equinoctial in two places: namely, at the beginning of Aries and Libra, where there is an equal duration of day and night throughout the universal world, of which the half declining from the said equinoctial towards the North, containing the first six signs, is called Northern: the other tending towards the South, Southern, which has a latitude of 23 and a half degrees, which is partly divided by a circle that the Sun describes in the middle, named the path of the Sun; otherwise the Ecliptic, beneath which the Eclipses of the Sun and Moon occur: and it touches the two tropics of Cancer and Capricorn at two opposite points. These tropics are circles described in the sky by the daily movement of the Sun, when it enters the first degree of Capricorn, which marks the beginning of our Winter, or the first degree of Cancer, which is the beginning of Summer, from which things the figure follows.

[Material: Treatise on the Sphere]
Material. ij We have here changed the text of the first impression, which seemed to us a little too obscure, as well as to the authors of the second and third impressions. Nevertheless, it is necessary to distinguish the divisions of the great circles from those of the lesser circles; all are indeed divided into 360 equal parts; but those of the lesser circles are not equal to those of the great circles, so that this equality should only be understood as equality of parts of the same circle. And it will be said below [Material] ij which circles in the Sphere are called great or small.

[Jacquinot's Latitude and Modern Observations]
Jacquinot recognized this latitude as only 23 and a half degrees, following the doctrine of ancient Astronomers: but moderns have assigned 16 degrees to this, convinced by the observations they have made of the movement of the two planets Mars and Venus.

[Solar Year and Calendar Reform]
Here David Robert, author of the third impression, presupposing that Jacquinot had determined the Solar year or the time the Sun takes to describe the said circle or ecliptic as 365 days and 6 hours, makes a long discourse on the subject of calendar reform, but this determination is not Jacquinot's (who contented himself with saying in general at the beginning of this Treatise that the period of the Sun's movement was one year); it was added by those good doctors who had it printed for the second time, who examined it only over a period of four years to find the reason for the intercalary day in each Bissextile year. And although this discussion brings nothing to the knowledge of the circles of the Sphere, which is the sole purpose of this treatise, we will nevertheless say more correctly than Robert that this quantity of 365 days, 6 hours, of which the Julian year is composed, exceeds the mean annual movement of the Sun, which is 365 days, five hours, 49 minutes, 16 seconds, by 10 minutes and 44 seconds, which in 154 years make up one day of difference. Neither the one nor the other is the space of time in which the Sun describes the said ecliptic; for the true space is the quantity of the true tropical or apparent year containing in these times 365 days, 5 hours, 48 minutes, 45 seconds, all according to the most accurate calculation of Tycho Brahe.

[Vernal Equinox and David Robert's Error]
We will also add this word, that David Robert is mistaken in saying here that the vernal equinox, at the time of the Council of Nicaea, was on March 20th, for it was on the 21st.

[Zodiac vs. Ecliptic and Tropics]
This must be understood not absolutely of the Zodiac, but only of the Ecliptic, for as regards the other planets, the Zodiac is said to have some width, so according to its said width it cuts the said two tropics each in two different points: But the Ecliptic only touches them each at a single point, one of which is opposite to the other, which the following figure represents better than Jacquinot's; by two hemispheres, in one of which the said Ecliptic touches the Tropic of Cancer, and in the other the Tropic of Capricorn by diametrically opposed points.

[Arctic, Antarctic Circles and Terrestrial Zones]
Material. 17 Consequently, there are two other circles, namely the Arctic, which is described from the pole of the Zodiac around and about the pole of the world, distant from it by 23 and a half degrees. And the Antarctic, which is figured entirely opposite at a similar distance from its pole. And it must be understood that Cosmographers call the four aforementioned circles, together with the equinoctial, parallels, which with the two poles of the world distinguish the entire surface of the earth into five regions called Zones, of which three are intemperate, namely the two extremes towards the poles, due to the great cold caused by the distant removal of the Sun: but the two others enclosed by the hot and the two cold are temperate, due to the participation of the one and the other contrary quality of these three Zones, of which two temperate ones we inhabit one, and the Antipodes the other.

[Zodiacal Poles and Polar Circles]
18 Treatise on the Sphere m The poles of the Zodiac are situated in the Colure of the Solstices, as far distant from the two poles of the world as the arc of the said Colure comprised between the equinoctial and the ecliptic is great, namely 23 degrees 31 minutes, according to the calculation of Tycho Brahe, to which our observations have often been found to conform, so that the said poles of the Zodiac, making a circumvolution around the poles of the world, describe the said two Arctic and Antarctic circles, otherwise called polar.

[Zones and Solar Influence on Temperature]
Material. 19 n The last two impressions have followed the deficiency of the first in the enumeration of the five Zones, for after the exposition of the two intemperate ones (situated between the Arctic and Antarctic polar circles towards one and the other of the poles, cold because of the distant removal of the Sun), it should truly follow, "And the third (that of the middle, near the equinoctial, comprised between the two Tropics, hot because of the proximity of the Sun): But the two others, etc." But it is to be noted that it is neither the absolute vicinity and proximity of the Sun that gives us heat, nor its removal that gives cold, but the directness or obliquity of its rays, so that where the Sun casts its rays more directly, there is the greatest heat, or more obliquely, there is the greatest cold, so that nevertheless time can increase or temper and slow down both. Hence it comes that under the equinoctial, where the Sun passes quickly, although perpendicularly, and stays only for the space of 12 hours above the horizon, the heat is much more temperate than under the Tropics where it stays for some days (hence they took their name from solstice), passing during the said days almost vertically under the meridian above their horizon, and moreover staying longer than the said 12 equinoctial hours by about 40 minutes or two-thirds of an hour. This truth, founded on the reason of Catoptrics, is further confirmed by the report of all those who have breathed both airs and experienced both rays; indeed, even in this our Northern temperate Zone, we experience in Summer 10 Treatise on the Sphere

[Sun's Apogee and Perigee]
When the Sun is at its apogee, it is further from the Earth and less oblique, and present for a longer time, it is much hotter and causes greater heat than in winter, when the Sun is at its perigee, and therefore closer to the Earth and to us, but its ray is more oblique and with less duration.

[Antipodes and Zones]
I will further explain this for the understanding of the word 'antipodes'. Those who inhabit the absolute torrid zone are called by cosmographers 'Asci', meaning 'shadowless', who have their shadows to the East, West, South, and North, and perpendicular, but in different seasons. Under the temperate zones, the inhabitants are those who are called 'Amphiscii', having two differences of shadows, East and West, or North for some, or South for others. Under the frigid zones, the inhabitants are called 'Periscii', who have four differences of shadows in the same day, because their shadow turns around them, from South and West to North, but in terms of habitation, the inhabitants under one zone and the same meridian, but in diametrically opposed points, are called 'Antoeci', who are under two points of the same meridian equidistant from the equinoctial, and therefore have the same length of day and night, but the seasons are contrary. Finally, those who are under the same meridian, but under different hemispheres and zones, and therefore under two diametrically opposed points, are called 'Antipodes' or 'Antichthones', as having the soles of their feet directly opposed, and walking on different parts of the Earth, also directly and diametrically opposed.

[Colures in the Sphere and Astrolabe]
Finally, in the Sphere, two circles named Colures are described, which are not placed on the Astrolabe, and serve in the Sphere only to solidify its parts; nevertheless, they distinguish the four Cardinal points in the Zodiac, namely the two equinoxes with both solstices. The two colures of the Sphere are represented in the Astrolabe by the two lines that intersect at its center, representing one of the poles of the Sphere, and one of which represents the horizon, and the other is perpendicular to it; for this one passes through the Solstices, and the other through the equinoxes. These two circles are called colures, from the Greek word 'kolouros', meaning 'mutilated', 'truncated', and called 'mutilated' because, with respect to any oblique horizon, these two circles are the only ones among all the other movable circles of the Sphere visible on some horizon, of which only the same part always appears above the horizon, and the other part is always hidden below, whereas all the others, though partly visible, by the circumvolution of the Sphere successively manifest all their parts, and finally make themselves known as whole, such are the parallels ascending on some horizon. But to tell the truth, this etymology is not among the most elevated, firstly because for the same reason all the hourly circles should be called colures, and thus there would be more than two colures, and even more so the meridian, of which, as immobile, only one half can appear, and the other is always hidden, and in the said two colures one can observe more than one half of their circle; moreover, under the equinoctial, or in the right Sphere, this reason ceases, and therefore there can be no colures, so that the said two circles passing through the points of the solstices and equinoxes, and intersecting at the poles of the world, should be provided with another name. I would therefore willingly call them 'Circulos Cardinales', as principal and cardinal circles which enclose, and at the same time divide and distinguish the entire Sphere into four principal and cardinal points.

[Eleven Circles of the Material Sphere]
In summary, in the entire Sphere, eleven circles are found, namely seven great circles which always divide the Sphere through the center into two equal parts, such as the equinoctial, the meridian circle, the right and oblique horizon, the two Colures, and the ecliptic. And four other small circles which divide the world into two unequal parts, such as the two Tropics, the Arctic, and the Antarctic circles mentioned above.

[Jacquinot's Eleven Circles Defense]
Here David Robert, where he makes a subtle point to criticize Jacquinot, shows a misunderstanding; but since in his preliminary epistle he himself presented his third edition of Jacquinot, annotated by him, as a piece of his labors judged excellent by many, and desired by a greater number, we believe that he employed his best mathematics and the best of his knowledge, in order to earn the reputation of having knowledge of mathematics, to be considered, as he says, among those pure, elevated, and subtle spirits; and we therefore believe that his criticism of Jacquinot is serious and sincere. It remains for us then to show what procedure he followed not to have well understood this passage, and to say that Jacquinot, with reason, mentioned only eleven circles in the material Sphere, and no more or less, including the right horizon and the oblique horizon as two, even if he had recognized the parallel horizon, inasmuch as making a distinction between the movable great circles of the said Material Sphere and the immovable ones, it is certain that the two colures, the equinoctial, and the Zodiac are movable, and the meridian immovable; but the horizon being able to be considered in two differences, and according to these alone distinctly situated and separated from the said other great circles, namely for the right Sphere, and for the oblique, and that it is not inconvenient, and can be done without confusion in the same Material Sphere, to apply the said two horizons, of which one being immovable, would always represent the right horizon, the two poles of the Sphere remaining fixed and arrested in it, the other being movable and able to incline, could represent the oblique horizon in any proposed Sphere situation, and thus the said Jacquinot could with reason note eleven distinct circles, separated from each other in the said Sphere, namely seven great and four small, and no more or less; for the parallel horizon can only be considered as the same circle and the same plane as the equinoctial, since both are great circles and will have the same Zenith for a pole, and therefore not considerable in the Material Sphere, to increase the number of its circles. As for the multiplicity of horizons and meridians alleged by the said Robert, it is a ridiculous objection, inasmuch as there is indeed a difference between the material sphere (the subject that Jacquinot treats, composed of certain circles, of which the said eleven represent the primum mobile) and the celestial sphere, for in this one, in truth, a great number of meridians can be imagined, with regard to a great number of different horizons, but with regard to a single horizon, all the said meridians would serve only as a single meridian considering the movable celestial Sphere, where they are like hourly circles or longitudes, considering the celestial Sphere fixed by the said horizon, and this is what the said material Sphere represents; but just as under the same meridian one can consider several different horizons and diversely situated with regard to the Poles, namely two right ones, and all the others oblique, so also in the material Sphere.

[Oblique and Right Spheres]
We can have a single mobile oblique horizon for every constitution of an oblique sphere, which can represent, according to its diverse situation, all sorts of different imaginable oblique horizons, not only under the said meridian circle, but generally under all other meridians at the location of the same or equal parallel: and this with regard to the circumvolution of the entire sphere over the said horizon. And for the right sphere, we have an immobile horizon, representing all the right horizons that can be imagined, with regard to the circumvolution of the entire sphere over the said horizon in the said material sphere. And thus for the entire said material sphere, inasmuch as it represents the prime mover to us, we only note the said two circles, namely 7 great and 4 small.

[Sphere Projection onto the Astrolabe]
Furthermore, what has been previously stated and well understood for the reduction of the flat sphere, we will suppose a sphere to be made of wax, or some other soft and flexible material. Then we will place and set the North Pole on some flat surface, compressing the other Pole; Then you will see how the said Poles will meet, and will be joined one with the other at the center of the instrument, so that the right horizon with the meridian circle will fall in straight lines and intersect at right angles at the said center, upon which the two tropics and the Equinoctial will be described in this manner: that is, to properly describe our Northern part of the Sky on the Astrolabe, one causes the Tropic of Capricorn (beyond which nothing is inscribed on the said Astrolabe) to fall next to the limb of the tables: and the Tropic of Cancer, towards the center, describing the equinoctial between them, according to the proportion of their distances of 23 degrees and about 30 minutes, which is the greatest declination of the Sun, both on the South and North side.

[Critique of Astrolabe Terminology]
This discourse on the relationship of the principal circles of the sphere on the Astrolabe, concerning the reasons for the projection of the solid sphere onto a plane, was inadvertently omitted, or perhaps deliberately removed by those who procured the second impression; and I would willingly believe it, since they not only testified in various places that the word planisphere did not please them, but also, in addition to what was said at the beginning of the following treatise, that it was abusively that the Astrolabe was so named. That for lack of being able to understand the reason for this projection, their ignorance led them to remove this passage which could have forced them to embrace and accept this name of planisphere as proper and suitable for the Astrolabe, as we will show hereafter in its place: But with reason we can here complain for Jacquinot of David Robert, who, having by his third impression followed the errors of the second, and consequently passed over this place without recognizing it, took occasion to criticize the said Jacquinot for having named the ecliptic line separately, and the Zodiac separately as being two different circles: which Jacquinot did not do, but in the enumeration he previously made of the great circles of the Sphere, he made mention of the Ecliptic and in the remark he afterwards made of the circles of the said projected Sphere on the Astrolabe, he named the Zodiac described with some fixed stars in a particular table: which words, not being able in any way to adapt to the material Sphere, should have made the said Robert suspect that there was an error in the said second impression and to research the truth rather than accuse and blame the Author without subject, whom we judge to have had better knowledge than him, of the Sphere and of the Astrolabe, both in their composition and in their use.

[The Zodiac and Sphere Reduction]
Finally, there remains the Zodiac which is described with the fixed stars in a particular table (of which we will have a figure in the following treatise) and is mobile to show the proportion of the movements of the Sky, which is applied to the tables of the Astrolabe, where the equinoctial, the meridian, and other circles are described as said above. And in this manner, the solid sphere is reduced to a flat form for the use and observation of Cosmography, in which there are many other descriptions of circles and lines, of which we will treat hereafter. This text has been improperly changed by the second and third impressions, which have castrated and removed what was best in it: namely, the conclusion of the entire preceding treatise on the Sphere, which was only introduced to give knowledge of the principal circles mentioned in the Astrolabe, which are projected from the solid sphere onto the plane of the Astrolabe. Furthermore, for this word Zodiac, we have the most truthful etymology in Julius Pollux, who says, 'Zodiac is a circle in the sky, which is divided into twelve parts, and in which the sun, moon, and planets move.'

[Second Treatise: Astrolabe Description]
SECOND TREATISE CONTAINING A brief declaration of the parts of the Astrolabe. After having succinctly declared the Material Sphere, it is necessary to explain the names and parts contained in the Astrolabe, to more easily understand its use. And following the doctrine of good Authors, we will begin with the definition.

[Definition and Etymology of Astrolabe]
The Astrolabe is a flat and round instrument, composed of several lines, both straight and circular, for knowing and examining the movements of the heavens, stars, and other things pertaining to the science of Astrology and Geometry, called by some a Planisphere, that is to say, the solid Sphere placed and extended in flat form, and is derived from this Greek name 'Astro', meaning in French 'Star' or 'Constellation', and 'Labio', which means 'handle' or 'grip', as if the handle of the Stars, for by holding this instrument by its handle, we observe the movements of the Stars, and dimensions of bodies; Or otherwise it is derived from the Greek verb 'Astrolabein', which means 'to comprehend', because by it we examine and know the movements of the heavens, and make other astronomical observations: Its invention some have attributed to Messahalach, others to Ptolemy, although long before it had been invented by Abraham, or by one named Lab, from whom some have wished to derive this name Astrolabe, as from the first Author.

[Jacquinot's Astrolabe Definition]
Jacquinot in this place followed the common usage both in the definition of the Astrolabe and in the derivation of its name. The Astrolabe is an instrument composed of a solid in the form of a disk, flat and round, having two parallel planes or faces on which, with the aid of the projection of the solid Sphere, it takes a flat figure. Such is the Astrolabe of Gemma Frisius, which he calls the Universal Astrolabe, inasmuch as on the back he has placed the universal planisphere, and on the face, within the mater, the particular planispheres found in common Astrolabes, which on this single side only, namely in the said mater, commonly contain the said projection of the Sphere in a plane, but on the back they are greatly diversified from one another.

[Astrolabe Name and Purpose]
In which it is to be noted that as this instrument was particularly destined for Astronomy, and is also for the most part composed of planispheric plates serving for the knowledge of the movement of the stars, so it has deservedly borne the name of Astrolabe: The Astrolabe is an instrument which contains and comprehends the knowledge of the Stars and their movements; thus its name carries its definition and its origin, and therefore ridiculous is that which this Author and others report from 'Anse' or 'Labio', which signifies handle or grip, and from the name of Lab, the supposed first inventor.

[Astrolabe as Planisphere]
There is therefore no abuse (as these good doctors say in their second and third impressions, contrary to Jacquinot's text) in calling the Astrolabe a planisphere, since it only comprehends the movement of the stars insofar as it contains within itself the Sphere reduced to a plane. Also, this name of planisphere was given to it by the Prince of Astronomers himself, Ptolemy, in his particular treatise that he composed on it, from which our common Astrolabes, without doubt, first drew their origin, just as later the universal astrolabe of Rojas came from another treatise on the Analemma by the same Ptolemy.

[Parts of the Astrolabe]
Therefore, in order to have a more ample and easy understanding of the said Astrolabe, we will now declare the names and parts contained therein. First, there is the Armilla or ring, or rather the handle, by which we hang the Astrolabe to take the heights of the Sun and stars, and to make other observations.

[The Astrolabe's Faces]
Afterwards is the Astrolabe itself, in a flat and round form (as we have said), which has two faces or surfaces, namely, the anterior, otherwise called the mother, because it can contain in its concavity several tables, serving for diverse elevations of the Pole above the horizon, of which we will speak hereafter. Here Jacquinot, by separately naming the Armilla with its handle and the Astrolabe, recognizes that the handle is not of the essence of the Astrolabe, and therefore there is no appearance in saying that the Astrolabe takes its name from its handle, which is only a part of the Armilla.

[The Dorsum (Back) of the Astrolabe]
And the posterior is called the dorsum, the declaration of which follows. On it are several lines and circles, the first of which are near the margin, containing the degrees of altitude, which have two functions: for by referring them to the numbers written near the extremity of the instrument, where the number does not exceed 90 degrees, they represent the degrees of heights, to know how much the Sun or the stars are elevated above our horizon, and other conveniences; but by addressing them to the numbers described below, which proceed from 30 to 30, they denote the degrees of the 12 signs of the Zodiac where they are described with their names and characters, to find the true place of the Sun each day.

[Months and Zodiacal Circles]
Afterwards come other circles where the twelve months of the year are described, corresponding to the twelve signs of the Zodiac, and their days, each divided separately, or by two and two with their numbers of 5 by 5, or 10 by 10, not exceeding nevertheless 31 days, which is the quantity of the largest month according to Roman computation, by which one knows each day in what degree and sign of the Zodiac the Sun is.

[Meridian Line and Horizon]
Consequently, there are two diametrical lines which intersect at the center of the Astrolabe at right angles. One is called the Meridian Line, which descends from the ring through the said center downwards, the other beginning in the East through the center tending to the West, which universally represents the horizon to us: at the extremities of which the degrees and numbers of the aforesaid heights begin indifferently.

[Unequal Hours and Altimeter Scale]
And near this line are, on some Astrolabes, six small lines in the manner of arcs which all join at the center with the said horizon, described on one or both sides to find the unequal hours: although they are not very necessary in this place, inasmuch as they can be practiced more justly by the description of these, which is done under the horizon in the tables of the Regions, unless they are general for all elevations of the pole to be used where some table would be needed. Moreover, these lines in the manner of arcs are truly arcs and portions of circles described from different centers and semi-diameters. Similarly, there is a Geometrical square, called the Altimeter scale, divided on two sides, each into 12 points or equal parts, of which we will speak amply in the last treatise.

[Winds and Alidade]
Furthermore, we have described on our Astrolabe above the degrees of heights on the edge of the instrument the 12 winds, in order to know from which part of the world each wind proceeds, with a banner, and a small quadrant and needle, as we will say within. We have added this description and figure of the 12 winds so as not to change anything from Jacquinot's text, but we have put nothing of it in our small Astrolabe, to avoid confusion, also because the practice of winds on the Astrolabe is of little fruit. And finally, there is the rule which turns on the dorsum of the Astrolabe, called Alidade in Arabic, or Dioptra in Greek, and Mediclinum or Radius in Latin: in which there are two tablets, otherwise called Pinnules, pierced with two small holes or slits to take the height of the Sun and stars and make other observations.

[The Limb (Mother) and Degrees]
The Names of the Parts of the other face called the Mother. First, on this face is the circuit, called the limb or margin, divided into 360 degrees, with numbers commonly increasing by five by five, and distinguished by lines longer than those of the degrees, which numbers we have continued up to 360, and not by 90, beginning at the direct horizon in the Eastern part, and this to find more easily the ascensions of the signs and stars (as you will see hereafter). These circles and degrees represent the Equinoctial where the 24 equal hours are measured and distributed, which we call clock hours, of which each contains 15 degrees, and each of the said degrees is worth 4 minutes, so that each hour is composed of 60 minutes.

[Critique of David Robert's Divisions]
Here David Robert takes issue with the fact that although the auditor says he has divided the limb into 360 parts of the same sequence and continuous division, nevertheless the figures show nothing of it and are divided by four times 90: but he has only seen figures supposed in the second Impression that his Authors borrowed from Stoeffler and not Jacquinot's original figures; and therefore one should not be surprised if in the said second Impression, as also in the third of the said Robert, there is something in the figures that does not correspond to the discourse and vice versa. This has led us to trace and engrave new figures both to represent Jacquinot's originals, where we have judged them good, and to correct them where we have found them faulty, as particularly in this place where we have seen the continuous division of 360 degrees begin in the East and pass through the South to the West, as Jacquinot's text states, which on the contrary should begin at the direct horizon on the West side, and pass through the South to the East, according to the natural sequence of the signs, to serve promptly for the knowledge of the right ascensions, in the absence of which, in the use and practice of the said right ascensions, it will always be necessary to count by continuous addition of degrees starting, as said, from the true point of the West and not of the East, as David Robert has noted, as little understood in this as in the division of the equinoctial circle, which he says here by a rich consequence, is divided into 360 degrees, inasmuch as there are 24 hours in the day.

[The Concave Interior and Tables]
Then follows the concave interior of the astrolabe, designed to hold several tables or tympans, serving diverse regions according to the variety of latitudes or elevations of the pole above the horizon. On the center of these tables are described three concentric circles, of which the smallest, towards the center, is the Tropic of Cancer, named in the Sphere the Summer Tropic; and the middle circle represents the equinoctial, which passes through the beginning of Aries and Libra, where the equation of day to night is made throughout the entire world when the Sun passes through it: namely, around March 10th and September 13th in our time. Consequently, the largest of the aforementioned circles, near the edge of the tables, is the Tropic of Capricorn (beyond which nothing is described on the tables), named in the Sphere the Winter Tropic, which causes us the shortest day of the year around December 12th.

[Sun's Entry into Tropics and Equinoxes]
The Sun, in these times since the reformation of the Calendar, enters these four principal points: the Tropic of Cancer on June 21st, the Tropic of Capricorn on December 21st, and the two equinoxes on March 20th and September 23rd.

[Equinoctial and Tropics on the Horizon]
It must be understood that the aforementioned equinoctial and Tropics on the oblique horizon, both towards the East and towards the West, distinguish three memorable points: namely, the equinoctial in the Eastern part, the true East; the Tropic of Cancer, the Summer East; the Tropic of Capricorn, the Winter East.

[Corresponding Western Points]
Similarly, they denote three similar Western points in the opposite part.

[Points of the Astrolabe]
A. represents the Winter East. B. its West. C. represents the true East or equinoctial. D. its West. E. the Summer East. F. its West.

[Almucantaraths and Hemispheres]
Also on these tables are the Almucantaraths, or circles of altitude, which are described above our hemisphere, some of which are complete and others imperfect. The first of these represents the oblique horizon, which divides the world into two hemispheres, one of which is manifest to us, and the other hidden beneath the earth towards the Antipodes.

[Zenith and Almucantarath Degrees]
It must be noted that the Zenith of a region is the pole of its horizon for which the table is drawn, and it is understood by the center of the smallest Almucantarath; for between them, from the horizon up to the said Zenith on all sides, are comprised 90 degrees, divided and traced by 2s, 3s, 5s, or 10s, according to the capacity of the instrument and the interval of these Almucantaraths, which are made to apply the Sun or the fixed stars at any hour one takes their altitudes above the horizon.

[David Robert's Useless Remarks]
Throughout this, David Robert preferred to say something useless rather than nothing at all, particularly on this true maxim, that every Zenith of a region is the Pole of its horizon. He gives us for conception a very lofty and elevated idea, that a beautiful consequence can be drawn from it, namely that there is an equal distance from each Zenith to the equinoctial as from the Pole of the world to the horizon: as if this truth were the only, or at least the most eminent, that could be drawn from the aforesaid maxim. But we could add others more important, such as to say that since the Zenith is the Pole of the horizon, it is at the point of the common intersection of all vertical circles; inasmuch as every great circle that cuts another at right angles has within itself the Pole of the one it cuts, and since every Pole is equidistant in all parts from its great circle by a quarter of it, the Zenith of each place divides into two the apparent halves above the horizon, both of the meridian circle and of all other vertical circles; and therefore, as our author says, it is distant on all sides from the horizon by 90 degrees, or by a quarter of a circle. But the Pole of the world also being distant from the equinoctial by 90 degrees, or a quarter of a circle, it follows that, as much as the said Pole of the world withdraws from the Zenith and inclines towards the horizon, so much the said equinoctial withdraws and rises above the same horizon; and as much as the said Pole inclines and approaches the horizon, so much the equinoctial approaches and inclines to the Zenith. Refer to the first figures on the preceding treatise of the Sphere.

[Azimuths and Vertical Circles]
Similarly, on these tables is another kind of imperfect circles called Azimuths by the Arabs, which pass through our Zenith, from which they can be named vertical circles, which intersect and divide each Almucantarath into 360 degrees, described by 5s, 10s, or 15s, according to the capacity and size of various instruments; and this by four quadrants, each of 90 degrees, which are distinguished from one another by two principal Azimuths, namely the meridian line and the equinoctial Azimuth which passes from the true East through our Zenith to the true West, where we commonly begin to count the degrees of the said quadrants, drawing towards South or North, which are made to know in what part of the world the Sun and stars are found, both rising and setting, and at other hours one desires.

[Equinoctial Azimuth and Prime Vertical]
This circle, which our Author calls the equinoctial Azimuth, is what is called the prime vertical, or first and principal vertical, passing through the equinoctial regions, or points of common section of the equinoctial with the horizon, and through the Zenith. In the authors of Gnomonics, it is called verticalis primarius, to which any plane perpendicular to the horizon and facing directly South or North is said to be analogous, and this circle is represented whole, but dotted, in our figure and serves with the dotted lines to show the method of construction, which we have also observed in the preceding figure.

[Unequal Hours and Planetary Hours]
Afterwards, below and outside the oblique horizon, are the unequal hours, otherwise called planetary hours, comprised by 12 small arcs, which, with the midnight line and the said horizon, both in the Eastern and Western parts, distinguish the 12 unequal hours, and have their beginning in the Western part, tending to the midnight line, ending in the East: as you can see by their numbers described below the said horizon in the following figure.

[Names and Characters of Planets]
The names and characters of the seven Planets, according to their order, are figured in this manner: 1. Saturn. 2. Jupiter. 3. Mars. 4. The Sun. 5. Venus. 6. Mercury. 7. The Moon.

[Unequal Hours and Their Description]
The commencement of the numbering of these unequal hours is in the western part, because the said hours are described in the lower hemisphere, below the horizon. Nevertheless, they serve as much as if they were inscribed in the upper hemisphere, in which case their numbering would begin in the East, to then come to an end and terminate in the western part by the middle. But inasmuch as in this case, two of the said unequal hours, equal among themselves, would always be found diametrically opposed, and by knowing one, the other could not be ignored, to avoid the confusion that would arise in the description of the said hours in the upper hemisphere due to several other circles or portions described therein, it has been found more appropriate to describe them only in the lower hemisphere. And it should always be noted that the aforementioned arcs of unequal hours, commonly described by the division points into 12 equal parts of the equinoctial circle and of the two tropics, cannot serve precisely for the intermediate parallels, as for the said equinoctial and Tropics, but only approximately and with an almost imperceptible difference. And to have the said unequal hours precise for the entire length of the year, that is to say for all the parallels of the Sun, it would be necessary to trace the said parallels, and divide each into equal parts, or at least half below the oblique horizon into 6 parts for each division point, and trace a curved line, in the manner that lines are traced for hours on the Cylinder.

[Twilight Line and Stöffler's Error]
You will also find described below the said oblique horizon, a line named crepuscular (twilight) between the unequal hours, denoted by these two letters A and B, to find the point of day and the beginning of night, as we will say hereafter in our Canons. This crepuscular line, marked by Jacquinot in the preceding figure, has been marked and described by us in the following figure below the oblique horizon between the arcs of the 12 houses. And we will note here in passing that in most Astrolabes, this line is poorly described. Stöffler having made the first mistake, has been followed by all the others who imitated him (as most of those who have written on this subject have only imitated Stöffler and not reasoned, invented, constructed, and described by themselves). Those who understand well the projection of the Sphere will easily recognize Stöffler's error in the description of the arc that represents this line. In a word, its description is such that of the Almucantarats, it is nothing other than the 18th Almucantarat or circle of 18 degrees of depression below the horizon.

[Meridian and Horizon Lines]
Similarly, there are two diametrical lines that intersect at the center of the said tables. One is called the meridian line, descending from the ring through the center downwards, of which the part comprised above the oblique horizon represents the meridian line, just as the other part below represents the nadir line. And the other line, which cuts the meridian perpendicularly through the center at right angles, denotes the right horizon, that is, for those who live under the equinoctial, of which the part from the center towards the left hand of the observer of the Astrolabe is the Eastern line, the other part is that of the West. And these lines divide the tropics with the equinoctial into four equal quarters.

[The Twelve Houses of the Heavens]
Furthermore, four great arcs are described, touching the circle of Capricorn at their extremities, which pass through the point where the meridian and the oblique horizon intersect. And with these, they divide the equator into 12 equal parts, in the same way that both the upper and lower parts of the tables are distinguished and divided by them, each into 6 parts or intervals, which represent to us the 12 houses of the Heavens, added for more easily casting and erecting Astronomical figures at all hours according to Urbach and Regiomontanus, of which the first house begins in the Eastern part of the oblique horizon, up to an interval of 50 degrees in the equinoctial, or where the second begins, and so on for the other houses according to the order of the signs. And the first six of these are below the horizon, and the other six above in our hemisphere.

[House Division Methods]
This division of the 12 houses, which our author attributes to Regiomontanus, which he followed and called rational, dividing both the upper and lower part of the equinoctial (extending above and below each proposed horizon) each into six equal parts. There are five methods noted, of which there is only one, besides the one above, which could conveniently be marked on the said horizontal or particular plates of the Astrolabes, namely that which, in my opinion, was most appropriately invented and followed by Campanus and Gazulus, and which divides the first vertical circle (or that which passes from one point of an equinox through the Zenith to the other equinox) into 12 equal parts by circles, intersecting at the points of common section of the meridian circle with the horizon. The three other methods are more bizarre, inasmuch as just as the constitution of the Sky varies from day to day and at every hour of the day, so it would be difficult to make a description on the Astrolabe that could be general, that is to say, serve every day and at all hours of the day like the two above. Of these three, nevertheless, there is a first and older one, which although it does not have its own proper and particular circles on the Astrolabe, can nevertheless be easily practiced on it, as the author will show hereafter in proportion 41.

[The Rete and Fixed Stars]
After having declared the parts of the tables, follows the Rete of the Zodiac, which is a table described separately, and universal for each of the said tables, containing first a circle, where are the names of the 12 celestial signs, divided into 360 degrees by their numbers proceeding by 5s or by 10s up to 30, which is the quantity of one of the 12 signs, and the circumference of this circle represents to us the ecliptic, or path of the Sun. The said Rete also contains a certain number of the brightest and most shining stars in the Sky, situated and calculated according to their true places, on small points, with their nature and magnitude, denoted by certain numbers and characters, namely 1, 2, 3, etc., which signify these stars to be of the first, second, or third magnitude. And by these characters of the planets, Jupiter, Venus, Mars, Mercury, and Saturn, and so on for the others.

[The Index or Ostensor]
Consequently, there is the small ruler named Index or Ostensor, which turns on the center of the instrument around the limb, to show the rising and setting of the Sun and the Stars, and to serve for other conveniences.

[The Central Pin and Poles]
Finally, there is the central hole of the Rete, just as there is in each of the said tables, which represents to us the poles of the world, by which hole and the central pin the said tables are joined together with the whole instrument. This will be the end of the names of the parts contained in this present instrument, which being well understood, it will be easy to comprehend what will be said hereafter in our Canons.

[Brief Description of Astrolabe Parts]
HERE FOLLOWS A BRIEF DESCRIPTION of the parts of our small Astrolabes, the knowledge of which will greatly facilitate their use.

[Astrolabe Back Divisions]
On the back of our Astrolabes, towards the extremities, we have had three types of divisions engraved. The first, closest to the edge, is divided into four times 90, making a total of 360 parts or degrees. It is arranged so that it can serve both for the ordinary division of the circle (horizontally, to obtain azimuthal differences when the Astrolabe is placed flat, parallel to the horizon; and vertically, to take heights when the Astrolabe is fixed or hanging freely perpendicular to the horizon), and for the division of the Zodiac into its twelve signs and their 30 degrees, even half-degrees, as in the largest, where each of the said 360 degrees is further subdivided into two.

[Months and Days Division]
The second closest division is that of the months of the year and their days, arranged in such a way that it maintains a similar correspondence with the degrees of the Zodiac, as the months and days will with the movement of the Sun precisely in the coming year 1625, but a few years before or after, without any sensible difference.

[Sun's Declination Division]
The third and inner division is that of the Sun's declination, distributed to each degree of the Zodiac or day of the year, starting from either equinox and ending at the solstices, according to how far the Sun is found from the equinoctial at that degree or day. And although this division is only placed in the upper part of our Astrolabes, opposite the middle of the Zodiac, it can nevertheless serve as much as if it were continued along the entire Zodiac, as will be shown hereafter in its use on proposition 29.

[Geometric Quadrant]
Besides the aforesaid divisions, and in the middle space of the back of our Astrolabes, we have had the Geometric Quadrant engraved, which we have had divided into 100 equal parts, this division being more suitable for the promptness of operation than any other, as will also be shown hereafter in its place.

[Geometric Trellis]
Below this Geometric Quadrant, on one side, we have had a kind of division made in the form of a trellis by small equal squares, distinguished by tens and hundreds, and with small dots or points, the use of which, as pleasant as it is prompt and easy in the rule of three, even for those who are entirely ignorant of Arithmetic, will be placed hereafter in its place under the name of the Geometric or Proportional Trellis.

[Bimback Quadrant]
On the other side is figured a small rectilinear clock, commonly called the bimback quadrant, which we have added to facilitate the knowledge of equal hours of the day by means of the Sun, for those who find the ordinary inquiry too long and tedious. We will show this in its place, where, inasmuch as such clocks are easy to describe and portable, we have wished to include in passing something of their use.

[Mother Plate Divisions]
On the side of the mother of our Astrolabes, we have had engraved on the edge or limb the ordinary division of the circle, namely, in the larger one, four times 90 degrees, starting from either side of the right horizon, and in the smaller one, 360 degrees, starting and ending in the Western part of the said right horizon, to which, distinguished by a space of 15 by 15 degrees, are added the characters of the equal hours of the day, numbering two times 12, to serve for the knowledge of the said hours.

[Multiple Plates and Universal Planisphere]
Inside the said mother, we have had several differently engraved plates placed, to serve for various uses and in various and different places, such as for some principal cities: Paris, Lyon, Rome, Antwerp, and others, as indicated on each of the said plates. Among these, we have added a universal planispheric plate, figured hereafter, for the curiosity of those who desire to have a general knowledge of what they can learn separately from the particular plates.

[Rete and Fixed Stars]
Upon all these immobile plates is added the mobile Rete, with its Zodiac, to which we have had several of the most apparent Fixed Stars attached, with the mark of their magnitude and the character of the Planets whose nature they partake, situated according to their true and proper place computed for the said coming year 1625, from which they can now differ only insensibly. The rest of the composition of our Astrolabes differs in nothing from what is contained in Jacquinot's treatise on the declaration of its parts; so that, being each accompanied by its armilla and its two ordinary alidades, namely the dioptric on the back and the index (otherwise simply called a rule) on the mother (which armillae and alidades we will endeavor to make just and suitable), we promise ourselves that their use, as will be deduced hereafter, will give satisfaction to everyone, and this all the more because the operation will be found much more accurate than with previous printed ones, however larger they may be.

[Universal Planispheric Plate Explanation]
Explanation of the Universal Planispheric Plate. Inasmuch as the greater part of the ordinary operations in the practice and use of common Astrolabes can be easily resolved in practice and use of this universal planispheric plate that we have inserted with the particular plates in the mother of our Astrolabes, we have deemed it appropriate to add something further to serve as an explanation of what is engraved on the said plate, in order to facilitate the knowledge of its use.

[Planispheric Plate Composition]
This universal planispheric plate is composed of a single circle filled with various portions of other circles described from various centers, all of which portions nevertheless represent semicircles, but differently. For those that concur and intersect all at two opposite points represent halves of great circles, and the others that do not intersect each other, but cut the preceding ones, and terminate on both sides at the said great circle that encloses them all, indeed represent halves of circles, but of smaller circles and of different diameters, some larger and others smaller.

[Meridian, Poles, and Equinoctial]
The great circle that encloses the others, which you see divided into four times 90, represents principally the meridian. The two opposite points of the said great circle, where all the said portions of great circles concur and intersect, represent the two poles, one Arctic, the other Antarctic. The line that divides the said great outer circle (namely the said Meridian) into two equal portions, passing through the center and terminating at two points equally distant from the said two poles, represents the equinoctial, or its common section with the said Meridian. And that which, passing through the center of the said Meridian, terminates at the said poles, represents the circle of 6 hours, where the cover of the equinoxes being the axis of the world or the common section of the said Meridian, like the cover of the solstices, with the said circle of six hours, like the cover of the equinoxes; so that in practice and use, the said two straight lines will serve as two great circles in the Sphere.

[Meridians and Hour Circles]
The portions of great circles which concur at two poles represent the Meridians in the convertible Sphere, passing through the said poles, and through all the divisions of the equinoctial, inasmuch as each of the said circles, by the circumvolution of the Sphere, can be placed under the meridian, and thus all successively can represent it. We shall call them hereafter, sometimes hour circles, sometimes vertical circles or Azimuths, sometimes meridians, sometimes circles of longitudes; and the one in the middle, represented by a straight line, shall be called by us the straight meridian, or circle of six hours, or straight vertical, first and principal, as their use shall require. And they are distant from each other in our Astrolabes to avoid confusion, from 10 to 10 degrees at most, and from 5 to 5 at least. Moreover, we have had them distinguished by 15-degree marks to distinguish the principal hour circles from the intermediate hour circles, namely the whole and complete hours from their parts, which will be known by the characters of the 24 hours that we have had affixed to them.

[Circles of Latitudes and Altitudes]
The portions of circles terminating at the said meridian in two equidistant points from one and the other pole, represent the circles of latitudes, or rather the parallels describable in the Sphere through all the divisions of the said meridian as degrees of altitudes, and the said circles are also distant from each other by two degrees in one, and by five in five in the other. They could be called Almucantarats or Circles of Altitudes, principally in the parallel Sphere. We shall call them sometimes simply parallels, sometimes circles of altitudes or latitudes, or of declinations; and the one in the middle, larger than the others, and represented by a straight line, we shall always call the equinoctial or straight horizon, or straight parallel. And the two, among the aforesaid parallels, which terminate at two points equidistant from the said equinoctial or straight horizon towards one and the other pole by a space of about 15 degrees, we shall call the two solstices or Tropics.

[The Ecliptic and Zodiac]
The straight line which, passing through the center of the said Meridian, and obliquely cutting all the aforesaid circles, joins the said two parallels (namely one and the other Tropic, in two opposite points of the said meridian), represents the circle of the ecliptic, and shall be called by us the Zodiac or Ecliptic, of which the central point represents the point of the equinoxes, or the beginning of Aries and Libra: and of the two other opposite points, one represents the beginning of Cancer or the Summer solstice, and the other the beginning of Capricorn or the Winter solstice. And between these are marked in their respective places the beginnings of the other signs and their intermediate degrees.

[The Movable Horizon (Ruler/Alhidade)]
And as the said planispheric plate is universal, so no particular horizon is described on it, but the ruler or common Alhidade of the Astrolabe, turning above, can represent an infinity of different horizons, so that according to a certain proposed place whose latitude is known, it is easy to fix the said ruler in such a situation that it represents the horizon of the said place, and the planispheric plate the constitution of the entire Sphere upon it. For this reason, in the use of the said plate, the said ruler will sometimes be called by us the movable horizon.

[Assembly of the Astrolabe]
All these parts of our Astrolabes, premised and explained, must finally, after due and proper arrangement, be joined, tightened, and secured into a single body of instrument by means of a screw-pin which traverses it through the middle with its small nut. Of which screw-pin and nut follows the figure.

[Preparations for Using the Astrolabe]
PREPARATIONS FOR THE USE of the Astrolabe.

[Knowing Table Elevation for Latitude]
PROPOSITION I. To know for what elevation of the pole each table of the Astrolabe is described. If you wish to know for what latitude or elevation of the pole each table of the Astrolabe is described, look in the Meridian line, how many Almucantarats or degrees are comprised from the equinoctial circle to the Zenith, or from the Pole (which is the center of the Astrolabe) to the horizon towards the North. And for this polar elevation and latitude, the table is composed, which can serve you for a place of similar latitude, or within one or two degrees: for example, 48 degrees of elevation will serve without sensible error for 47 and 49, and in a greater difference in case of need. This example is not difficult, and one only needs to pay attention to how many degrees the Almucantarats are drawn and divided, namely by two and two as in large instruments, or by three and three, or by five and five, as in small ones. This proposition is the 17th of Jacquinot's book, which seemed to us to precede the treatise on use, and is a preliminary step to knowing and choosing from an Astrolabe the plates of tables suitable and convenient for the place where they are to be practiced, before coming to their use and practice, even though the understanding of this proposition does not depend on any of the 26 preceding propositions concerning the use of the Astrolabe, but purely and simply on what has been said above in the treatise on the composition and explanation of its parts. This choice and preliminary preparation will give you this advantage, that the content of the said 16 propositions can be usefully practiced and put into use, or on the contrary, for lack of having made this choice and prepared the suitable and necessary plate for the place of your observation, or of the operation you will perform, in putting the said 16 propositions into practice, you will not be able to profit anything else, except to learn the simple method of practicing it, but nothing true will remain after your operation for the place of your dwelling. In a word, you will learn to handle and practice the Astrolabe, and you will learn nothing from the Astrolabe.

[Unknown Latitude and City Names]
And it should be noted, if you find yourself in some place whose latitude you do not know, that you can ascertain it by the following propositions. We have in some measure remedied this inconvenience, for in our small Astrolabes we have had the names of some principal cities inscribed, for which, and for nearby places, each table has been drawn.

[Choosing the Right Plate for a Location]
PROPOSITION II. To choose in the Astrolabe a suitable plate for each place. We add this second proposition as being the practice of what the preceding one has taught us, and as being its converse, and therefore easy to practice. For if in any proposed plate the number of Almucantarats or degrees comprised between the center of the Astrolabe and the horizon makes us know the latitude or elevation for which the said plate has been drawn: so also, if any latitude or polar elevation is proposed to us, we choose among the plates the one which will contain an equal or closer quantity of degrees, or Almucantarats, between the said center and horizon, and thus we can usefully enter into the practice and use of the Astrolabe, following the propositions of the treatise which follows.

[Examining and Rectifying Astrolabe Balance]
PROPOSITION III. To examine and rectify the balance of one's Astrolabe, and prepare it for observation.

[Astrolabe Leveling and Balance]
Let us add this third proportion, in the form of a very necessary precaution, to be able to use the Astrolabe both astronomically and geometrically in a very useful way.
