AFTERWARDS follows another method which properly belongs to the Astrolabe, which can be practiced by the Azimuths, which divide (as has been said) our hemisphere into four quadrants, each of 90 degrees, which are similarly understood on the back of the Astrolabe, namely the quadrant which is from the left extremity of the transversal line called horizon, up to the midday line next to the Armilla, represents to us the Eastern Southern quadrant: and from this Armilla up to the right extremity of the transversal line, demonstrates the Western Southern: The subsequent up to the midnight line, is called the Western Northern quadrant: and the last from this midnight line up to the left part of this transversal, represents to us the Eastern Northern quadrant: provided that you have the face and the Armilla turned towards the South. To observe the said parts, take the height of the sun at whatever hour you wish, then place the degree of the sun at a similar height among the Almicantaraths, considering in which quadrant it will fall among the Azimuths, and at what distance from the beginning of the quadrants, namely on which of the said Azimuths: then lay the Astrolabe on its face, and place the rule in a similar quadrant and height as the sun was found among the Almicantaraths, making the shadow of the pinnules equidistant and straight according to the rule: then you will have the four parts of the world by the four extremities of the two diametral lines of the back, namely South by the line extending from the center towards the ring, and North opposite: East and West, by the transversal line.

[Example: Finding Directions with Astrolabe]
EXAMPLE. On the fifteenth of April, the sun being in the 5th degree of Taurus, I take its height above the horizon, which I find to be 40 degrees before noon. This being placed among the Almicantaraths, I find this degree to fall on the 30th Azimuth in the eastern northern quadrant. This done, I lay the Astrolabe with its back up, equidistant from the horizon, so that the line drawn from the center to the armilla is towards the southern part: then I place the rule in a similar quadrant and degrees as the sun was among the Azimuths, namely on the 30th of the transversal line in the East, extending towards the North, and I turn the Astrolabe (the rule remaining fixed) until the shadow of the pinnules responds and is equidistant to the lines of the rule. Then the line from the center extending to the armilla shows me the southern part, and its opposite the northern, and the extremity of the transversal line, which is on the left side, the true East, as the other part, the West: provided that the face of the man and the armilla of the Astrolabe are turned towards the South.

[Critique of Meridian Line Methods]
This first method of finding the meridian line by the magnetic needle presupposes the practice of the second method, and is subordinate to it as a direct dependence thereof; and although the said second method can be practiced differently, nevertheless what Jacquinot says may suffice. But these are different things: the meridian line of a magnetic needle and the true meridian line. This latter, once exactly found and marked, it is easy to examine the other, and having recognized the difference, one must mark it in the compass box, so that whenever one needs to quickly trace a meridian line, and do what Jacquinot wants to teach with the Astrolabe, one only has to apply the said box, quadrant or compass, whose side will represent the said meridian line when the needle is arrested on this marked line of difference. Here one can recognize the impertinence of those good Doctors who procured the second impression of Jacquinot, for instead of a rod smaller than the semi-diameter of the circle, they made it equal, which David Robert having followed, he took occasion to criticize Jacquinot, and to say that this rod equal to the radius of the circle would be useless for a good part of the year, until the meridian height of the Sun was greater than 45 degrees, and by a sensible excess, so that at two different hours, equidistant one before and the other after midday, the shadow of the said rod could terminate at the circle described.

[Improved Gnomon Method]
We say then that for the promptness of the operation it would be appropriate to describe several circles from the same center at some distance from each other, and on the said center to erect perpendicularly a rod of average size, between the semi-diameter of the said circles, so that the extremity of the shadow can be promptly recognized to terminate at one of them, which will serve for the second observation after midday. But it is first necessary to take this precaution, which neither Jacquinot nor Robert have remarked. That is, the surface on which this observation is to be made must be level and parallel to the horizon, otherwise it would be useless if the rod were perfectly perpendicular to it, which you will judge to be so when its extremity is found to be equally distant from the circles described from the same center on which it is erected. And inasmuch as this observation is one of the first, principal, and fundamental of all astronomical observations, and it is very important that it be made exactly, we will say further, to remark the difficulty encountered in drawing the meridian line through the center, when there is an attached rod, that it is

[Meridian Line Accuracy]
Sometimes it is more appropriate, after having traced the plane of the dial and drawn several circles from the same center, to attach to the said center a piece of iron wire or brass sharpened to a point, and to solder it in such a way that the extremity of its point precisely corresponds to the said center. This will happen when, with a compass, you see that it is equally distant from all parts of one of the described circles. For the extremity of its shadow being marked on the same circle before and after midday, it will be easy to cut the arc comprised between the two marks in two, equally, by a line that will pass exactly through the center: and such a line will be the meridian line. But above all, it will be noted that such a practice and observation of the meridian line cannot be practiced precisely and without some error, however imperceptible, every day of the year, because the Sun's declination varies more or less from one hour to another, more noticeably near the equinoxes and less near the solstices. And inasmuch as at the winter solstice the Sun's altitudes are doubtful and less exact, due to the inequality of the air, sometimes thicker, sometimes thinner, which also causes sometimes more, sometimes less refraction; the safest will be to make such observations at the summer solstice to establish it as a certain foundation for all other observations; even for the declination of the magnetic needle, because then, for the space of 7 or 8 days, the Sun's declination being almost invariable, it cannot cause any difference, and the Sun is not subject to refraction for about two-thirds of the day. This third method is more fallacious than the preceding ones, and can only be approximate, not precise, inasmuch as between the observation and the direction of the Astrolabe, as Jacquinot teaches, there is time, during which the Sun can have changed its Azimuth, so that this practice can roughly give the four cardinal directions, but not a meridian line, nor the true points of East and West.

[Universal Planisphere Method]
This third method of practice is nothing else on our universal Planisphere than what we have said in the last preceding proposition, since it is only a question of finding the Azimuth, and it can also be practiced with the stars.

[Recognizing Stars at Night]
You must, by the 10th, know their rising, and their eastern Zenith by the 22nd. Then, at a certain hour examined by a well-regulated clock, you will arrange the Astrolabe (as we have shown for the four cardinal directions). Afterwards, you will direct the rule of the back towards the point of the horizon where one of the stars marked on your Rete should rise, and that star which you will then see elevated through the pinholes of the sights above your horizon will be the one you are looking for, which you must observe by the figure of the stars near it, or another sign, so that by it you can know the hour of the night, and the other unknown stars. Likewise, you can do this when the star sets, though not as conveniently, because it separates from our sight. Wanting to know the star Spica Virginis, I look through the Astrolabe for the hour it should rise, and in which quadrant or part of the world. Then I go to an open place, arranging my Astrolabe according to the four cardinal directions, as per the preceding proposition, and directing my back rule to the corresponding degree of the quadrant where the star should rise: then looking through the pinholes of the sights, that bright and sparkling star that I then perceive rising above our horizon, I am certain that it is the one whose knowledge I seek.

[Alternative Star Identification Method]
Alternatively, in the evening when the sun has set, set the rule at some certain hour, observed by a clock, as before: and with the small rule placed at the hour, turn your zodiac until the degree of the Sun falls at the place of the fixed rule. Then look in the Astrolabe for the star you wish to know in the sky, at how many degrees of the Almicantaraths it has in height, and in which part, Oriental or Occidental. Afterwards, take the rule of the back, and place it at as many degrees, in the circles of height on the back, as the star had in altitude in the Almicantaraths; then hang your Astrolabe by its handle, and look at the sky in that part where the star was found, towards East or West; and the brightest and most apparent star that you will see exactly at the place of the two sights will be the one you are looking for. EXAMPLE: On June 4th, wanting to know the star Spica Virginis, at ten o'clock in the evening, taken by a clock, I direct the rule and the degree of the Sun (which is the 22nd of Gemini) to that hour: and the Rete thus arranged, I find among the Almicantaraths, the star elevated by 25 degrees in the Western part: therefore I arrange the rule of the back at such a height; and I turn towards the Western part, until by this arrangement I can perceive a bright star which I say is Spica Virginis. Furthermore, you will be able to easily recognize the stars that are in the Zodiac, or those that do not have great latitude, by knowing which night, and at what hour the moon should come near them, which is known by the lines and degrees of both, by the ephemerides, or other tables. Wanting to know the star Cor Leonis, I observe by the ephemerides, or almanacs, when the Moon will be in the sign of Leo, around the 22nd degree: and the star that I see that night very bright near it, I infallibly judge to be Cor Leonis. And by this means you will also come to the knowledge of the planets, which, well observed, you can also recognize several unknown stars. Of these two methods, this second one seems much more appropriate than the first, indeed, the first cannot even be practiced except by this one: inasmuch as it is almost impossible to recognize, or even perceive stars at their rising or setting, as much because of the inequality of the earth and obstructions of some mountains, trees, or buildings, as principally because of the ordinary vapors and clouds that almost always appear above our horizon, so that this horizon point of their rising having passed, it is necessary to seek them at their height above the horizon by this second method: and if it happens that two stars in the sky are of almost similar height and magnitude of light near each other, the larger in appearance, and less brilliant, is one of the seven planets, and the brighter is the star you are looking for. This method of knowing fixed stars or planets is very uncertain, inasmuch as even if you have knowledge of the Moon's place in the Zodiac, or of some other planet by the Ephemerides; if you do not also have knowledge of their latitude, together with the longitude and latitude of the stars, you will labor in vain. Therefore, this method is not appropriate here.

[Universal Planisphere Star Recognition]
This proposition can also be practiced on our universal Planisphere, if you propose to know in the Sky some star marked on it. The rule placed at the correct height of the pole for your horizon, mark the parallel of declination of the said star, and forming a triangle with your rule, make its apex fall on the said parallel exactly at the place of the hour circle that is known to you, and the said rule being transferred to the equinoctial or straight horizon, note on which parallel of height the apex of the same triangle will fall, as well as on which vertical circle, or in which part of the world; thus the height

[Identifying a Star by its Altitude]
of the said star being known to you, and applying the rule over the same degree of altitude, if you present the Astrolabe towards the same part of the world, the star that you will perceive through the sights of the pinnules will be the one you have noted on the said planisphere, and of which you desire to have knowledge.

[Finding Other Stars from a Known Star]
After having identified one star by the preceding methods, you will easily recognize all the other stars described on the Astrolabe, by taking the altitude of the known star and placing it among the Almicantaraths. This done, observe the position of the star you wish to identify, that is, on which Almicantarath it lies, and in which part of the sky it is found. Then, from that part, look through the sights of the pinnules, and the brightest star among the others will be the one you are seeking. Thus, one known star will show you the hour at which the others rise, and by this means, you will see them rise, to know them more surely than by a clock. EXAMPLE: Wishing to know the star Cor Leonis, I take the altitude of Spica Virginis, which is known to me, and find it to be 30 degrees towards the East. Then, having placed it at its altitude among the Almicantaraths, I see the point of Cor Leonis fall on the 15th Almicantarath, after the meridian line. Therefore, I set the rule on the back to a similar altitude, and turn towards that part. The star I see at the end of the pinnules I judge to be Cor Leonis. You can do the same for others.

[On the Universal Planisphere]
This knowledge of the stars could be derived from the universal planisphere; but because its practice is too tedious on our instruments with only the rule and compass, we will pass over it here. For even with a universal planisphere equipped with its horizon, its quadrant of altitude, and its brachiolum with its movable pointer, this proposition would still be tedious to practice, unless there were as many movable pointers as there are marked stars, or at least two. But to tell the truth, I would not at all advise this research by the Astrolabe, except to gain knowledge of one or two. The easiest way is by some globe, on which, having marked those stars that will be known to you by the Astrolabe, or otherwise, having recognized in the Sky the common chariot, frequently seen, which are the seven stars of the tail of the Great Bear, and having marked them on the globe, you will compare them with the nearest stars in the Sky and on the said globe, and by the difference of their magnitude and situation, you will distinguish them, and by their names, such as they are marked and named on the said globe, and so forth, by these, you will recognize others.

[Identifying Stars Not on the Astrolabe]
This present canon, and several others (such as knowing the Sun's position without knowing the day, the latitude of fixed and erratic stars, or whether erratic stars are retrograde or direct, and in which mansion of the sky the Moon is) are of little utility and no certainty by the Astrolabe. Rather, they require proper and particular tables, calculated for this purpose, such as ephemerides and others, which is why we will abandon them and refrain from the present use, as Stophler has done, who among others has treated this matter amply and very learnedly. The knowledge of the planets depends entirely on the tables of their movements, such as ephemerides. If you wish to know them in order to distinguish them from the fixed stars, take their position in the Zodiac from the said ephemerides for the day and hour of your observation, and mark it either on the rete of your Astrolabe in the manner in which fixed stars are marked, or on a celestial globe. For the Astrolabe, you will practice the two preceding propositions described above; and for the globe, the comparison you will make of the nearest stars, both in the Sky and on the said globe, will give you knowledge of the planet you have set out to find.

[Critique of Bassantin's Astrolabe Amplifications]
It is with reason that Jacquinot and Stophler have rejected from the use of the Astrolabe certain propositions, such as those of Jacques Bassantin in his treatise on the amplification of the use of his Astrolabe. For besides the fact that the practice and operation of such propositions require some change and addition to the construction of common Astrolabes, the procedure is so tedious that the result can only be very doubtful and uncertain, which is confirmed by Bassantin himself, in that he requires a pole of the Zodiac marked on the rete, and an extension of the particular plates to include the complement of 8 Almicantaraths below, or, as he says, beyond the Tropic of Capricorn. For the first, we say that although the Pole of the Zodiac can be easily marked during the construction of the Astrolabe, it would nevertheless be almost useless for the operation, inasmuch as in small Astrolabes the width of the rule, called the alidade, would infallibly cover it, being too close to the center. And in larger ones, where this same thing would happen, or the said rule, lacking sufficient width, would be less suitable for a just and precise operation. For the second, we say that it is pure impertinence to think of amplifying the use of our common Astrolabes by an amplification or extension of the plates enclosed therein, which the limb cannot tolerate; or else such amplified plates will always be distinct and separate from the body of our said common Astrolabes. But in a word, Bassantin's propositions are such that he who could not have knowledge of them and could only practice them by the Astrolabe will never know anything, and will derive no fruit from them; and he who has knowledge of them from elsewhere will consider their practice on the Astrolabe not only useless and unfruitful, but purely ridiculous. Not that we consider what Bassantin says impossible; for just as the Astrolabe by its projection represents the sphere to us, so it is reasonably susceptible to all the encounters and questions that can be made on the sphere. We say further, envying Bassantin's amplification, that the solution of no spherical triangle will be impossible by the Astrolabe; but each mathematical instrument has its particular operations as its principal and proper functions to give satisfaction to the curious mind, otherwise there would be confusion.

[Finding the Sun's Position Without the Day]
It is indeed true, nevertheless, that the knowledge of the Sun's position is not impossible by the Astrolabe without knowing the day, as Jacquinot thought. For, firstly, by two meridian observations, one can know by the difference in altitudes in which half of the Zodiac the Sun is: namely, the ascending half from the Tropic of Capricorn through Aries to the Tropic of Cancer, if the last altitude was found to be greater than the first; or the descending half from the said Tropic of Cancer through Libra to Capricorn, if the contrary. So that at the time of the last observation, the rete being so turned that the said known half of the Zodiac passes over the meridian line between the Almicantaraths, the degree of the said half that is found to pass over the said line at an equal altitude to the last one found, will be the degree of the Sun on the day of the last observation. This known degree will give you in the calendar placed on the back of the Astrolabe, the corresponding day of the year; except for the necessary practice, explained above, for the leap year.

[Example: Unknown Day]
EXAMPLE: On some unknown day of the year, you take

[Determining Sun's Position from Meridian Altitude]
the said meridian altitude of the Sun, and you find it to be 37 degrees 51 minutes, and the following day you find it to be 37 degrees 17 minutes, you will first know from these two observations that the Sun is in the descending half of the Zodiac, since its meridian altitude has decreased since your first observation. Therefore, if you cause the alidade to pass over the meridian line between the almucantarats, the said descending half of the Zodiac from the Tropic of Cancer through Libra up to Capricorn, and you mark the degree of the said half passing over the said line with a similar almucantarat as that of the Sun's last altitude, namely 37 degrees 17 minutes, you will recognize it to be about the 9th degree 30 minutes of Libra. On this degree, on the back of the Astrolabe, placing the alidade, you will see it pass over the third day of October; for which, if it is a leap year, as in 1610, you will take the fourth of October; if in 1611, the third, plus a quarter; in 1612, the third plus half a day; and so on until the full day of the fourth leap year.

[Another Method: Sun's Position Without Knowing Day]
OTHERWISE. We will also perform this proposition on the small rectilinear dial. To know the Sun's place without knowing the day. This proposition will be practiced quite readily on the Astrolabe, 159, in this manner: take the Sun's meridian altitude on two different days to know if the Sun is in ascending or descending signs, as has been said, taking care in the second observation to turn the part of the Astrolabe where the said dial is drawn towards the Sun, for the alidade, showing the meridian altitude on the limb, will also show on one or the other meridian line of the said dial the Sun's parallel, by the point where it intersects them. EXAMPLE. In the preceding example, having found the Sun at noon at an altitude of 37 degrees 51 minutes in the first observation, and 37 degrees 17 minutes in the second, the rule remaining in the same state, you will see that it will cut the lower meridian line at a point, and you will say that it is a descending sign, and therefore about the 9th degree and 30 minutes of the said sign of Libra.

[XXVIII. PROPOSITION: Sun's Distance from Zenith]
XXVIII. PROPOSITION. To observe daily how far the Sun is from our Zenith. To do this, there are two ways, one of which is particular and refers only to places for which tables are made on the Astrolabe, and the other is universal. So, if you wish to know the distance of the Sun to the zenith by the first method, place its degree on the meridian line, at the table made for your elevation, and count between the almucantarats from your zenith to the said degree, thus you will have the difference. By the second, which is general, it can be done at noon or any other hour of the day; for by subtracting the Sun's altitude at noon from 90 degrees, the distance between the zenith and the Sun will remain. Similarly, at all other hours, by subtracting the Sun's altitude from 90 degrees, you will find the said distance of the Sun to our zenith. EXAMPLE. I find the Sun's altitude at noon to be 42 degrees, which I have subtracted from 90, and 48 degrees remain, which is the distance of the Sun to our zenith that day. Thus you can do the same for fixed stars, by taking their meridian altitudes and subtracting them from 90 degrees. There is indeed a great difference, as we have previously remarked on the 5th proposition of this present treatise, between knowing on the Astrolabe and observing by the Astrolabe the vertical distance of the Sun. The first is free to us at all hours, day and night, provided we have a proper and particular plate for each proposed place. The second requires the presence of the Sun and its ray with the observation of its altitude on the horizon, for which an infinity of mathematical instruments can serve, and in any place whatsoever. For this, I find that Jacquinot has forgotten to give his method of knowing the said vertical or zenith distance at any hour other than noon, which is nothing other than knowing the Sun's altitude on the horizon at any hour of the day, for one is the complement of the other, which is greatly useful for the construction of various scioteric instruments, such as rings, cylinders, quadrants, and others which are constructed by the reason of shadows according to the various altitudes of the Sun at each proposed hour, which we will supply here as follows: Let the rule or alidade be placed on the proposed hour, and under its sidereal line let the proposed degree for the Sun's place in the Zodiac be moved and stopped, and mark on which almucantarat it will be found, for the number of these almucantarats comprised between the said degree of the Sun and the zenith will be the distance of the Sun from the said zenith; therefore, the complement (namely, the number of the said almucantarats comprised between the said place of the Sun and the oblique horizon of the proposed place) will be the Sun's altitude on the horizon at the said proposed hour; which having observed for all the degrees of the Zodiac, or at least for the principal divisions in each sign, as for every five, ten, or fifteen degrees, one will have tables for the construction of the said scioteric instruments. But before giving an example of this operation, it will be remarked here that just as this proposition can serve for several instruments by means of the Sun's altitudes known for all hours of each proposed day, so also the preceding proposition can serve for the construction of quadrants or dials in which either the extremity of the gnomon or its common intersection with the Sun's parallels shows the hours, the construction of which depends on the knowledge of the Sun's azimuth at each proposed hour, that is to say, by how many horizontal degrees each vertical circle passing through our zenith and through the center of the Sun, being in each hourly circle, is distant from the meridian, which having observed for each degree of the Zodiac and for each hour of the day, one will have tables for the construction of the said quadrants. EXAMPLE. For example, then, of both, if you wish to know what the Sun's altitude on the horizon of Paris should be, and what its azimuth or horizontal distance from the meridian at 10 o'clock in the morning, or 2 hours after noon, on the 8th day of April, the Sun being in the 19th degree of Aries. Having placed the rule on the said 10 o'clock in the morning, and having moved and stopped the said 19th degree of Aries there, you see that it falls between the 41st and 42nd almucantarats, namely on 41 degrees and 36 minutes, and such should be the Sun's altitude at the said hour, as you can also know that the said degree also falls between the 41st and 42nd azimuths, namely on 41 degrees and about 50 minutes: and this is the horizontal distance from noon that the Sun should have at the same proposed hour. Which will also be found to agree for 2 hours after noon. The same by the Universal Planisphere.

[Constructing Altitude and Azimuth Tables]
This instrument is singular for the construction of the aforesaid tables of altitudes and azimuthal distances of the Sun at each proposed hour, and for any given place. For the horizontal rule, being fixed on the degree corresponding to the polar elevation of the proposed place, such as for Paris on the 48th degree 45 minutes, counting from each pole to the equinoctial; and having marked the degree of the Sun, and its parallel of declination, if one forms a triangle whose apex falls precisely on the intersection of the said parallel with the circle of the proposed hour, the said rule being transferred to the equinoctial or right horizon, the apex of the said triangle will fall precisely, between the parallels or circles of altitudes, on the Sun's altitude at the proposed hour, counting from the equinoctial between the meridians or vertical circles, counting from the largest and outermost, is the required Azimuth. Thus, the Sun being at the 15th degree of Leo, around the 7th day of July, it will be found to have an altitude above the horizon of Paris, at 8 o'clock in the morning, of about 43 degrees 20 minutes, and its vertical circle will be distant from the meridian by about 67 horizontal degrees.

[Finding Declination of Sun and Stars]
XXIX. PROPOSITION. To know each day by how many degrees the Sun, or other stars, declines from the equinoctial. Declination is taken from the equinoctial towards one or the other pole of the world, for which reason one is northern, and the other southern: and the greatest does not exceed 90 degrees. Therefore, to discern it, one must place the degree of the Sun on the Meridian line in one of the tables of the Astrolabe, and the degrees of the Almicantaraths which are from the equinoctial up to the said degree of the Sun will show its northern declination, if it falls within the equinoctial towards the arctic pole: or southern, if the degree falls outside the equinoctial, moving towards the Tropic of Capricorn. EXAMPLE, I wish to know the declination of the Sun, being at the 5th degree of Taurus, I place that degree on the Meridian line, and find its declination to be 15 degrees, counting from the equinoctial up to the said degree. Then I say that this declination is northern, because it falls within the equinoctial towards the center of the Astrolabe.

[Using Declination Tables for Stars]
Similarly, you can judge the stars, by directing their point on the Meridian line, and counting from the equinoctial, as we have done for the Sun's degree. And because this present proposition is very useful for helping to know the latitudes of the fixed stars, we have added a small table of the Sun's declination, by which one will know this declination more accurately than by the Astrolabe. The Use of the said Table: After having found the degree of the Sun, to know its declination, one must find the sign of the Sun, at the top or bottom of the table, according to how its name or character is written, and the number of its degrees, either on the side of the table (if the said sign is at the front of it) or if it is found at the foot of the table, one must take the number on the right side. This done, by proceeding to the common angle, corresponding to the said sign and degree, the degrees and minutes of the Sun's declination will be found, which will be Northern if the Sun is in one of the first six signs, namely, Aries, Taurus, Gemini, Cancer, Leo, and Virgo: Or Southern if it is in one of the last six, such as Libra, Scorpius, Sagittarius, Capricorn, Aquarius, Pisces.

[Astrolabe Declination Practice]
Moreover, the practice of this proposition regarding the Sun is much quicker and easier with our small Astrolabes, for the rule being placed and fixed on the proposed day found in the Calendar on the back, it will likewise be fixed on the Sun's place in the Zodiac, and on the degree of its declination: so that at the same time it will show you both, notwithstanding that the said declination of the Sun is only placed in the upper part of our Astrolabe, opposite the Northern Signs, starting from each of the equinoctial points of Aries and Libra, ending on both sides at the solstitial point of Cancer: For just as in the entire Zodiac two points or degrees equally distant from one or the other equinox, or diametrically opposed, have equal declination, so also the rule being placed on any degree of the Zodiac, will always show its declination in the circle of declinations, which will be called Northern for the degrees of the Northern Signs and Southern for the Southern ones. Thus on the 15th day of May the Sun will be found at the 24th degree and about 40 minutes of Taurus, and to have 15 degrees of Northern declination: and on February 11th the Sun being at the 14th degree and about 10 minutes of Aquarius or Water-bearer, will be found to have about 15 degrees 30 minutes of Southern declination, which is equal to the Northern declination of the opposite degree, 24 degrees 10 minutes of Leo. We have added the table of declinations of Jacquinot following the ancient calculation, that of Tycho Brahe is more accurate and precise, although its practice does not differ in any way from what Jacquinot says of his own.

[Declination on the Universal Planisphere]
OTHERWISE On the General Planisphere. This 29th proposition is also very quick, simple, and easy to practice on the universal planisphere, having knowledge of the Sun's place on the proposed day: For counting the parallels comprised between the equinoctial and the said place of the Sun, this same number is the number of degrees of its declination. Similarly, the number of the said parallels comprised between the said equinoctial and any proposed star, marked however on the said planisphere, gives the number of degrees of its declination. XXX. PROPOSITION.

[Finding Latitude or Pole Elevation]
To know in all places where one may be, how much latitude or elevation of the pole there is. Latitude of a place (in this regard) is the distance from the equinoctial to the zenith of the meridian, which is measured in degrees of the meridian circle. This can be found in various ways, the easiest of which is by the meridian altitude of the sun when it is at the beginning of Aries or Libra. For by subtracting this altitude from 90 degrees, the distance from our zenith to the equinoctial remains, which is the latitude of the place where we are, always equal to the elevation of the pole above the horizon. EXAMPLE: In the year 1540, on March 10th, the sun entered the first degree of Aries at noon. We observed its meridian altitude within the city of Paris, which we found to be 48 degrees and about 20 minutes. Subtracting this from 90 degrees, there remain 41 degrees and 40 minutes, which is the true latitude of the said place. But if on the day one wishes to observe this latitude, the Sun has some declination, as happens in other signs and degrees: After having observed its meridian altitude, one must know the declination of its degree, by the preceding proposition: and if it is northern, it must be subtracted from the altitude of the Sun taken at noon: if southern, it must be added. This will give the altitude of the equinoctial, which, subtracted from 90 degrees, will leave the latitude of that place where we are making such observations. EXAMPLE: On April 14th, we found in Paris that the 4th degree of Taurus had 11 degrees and 55 minutes of declination towards the northern part, and that the altitude of the sun at noon was 54 degrees and 15 minutes. From this, we subtract the declination, which is 11 degrees and 55 minutes, so there remains 42 degrees and 20 minutes, which is the altitude of the equinoctial. I subtract this from 90 degrees, and there remains 48 degrees and 40 minutes for the latitude of this city of Paris, where we made this observation. This proposition is fundamental to all others, both preceding and following, and depends absolutely on observation. The more exact and precise it is, the more just and precise will be what we seek; but if it is crude and imprecise, however easy, it will render what we seek crude, imprecise, and less approaching the truth. This is why we will examine the two methods of observation that this author provides. For the meridian altitude of the Sun at the beginning of Aries and Libra, it is a rare occurrence that the Sun, being at these beginnings, meets the meridian in some place; that is to say, that it enters these equinoctial points precisely at noon. So if it is outside these points, it will infallibly have more or less declination, the more or less it is distant from them. And as its declination changes greatly and precipitously near these equinoctial points, it is also very rare to be able to make and practice this first method justly and exactly. The second method, which is by the meridian altitude of the Sun having some declination, to come to the knowledge of the elevation of the pole, depends on the knowledge of the true place of the Sun and its true declination at the time of observation; which is almost impossible to know justly without the aid of proper Tables or Ephemerides precisely calculated, or at least reduced to the meridian of the place of observation. Therefore, for surer and more exact methods, we will present three principal ones here. The first, by means of the Sun, being at the tropics, principally at that of Cancer, and therefore at its greatest declination, which in these places remains invariable for a few days at least in degrees and minutes, and therefore insensibly different. Taking then the meridian altitude of the Sun, being at the said tropic of Cancer, and from it subtracting the greatest declination, the remainder will be the altitude of the equinoctial, which subtracted from 90 degrees will give the latitude or polar elevation of the place of observation. In Paris, if you take the meridian altitude of the Sun as 64 degrees 46 minutes, subtracting 23 degrees 31 minutes for the greatest declination according to the most accurate calculation of Tycho Brahe, there remains 41 degrees 15 minutes of equinoctial elevation, which subtracted from 90 leaves 48 degrees 45 minutes of pole elevation. A second method is by means of some star whose declination is known; for taking its altitude when it passes the meridian, and from this altitude subtracting the declination of the star if it is Northern (which is the surest case) or adding it if the star is Southern: in the one case the sum, in the other the remainder, will be the equinoctial altitude, which subtracted, as said, from 90 degrees, gives the altitude or polar elevation of the place of observation. The third and most exact method of all is by means of circumpolar stars, of which we see the entire revolution around the pole, and which never set below our horizon, and principally of that which is closest to the pole, as in these Northern countries, the polar star called Cynosure. Choosing a proper night, that is, during which it passes twice below the meridian (which will occur every day from December 15th to January 15th in these times). For taking the altitude above the horizon at both passages below the meridian, and adding these two altitudes together, taking half of the total, this will be the just altitude of the pole above the horizon. On January 15th, in the year 1611, in the evening around 7:30, we found in Paris the said polar star to be 51 degrees 18 minutes high; and the next morning at the same hour we found it to be 46 degrees 1 minute high. These two altitudes joined together are 97 degrees 19 minutes and 18 seconds, of which half, 48 degrees 45 minutes and about 9 seconds, is the most accurate altitude of the pole above the horizon of Paris. We have deemed it appropriate for the less advanced to add here the two following propositions, inasmuch as their knowledge and practice can be very useful to them. To know in any place the altitude or elevation of the equinoctial. This proposition consists only of one word, which is to subtract the altitude of the pole, found by the last preceding proposition, from the number of 90 degrees, that is to say, from the entire quadrant of a circle, and the remainder will be the altitude of the equinoctial, inasmuch as the two altitudes assembled compose the entire quadrant of a circle, and one is the complement of the other. For example, the 48 degrees 45 minutes found by the preceding proposition for the altitude of the Pole in Paris, being subtracted from 90 degrees, the remainder amounting to 41 degrees 15 minutes, gives the altitude of the equinoctial for Paris; and this equinoctial altitude thus found and known by observation serves greatly for the true knowledge of many other propositions, but particularly for the following proposition: The equinoctial altitude of a place being known, to verify and certainly know the declinations of the Sun and the stars. Let the meridian altitude of the Sun or of some star be taken by the 5th proposition, and let the number of degrees and minutes of this altitude be compared with the number of degrees and minutes of equinoctial elevation. That is to say, let the smaller number be subtracted from the larger, and the remainder or the difference of the one from the other will be the required declination. EXAMPLE.

[Solar Declination in Paris]
In Paris, the equinoctial elevation being known as 41 degrees 25 minutes, on the 15th day of May, I take the Sun's meridian altitude, which I find to be 60 degrees 15 minutes. From this, subtracting the 41 degrees 25 minutes of equinoctial elevation, the remainder, 19 degrees, will be the Sun's declination, which will be called Northern, the meridian altitude having exceeded the equinoctial altitude. But it would be Southern when it is found to be less.

[Corollary: Finding Sun's Position from Declination]
By this known declination, you can know the Sun's place in the table of declinations, or on your Astrolabe, even, if necessary, come to know the day of observation that you will find to be the 15th of May, the Sun being at the 24th degree 40 minutes of Taurus. The same applies to the stars; for the one you will have found to have 50 degrees of meridian altitude will infallibly have 8 degrees 45 minutes of declination.

[Finding Longitude by Lunar Eclipse]
By the longitude of a place, we presently understand the distance from the meridian of the Fortunate Isles, extending towards the East, up to the Meridian of that place, which must be counted within the degrees of the equinoctial, or other parallels: but the longitude of one city to another is the difference of their two meridians, comprised in the degrees of the equinoctial. Therefore, it must be understood that Ptolemy, among other Cosmographers, observed a large part of the longitudes of several cities and regions, which are easy to find in his Geography: but when in some places the longitude is unknown, it is appropriate to know at what time a future eclipse of the Moon should begin in one of the places of known longitude. Then, in the place of unknown longitude, on the day that the said eclipse is to occur, you observe with the Astrolabe at what hour it will begin: for if it begins at the same hour that one finds by calculation it should begin in the place of known longitude, it would be manifest that these places would be of the same longitude: but if it begins earlier or later, there will be a difference according to the variety of time that will be found, as if it began one hour earlier in the place of known longitude than in that of unknown longitude, one can easily judge that the longitude of the unknown place is greater by one hour, which is worth 15 degrees, than that of the place known to us. And similarly, one must understand the other differences in time, according to the value of the degrees, always taking 15 degrees for one hour, and four minutes for each degree.

[Example: Tubingen Longitude from Lunar Eclipse]
FOR EXAMPLE, I find in Ptolemy that the longitude of the city of Paris is 25 degrees 30 minutes, and that a lunar eclipse should begin in the said place at three hours after midnight: at this point, I want to know how much longitude Tubingen, a renowned city, has. To do this, I observe in the said place the time that the said eclipse occurs, and I find its beginning, according to my knowledge, 24 minutes after midnight, which is 14 minutes, or 6 degrees, later than in Paris, which I add to the longitude of Paris, because the beginning of the eclipse occurred earlier there: and by this means, I know that Tubingen has twenty-nine degrees 30 minutes of longitude.

[Multiple Observations for Longitude]
Similarly, if one did not know the longitude of any places and wished to observe it in two, three, or as many places as seemed good: it is required for each of the said places to have astrologers, who in those places will truly observe the time when these eclipses begin, then these assembled will come to confer the said time and according to the difference they will have found, one will know the longitude of the places where such observations are made, always taking (as has been said) for each hour fifteen degrees. And for four minutes one degree, if one wishes to reduce the longitude into degrees.

[True Geographic Description]
Upon this observation depends all true geographic description: for to describe a place truly, it is only required to know its latitude and longitude, which longitude is more difficult to observe, and is not easily found every day like latitude.

[Finding Longitude Difference with a Watch]
OTHERWISE, To find the difference in longitude between two cities. We can know this with a small, well-regulated half-watch, by setting the hand to a certain hour, rectified with the Astrolabe when you leave one place to go to another, always paying attention to your watch to keep it accurately: and when you arrive at the intended place, then observe the hour with the Astrolabe, and see if it agrees with that of your watch or not, diligently noting the difference: for if there was none, you would still be under the same meridian; but if it is greater or lesser, you will divide the difference of hours by degrees and minutes, as we have said above; and thus you will know the difference of longitudes between two cities.

[Example: Paris to Lyon Longitude]
EXAMPLE, Departing from Paris to go to Lyon, I rectify my watch with the Astrolabe: and when I arrive at the said city of Lyon, I see that the hand of this watch precisely touches eight o'clock in the morning: and immediately with the Astrolabe I observe the hour, and find eight hours minus three degrees, which are worth 12 minutes: then I judge there to be a difference between the two cities of three degrees, such that if Paris has 25 degrees of longitude, the city of Lyon will have 16. And thus you can do for all other places.

[Note on Proposition 31]
This proposition would merit another place than this treatise, inasmuch as it cannot be put into practice on the Astrolabe, except insofar as it speaks of finding the hour when some eclipse begins, and besides, the subject is a little too long a discourse to speak of it here very pertinently.

[Definition of Longitude]
The longitude of a place is the quantity of meridians comprised between the first meridian and that which passes through the Zenith of the said proposed place, and therefore the difference in longitude of two different places is the difference of their meridians to be counted from the first. Or else we say that the longitude of some place is the arc of the equator comprised between the first meridian and that of the said place; and therefore the difference in longitude of two different places (which Jacquinot simply calls the longitude of one place to another) is the difference of their meridians, or the arc of the said equator comprised between their meridians, or the difference of the arcs of the said equator that each of their meridians encloses with the first. But it must be noted here what David Robert showed not to have understood, that both the longitude of some place and the difference in longitude of two different places can and should be taken as well on the parallels of the said proposed places, or others, as on the equinoctial: inasmuch as two meridians cutting and comprising an arc of the equator, cut and comprise similar ones on all the parallels which are also the measure of the said longitudes or differences as the said arc of the equator, even though their degrees are not each worth 60 miles, like those of the equator (according to Ptolemy). What David Robert believed to be necessary in the measurement of longitudes as well as latitudes: it is indeed true that for convenience in the practice of instruments, in which of all the parallels only the equinoctial is found divided into the ordinary degrees, one uses its division to estimate those of the other parallels.

[Scope of Jacquinot's Observation]
What Jacquinot says here should not extend further than to know only the difference in longitude of two or more places.

[Determining Longitude Difference by Eclipse]
If not simply their longitudes. Now, for the difference in longitudes, it is not necessary to consult Astrologers, it being a sufficiently easy and feasible thing, even for those least advanced in these sciences, by means of an automatic clock. Having recognized in each of the said places, by the movement of the Sun for some preceding days, and observed by sight alone the beginning of a Lunar eclipse in each place, to determine the hour: this being observed in several places, the difference in time and hours that the same eclipse began in each of them, being reduced to degrees of longitude (taking 15 degrees for each hour, and for 4 minutes of an hour, 1 degree) will give the true difference in longitude of the meridians of each of the said places; but to know the longitude of each place, as this author says, and to know by how many meridians or degrees of the equator it is distant from the prime meridian, the observation of the Astrologer will not advance it at all, unless it is co-existent with the said prime meridian, and of the hour that the said eclipse must begin under it: but Jacquinot! Hoc opus, hic labor.

[Finding Time in Another Place]
It will perhaps not be found out of place and useless to add here this proposition, as a converse of that part of the preceding, namely: Being the known hour in some place, to know what hour it is elsewhere. As in the preceding proposition, by means of the difference in hours, one can come to the knowledge of the differences in longitude; so also by means of known longitude, one can judge the difference in hours. If then one proposes some place whose longitude is known, comparing it to that of the place of observation, their difference will be taken, which, reduced to hours and parts or minutes of an hour, and added to the hour of the place of observation (if the proposed place is more oriental and at a greater longitude), or subtracted if it is more occidental and with less longitude, in one or the other way, the hour of the said proposed place will be given. EXAMPLE. Let it be proposed to know what time it is in Rome when it is noon in Paris: inasmuch as Rome has 36 degrees 10 minutes of longitude; and Paris 15 degrees 0 minutes. Their difference is 21 degrees 10 minutes, reduced to parts of hours, is 1 hour 24 minutes, or 40 minutes of hours, which added to the hour of Paris, inasmuch as Rome is more oriental, makes more than 1 hour or nearly 1 hour after noon for Rome, when it is noon in Paris.

[Distance Between Two Cities]
XXXII. PROPOSITION. To know the distance on earth between two cities, or regions. After having known the longitude and latitude of two cities, if you wish to know how many miles or leagues are between them, you must understand that there are three ways to find the distances: Because some places have only distance in latitude, some only in longitude, others in longitude and latitude together. The distance then between two places that have the same longitude, and differ only in latitude, is easier to find than the others: For (according to Ptolemy and others) since to each degree of latitude always correspond 60 Italian miles, after having the difference in latitude of two places, it is only necessary for each degree to take 60 miles, or 30 French leagues, and for each minute one mile, thus you will have the distance of the said places. As for places having the same latitude, differing only in longitude, it is necessary to reduce the degrees of difference of their longitude into degrees of the equinoctial by the following table. And then after this reduction, to do as we have said for degrees of latitude. Canon for understanding the table that follows. The degrees of longitude in this table are reduced to degrees of latitude, supposing that the said latitude does not exceed 58 degrees, and is not less than 35, which include the latitude of the principal places of all Europe. Therefore, to convert the difference of longitude into degrees of latitude, one must enter at the right of the number of latitude of these places, which will be found written below the word (degrees) of the table; and opposite this latitude, take the minutes that correspond to each degree, and by the number of these, multiply the difference of the longitude, thus they will be converted into degrees of the equinoctial, or other great circles, which are similar to degrees of latitude. EXAMPLE. Paris and Tübingen having the same latitude, differ in longitude by six degrees. Now, wishing to reduce this difference into degrees of latitude, or great circles, because the latitude of the said cities is 48 degrees, I enter the table with 48 degrees in number; to the right of which I find 40 minutes, which is the value of one of the degrees of the said longitude: Therefore, by multiplying the said 6 degrees by 40 minutes, it results in 240, which is worth 4 degrees, into which the 6 degrees of longitude are reduced, for each of which I take 60 Italian miles, as we have said for degrees of latitude, and it results in 240 Italian miles, which are worth about six score French leagues. Degrees. Minutes. Degrees. Minutes. 35 59 36 58 37 57 38 56 39 55 40 54 41 53 42 52 43 51 44 50 45 49 46 48 47 47 48 46 49 45 50 44 51 43 52 42 53 41 54 40 55 39 56 38 57 37 58 36

[Distance with Both Longitude and Latitude Differences]
The third way to find the distance between two places, when they have diversity in both longitude and latitude, is that one must take the difference of one and the other, and reduce that of longitude into degrees of latitude by the preceding table. After having multiplied the said difference of longitude in square: similarly that of latitude: and put the said numbers together, then from these find the square root according to the rules of Arithmetic. Which root will show us the degrees of distance of the said places. For each of which it is appropriate to take 60 Italian miles, or 30 French leagues, as has been said. This proposition is still of a very high style for a treatise on the use of the common Astrolabe. Also, Jacquinot has passed over it very modestly, and has only said three words about it as in passing, commendable nonetheless in that he did not wish to engage further in a subject that he testified, by the little he said, that he did not understand. Or on the contrary, the authors of the second impression, as much and more ignorant than Jacquinot in this matter, have filled this proposition with long and tedious discourses with a multitude of examples which are proof of nothing other than their ignorance, whatever excuse they might have taken to conform to the doctrine of Apian, Stöffler, de Mesmes, and others, from whom they took all that they amplified their discourse with, for either they should have been silent altogether, or said little like Jacquinot, or else they should have spoken with more edification, and shown in what the aforementioned Apian, Stöffler, de Mesmes, Jacquinot and others had erred. And it seems to us that this was something one should have expected from Robert's third impression; but far from it, for it still included all the impertinences of the second impression, on which, examining several examples where he found something to criticize, he took occasion to often reprimand Jacquinot for what he had never thought. And we do not doubt, after having noticed this lack in others, that one should, it seems, expect something better from us on this subject. However, it was not our intention to choose this place to speak of it at length as the matter deserves; it is a little too elevated to fall into ordinary and easy practice on common Astrolabes. But inasmuch as the practice is very proper and easy on universal planispheres, we will give the method for it by examining hereafter Jacquinot's text, and David Robert's examples.

[Terrestrial Distances and French Leagues]
It is indeed true that Ptolemy estimated one degree on Earth to be 60 Italian miles, but the experience of many has since testified that the number of 50 agrees better. For our French leagues, we have three kinds: large, medium, and small. Of the large leagues, 20 correspond to one degree on Earth; of the medium, 25; and of the small, 50. Each of the said large leagues is equal to 5 Italian miles; each of the medium, 2 ½; and each small, 2. Just as each German mile equals 4 Italian miles, so that to one degree on Earth correspond quite precisely 60 Italian miles, 15 German miles, and 20 large French leagues, such as those of Provence and Gascony, or 30 small ones, as here in the Île-de-France and its surroundings. And according to this estimation, the first terrestrial distance between two places differing only in latitude is easy to determine, as between Paris and Narbonne, cities situated almost under the same meridian. If from the latitude of Paris, 48 degrees 45 minutes, we subtract that of Narbonne, 43 degrees 40 minutes, there will remain 7 degrees 5 minutes of difference, which multiplied by 20 French leagues will give 141 leagues, or 423 Italian miles of distance in a straight line. It is in this second kind of terrestrial distances that Jacquinot began to follow the error of Apian, Stœffler, and many others, in which he has also been followed by those who have augmented and reprinted his treatise. We therefore say that the distance between two cities situated under the same parallel is not the arc of the said parallel comprised between the two meridians of the said two cities, as Jacquinot and David Robert, with an infinity of others, have imagined, but rather the arc of a great circle comprised between the said two cities, which arc determines the shortest and straightest path on the globe of the Earth, or the shortest distance from one of the said cities to the other. So that it is one thing to give the ratio of a parallel or of its degrees to the equinoctial, or to its divisions; it is another thing to give the arc of the said equinoctial, or of another great circle that can be comprised between the said cities situated under the said parallel. This being well understood here, it will be easy to recognize the error in the examples of David Robert and others who have here augmented Jacquinot's brief discourse with several impertinences. As when, to show that the measurement of differences in latitude cannot serve for differences in longitude outside the equinoctial, they tell us this reason: inasmuch as the terrestrial form, narrowing towards the poles, cannot be measured equally, except at the equator, or 18 degrees higher or lower. As if the terrestrial form were spheroidal and not spherical, and each meridian an ellipse and not a circle. It is indeed true that the space comprised between two meridians continually narrows from the equinoctial to the poles, even though the said meridians comprise the same quantity of degrees of each parallel as of the equinoctial, inasmuch as each such degree is as much smaller and different from each degree of the equinoctial as its parallel, of which it is a part, is smaller and different from the equinoctial. But this does not imply anything about the terrestrial form towards the poles.

[Correcting Terrestrial Distance Measurement]
The only remedy, therefore, that they have brought us to determine the just distance between two cities situated under the same parallel is none other than to reduce the degrees of the said parallel comprised between the two cities to equinoctial degrees, and to multiply the product by the quantity of miles or leagues corresponding to each equinoctial degree. In this, they have indeed given us the distance of the said cities in miles or leagues, taking the path along the said common parallel, but not their shortest distance, the path of which must be taken along an arc of a great circle, which would be represented by a string stretched and taut from one city to the other. And whoever wishes to recognize the difference between their measurement and the true one can do so by means of two circles, one representing the equinoctial, and the other the parallel under which two cities would be situated. For taking the same straight line for that which can and must be imagined to be drawn directly from one city to the other, and subtending it to the said two circles, one will see that it will comprise a much larger arc of the small circle than of the great one. Now, the arc of the distance must be equal to the arc of the greatest circle. Also, the measurements of Jacquinot, Stœffler, de Mermes, Robert, and others are greater than the true ones; and therefore they are not just, nor are their tables useful for this subject, except simply for knowing the difference in miles or leagues of each of the parallels to the equinoctial and of their degrees to its degrees.

[Finding Distance on the Universal Planisphere]
On the Universal Planisphere. Given two cities under the same parallel and under two different meridians, to find their distance. If the two cities are under the equinoctial, the difference of their meridians, multiplied by leagues or miles, will give their just distance. If the said cities are distant from the equinoctial, let the difference of their longitude be taken. Then, placing the rule or movable horizon on the equinoctial line or straight parallel, let the parallel of the proposed cities be counted between the said parallels from the equinoctial. And supposing one of the said cities, which has less longitude, to be under the outer meridian, let their difference in longitude be counted from it along the said parallel. And at the end of the numeration, let the point of the movable brachiolum be moved, or a triangle be formed with the two points marked on the said rule, the vertex of which falls precisely on the said end of the numeration. Then let the said rule be turned so that it represents the horizon of one or the other of the said cities, that is to say, that it represents their polar elevation. Then the said movable point, or the vertex of the same triangle, will show between the parallels, counting from the pole, the quantity of equinoctial degrees, that is to say, the arc of the great circle comprised between the said two cities, which, multiplied as said, either by miles or by leagues, will give you the shortest and just terrestrial distance between the said cities.

[Example: Paris and Tübingen Distance]
EXAMPLE. We will use the example where David Robert found fault. Let Paris and Tübingen be proposed, in latitude 48 degrees 40 minutes, but Paris under 15 degrees 5 minutes, and Tübingen under 50 degrees 5 minutes of longitude, the difference of which is 35 degrees. Having therefore placed the movable horizon on the equinoctial and the movable point of the brachiolum, or the vertex of a triangle, on the intersection of the seventh meridian, counting from the extremity of the rule, with the parallel of 48 degrees 40 minutes, counting from the equinoctial; transferring the rule to the common latitude or polar elevation of 48 degrees 40 minutes, counting from the pole, the said point or vertex of the triangle will be found stopped on the 5th parallel, counting from the same pole. Therefore, you will say that the arc of the great circle comprised between the said cities is 35 degrees, which multiplied by 20 French leagues will give 700 leagues of shortest and just terrestrial distance, or 2100 Italian miles. This number of leagues was rather guessed by Robert than found by a certain way with the aid of his table; for the aforesaid difference of 35 degrees, by his method, is only to be multiplied by 4 ¼ according to his said table, which will produce only 148 ¾ leagues for the distance of the path on the common parallel, and nevertheless he concludes 150, which we have found for the just distance on the great circle passing through both cities.

[Terrestrial Distance Calculation Challenges]
This third type of terrestrial distance in a straight line, as well as the preceding second, should be considered diversely, namely: the two proposed cities being both situated towards the same pole, or towards different poles; or both under the same or different meridians and hemispheres; and again, situated one from the other towards different poles, and under different meridians and hemispheres. But it would be too long and tedious to pursue here the necessary practice in all these different situations to know the distances. We will content ourselves with showing that just as this subject surpasses the practice of common astrolabes, so too it seems to have been little understood by the authors of the second and third impressions, who have greatly augmented and amplified this treatise with examples, as much as by Jacquinot himself. This is so that those curious about such and similar propositions may instruct themselves elsewhere than in their treatise on the practice of astrolabes, and above all, that they do not follow their method, which depends in no way on the astrolabe, but is purely arithmetic, though poorly ordered. And in fact, their method of practice, by extracting square roots, although drawn by them from Apian in Cosmographia, Münster, Stöffler, and others, can only give the required result approximately, more or less, whatever compensation one might make between the two latitudes. This is done by adding half of their differences to the lesser latitude to obtain an average latitude, which, with the difference of the longitudes, forms a rectangle. The extracted square root of its diagonal is then supposed to be the arc of the great circle comprised between the two proposed places. For if this distance were to be measured on a flat surface, their procedure would be founded on reason. But it is quite different on the convex surface of the terrestrial globe, which submits only to the laws of spherical triangles and the planisphere. For, in a word, all this calculation depends on the solution of an oblique spherical triangle with two known sides and the angle they comprise. The said two known sides are the two complements of the known latitudes, and the known angle is the difference of their known longitudes. By means of these, one seeks the side opposite the known angle, which side is the arc of the great circle comprised between the two proposed places. For this prompt and easy solution, the universal planisphere is greatly useful, as it is generally for all other triangles, which we will show in the practice of the examples added in the second and third impressions.

[Finding Distance on the General Planisphere]
On the General Planisphere. Given two places towards the same pole in the same hemisphere, but under different parallels and meridians, to find their distance. Let the Mountains of the Moon, source of the Nile, be proposed, having 11 degrees of south latitude and 57 degrees of longitude; and the Great Promontory of Aspre in Ethiopia, also in south latitude, of 1 degree and 7 degrees of longitude. Both are thus situated towards the same pole and in the same hemisphere. Their distance is sought. Let the rule or movable horizon be drawn and aligned on the equinoctial, and the movable point of the brachiolum applied. Or rather, let a triangle be formed whose apex falls on the intersection of the parallel of the lesser latitude (to be counted from the equinoctial towards one or the other of the poles, which will be taken for the Antarctic pole) with the meridian of the difference of longitudes (to be counted from the greatest and outermost to the extremity of the rule). Then, the rule being transferred to the parallel of the greater latitude (12 degrees, counting from the pole, that is to say, it represents the horizon of the place which has the said 11 degrees of latitude or elevation of the pole), the point of the said brachiolum, or the apex of the same triangle, being found fixed on the 16th parallel, and 7 degrees counting from the same pole, will show you the number of degrees comprised between the two proposed places, which is 16 degrees and 15 minutes. These, multiplied by 50, will give you 487 and a half French leagues, a more accurate and shorter measured distance, and not 494 or 7, as others have found. The same will be practiced for the following example, particularly for that of the distance from Paris to Constantinople. For the rule placed on the equinoctial, and the point of the Brachiolum on the intersection of the lesser latitude of Constantinople (45 degrees, counting from the equinoctial) with the term of the difference of longitudes (that is to say, with the 21st meridian, counting from the extremity of the rule). This being transferred to the latitude of Paris (48 degrees 45 minutes, counting from the pole), the movable point will be found fixed on the 11th parallel, counting from the same pole. And by so many degrees will be the arc of the great circle comprised between Paris and Constantinople. This arc, multiplied in French leagues, will give 690 leagues of distance and the shortest path on the globe of the earth. To avoid being further tedious by enumerating an infinity of other different examples that could be proposed and solved by the planisphere, we refer those who wish to acquire a more particular and perfect knowledge of this to the works of those who have treated it thoroughly, such as Nonius in Chapter 10 of the 2nd book of his Observations, Magini in Problems 15, 16, and 17 of the 1st book of his Primum Mobile, Pitiscus in his Geographical Problems, and particularly to the treatise on it by Petrius Cunerius at the end of his Trigonometry.

[Understanding Winds and Their Origin]
XXXIII. PROPOSITION. To have knowledge of the winds, and from what quarter they proceed. Since winds sensibly change the air and dispose bodies in this lower region, sometimes causing heat, cold, rain, or fair weather, it will be appropriate to know what wind is and whence it proceeds. Wind is an exhalation drawn from the earth by the virtue of the Sun and the stars, hot and dry at its first birth, which (after having been repelled by the cold, being in the second region of the air) moves obliquely around the earth and comes from diverse parts of the horizon. For this reason, winds have diverse names according to the parts of the world from which they issue and blow. To find, therefore, at a certain time what wind reigns and from what part of the horizon it proceeds, one must dispose the astrolabe with its back upwards towards the four cardinal points of the world (by the 25th proposition) in a place where the wind can come naturally, without any reverberation or impediment from a wall or other thing. And erect a movable banner at the center of the back, which, agitated by the wind, will turn so that the hinge or weathercock of this banner will show you the wind that at that moment blows and reigns around the back of the astrolabe, where we have described the four cardinal winds, with their collaterals: namely, on the line of the true East: Subsolanus, and its two collaterals Eurus and Vulturnus. And opposite, towards the Western part, Favonius or Zephyrus, and its two collaterals, Aphricus and Caurus. Similarly, we have described in the Southern part, Auster or Notus, wind of the sea; and beside it, its two neighbors Euroauster and Austroaphricus. Consequently, in the North is described the wind named Septentrio or Apardias, with its circumjacent winds, Circius and Boreas. As appears by the figure below.

[Nature and Effects of Winds]
And it must be understood that among these winds, some are salutary, others pernicious and unhealthy, according to the place and region from which they proceed. For Subsolanus, the wind from the true East, is hot, dry, pure, and subtle: it engenders clouds, makes trees flourish, and gives health to bodies; its collaterals are of the same nature, except that Vulturnus, the wind from the summer East, dries everything. And the winds opposite in the Western part are cold and humid, causing diseases, rains, and thunderstorms. After the wind that comes from the South, with its two collaterals, they are hot and humid, engendering many diseases and heavy rains.

[North Winds: Cold, Dry, and Beneficial]
Finally, the North winds, with their circumvolutions, are cold and dry, dispelling rain, bringing health to bodies, but harmful to the flowers of trees and the fruits of the earth.

[Ascensions of Signs and Arcs of the Zodiac]
It must be noted that the ascension of a sign, or another arc of the zodiac, is the arc of the equinoctial rising with it, or the space of time during which that arc rises above the horizon. Also, it should be understood that ascensions are of two kinds: namely, right and oblique. Right ascensions are those considered on the horizon of the right sphere, and oblique ascensions on the oblique horizon. And in one and the other, a sign is said to rise directly when more than 30 degrees of the equinoctial rise with it, or when it takes more than two hours to rise above the horizon; and obliquely when less than 30 degrees rise with it, or when it does not take two full hours to rise above the said horizon. Furthermore, it is to be considered that commonly these ascensions begin at the first point of Aries, except when one wishes to find the particular ascensions of a sign or degree, or of other arcs of the ecliptic. What Jacquinot says, that in both the right and oblique horizon a sign is said to rise directly or obliquely, should not be understood in such a way that in the right sphere one imagines the Sun or another star (being in whatever sign of the entire Zodiac) can rise or descend obliquely on the horizon; but rather that in comparison of the Zodiac with the equinoctial in the right sphere, as the parts of the said Zodiac are diversely inclined to the horizon, so also there are some which approach more the rectitude of the equinoctial than others. These, on this occasion, are said by Jacquinot to have a right ascension, and the others an oblique ascension. And inasmuch as the equinoctial always rises equally and uniformly, marking off by equal spaces of 15 degrees equal periods of time from hour to hour, that of the signs of the Zodiac, or a portion and arc of the said sign which comprises in the space of its rising more or less space of the equinoctial, is said to rise more directly or more obliquely. As in the said right sphere, the two signs Leo and Scorpio and their opposites, and Aquarius and Taurus, each comprise more than 30 equinoctial degrees, and therefore are said to rise directly and tardily. Those of Aries, Cancer, Libra, and Capricorn each comprise less than 30, and therefore are said to rise more obliquely and promptly. This also being considered in every other oblique horizon in the same comparison of the equinoctial rising, it can be said that there are signs or portions which rise more directly than others.

[Finding Ascensions in the Right Sphere]
You must place the end of any sign, or other arc of the ecliptic, on the right horizon in the East; and the rule, placed and situated at the beginning of Aries, will show you their right ascensions on the circle of hours, from the point touched by the rule to the right horizon. EXAMPLE: Desiring to know the ascension of the last degree of Taurus, I place its end on the right horizon in the East; and by directing the rule to the beginning of Aries, I see between the point touched by the rule and the extremity of the right horizon that there are 57 degrees for its right ascension. But if one wishes to know the particular ascension of the said sign of Taurus, one must only move the rule and transfer it to the beginning of the said sign; and from the mark it touches on the limb, up to the right horizon, there will be its particular ascension. As by the preceding example, leaving the end of Taurus on the right horizon, and the rule placed at the beginning of the said sign, I find on the limb its particular ascension to be twenty-nine degrees 54 minutes. And note that in the right sphere, 4 signs, namely Gemini, Cancer, and their opposites, rise and set directly, and the other 8 obliquely. Of the twelve signs of the Zodiac, the four closest to the equinoxes, namely Aries, Cancer, Libra, and Capricorn, rise promptly and obliquely, each with 17 degrees 54 minutes of the equinoctial; the four furthest and closest to the solstices, namely Taurus, Leo, Scorpio, and Aquarius, rise tardily and directly, each with more than 30 degrees, namely 31 degrees 11 minutes of the equinoctial; but the four middle ones, Gemini, Virgo, Sagittarius, and Pisces, although absolutely taken and entirely, they should be said to rise obliquely, as each rising with less than 30 degrees, namely with 19 degrees 54 minutes of the equinoctial; nevertheless, by reason of their medieties considered separately, they can be said to rise sometimes obliquely, sometimes directly, for their half towards the equinoxes rises with less than 15 degrees, as 14 degrees 37 minutes of the equinoctial, but the other towards the solstices rises with more than 15 degrees, namely with 15 degrees 17 minutes, all in the right sphere; which anyone can more particularly recognize by the table of right ascensions, by continuous subtraction of the ascension of each sign from that of its next following, thus the differences between the signs and their parts or degrees will be the particular ascensions required.

[Another Method: Using the Universal Planisphere]
On our universal planisphere, this proposition is easy to practice, provided that one represents to oneself another hemisphere opposite to the one presented by the said plate, in such a way that both the ecliptic line and the equinoctial line are considered two great circles, as they are the common sections with the exterior meridian or the plane of the said plate, and thus of the right line which represents the circle of 6 hours, or the circle of the colure of the equinoxes: at the intersection of which, with the two aforementioned, establishing the beginning of the numeration of the signs and degrees of the Zodiac and of the equinoctial degrees, coming to terminate a quarter of 90 degrees at the meridian towards the right hand, and from there, towards the left hand, again another quarter of 90 at the point of the equinoxes, and the third at the same meridian in its opposite part towards the left hand to return and finish the entire circuit of 360 degrees at the same equinoctial point. Making this distinction, that the said ecliptic line, or the circle it represents, is divided into 30 by 30 degrees by signs, and the said equinoctial line, or the circle it represents, its numeration continues from the first degree through 90, 180, 270, up to 360 and the last.
