These things presupposed and understood, if you desire to know the right ascension of any sign of the Zodiac or portion thereof, look under which meridian is its beginning and under which other its end: for the quantities will be such meridians counting from the point of the equinoxes, such will be the right ascensions of the beginning and end of such a sign, and thus of the beginning and end of any portion thereof. EXAMPLE: For example, of the first degree of Taurus, you will see it under the 27th meridian and 54 minutes counting from the equinoctial point; and therefore you will say that such is its right ascension 27 degrees 54 minutes, and for the last degree of the same sign, seeing it under the 57th meridian with 48 minutes, you will say that such is also its right ascension. Now let the 15th degree of Scorpio be sought in the ecliptic; inasmuch as it is found under the 42nd meridian with 30 minutes, counting from the said equinoctial point taken for Aries in the opposite hemisphere: if you add the two preceding quarters with the said 42 minutes, you will have 222 degrees 30 minutes of the equinoctial for the right ascension of the 15th degree of Scorpio.

[Finding the Ascension of Stars]
To find the ascension of the stars.

[Right Ascension of Stars]
To know the right ascension of the stars that are described on the Astrolabe, place their point on the right horizon, and the rule at the beginning of Aries. Then count from the right horizon the degrees of the limb up to the point where the rule lies, and you will have the said ascension.

[Example: Spica Virginis Right Ascension]
EXAMPLE. If I wish to know the right ascension of the star Spica Virginis, I place its point on the right horizon in the Eastern part, and by aligning the rule with the beginning of Aries, I find its right ascension to be 194 degrees, between the point touched by the rule and the right horizon.

[Alternative Method for Right Ascension]
ALTERNATIVELY. I would prefer to practice these last two propositions in this manner. Let the beginning of Aries be placed and fixed on the spider (from which all right ascensions and longitudes are counted and begin) on the right horizon or the equinoctial line on the western side, such that the rule applied to it precisely coincides with the beginning of the limb's division. In this case, the rule, transferred to each degree of the Zodiac or point of each star whose right ascension one wishes to know, will show on the said limb, by the quantity of degrees and minutes counted from the said right horizon up to the said rule, the true right ascension of the said degree or star. And it is to be noted that this method is all the more prompt and easy as it is entirely simple and natural, consisting only of a single circumduction of the rule successively over all the degrees of the Zodiac, or over all the stars marked on the spider, which remains immobile and invariable, and in the same constitution as the Zodiac and the stars are in the Sky. Also, in most Astrolabes, particularly our smallest one, the limb on the mother's side is found divided into its 360 degrees by a continuous numbering, starting from the right horizon on the Western side, so that by applying and fixing the beginning of Aries there, the rule, circumducted according to the natural sequence of the signs, will easily show all the right ascensions.

[Right Ascension on the Planisphere]
On the general planisphere. This proposition differs in no way from the practice of the preceding one, except that instead of the degree of the ecliptic, one must take the point marked for the star whose right ascension we wish to know (that is, under which and what meridian it is marked, counting from the first that passes through the point of Aries). But there is this precaution: one must carefully distinguish the hemispheres and not mistake one for the other, that is, that of Aries for its opposite of Libra, nor vice versa. To avoid this, we would find it appropriate to mark and distinguish the stars of the second hemisphere by two small circles, in order to know more easily and promptly in which quarter of the sphere each will be, and thereby to know its true right ascension, counting from the point and beginning of Aries. EXAMPLE. For example, the star Arcturus, marked with two small circles, and passing through the hemisphere of the East, is found under a meridian distant from the point of Aries by 19 degrees and 41 minutes. Therefore, adding the two preceding quarters, you will say that it is distant from Aries by 289 degrees and 41 minutes, and that this is its right ascension.

[Example: Hircus Right Ascension]
Conversely, the star called Hircus, marked with a single small circle, is in the first hemisphere, and being found under a meridian distant from the point of Aries by 21 degrees and 16 minutes, you will say that this is its right ascension.

[Oblique Ascension of Signs]
XXXVII. PROPOSITION. To know the ascension of the signs in the oblique sphere. Place the beginning of a sign, or another arc, on the oblique horizon of your table, with the rule, marking on the limb the point it touches. Then turn the zodiac until the end of the sign, or arc, comes directly onto the oblique horizon, leaving the rule on its beginning. This done, you must count the degrees on the limb from the first mark touched by the rule to the second, and thus you will have this oblique ascension. EXAMPLE. I wish to know how long the sign of Taurus takes to rise at an elevation of 48 degrees. To do this, I place its beginning on the oblique horizon of this table, with the rule, and note the corresponding point on the limb. Then I turn the zodiac with the rule until its last degree falls on the said horizon. Thus, the space between the two marks shows me that its particular ascension is about 19 degrees. And it must be understood that in the oblique sphere, six signs, namely from the beginning of Cancer to the end of Sagittarius, rise directly on our horizon and set obliquely, but the other six, from the first of Capricorn to the end of Gemini, rise obliquely and set directly.

[Simplified Oblique Ascension Method]
* One should not take such trouble as to want to move the spider of the Astrolabe without changing the position of the rule, for just as it does not ordinarily happen in Astrolabes that the movement of the spider causes the rule to move by the same arm, so too is this not absolutely necessary in the practice of this proposition. It suffices only, after having marked on the limb the passage of the rule fixed on the first degree, or beginning of some sign, or arc, and portion thereof constituted on the oriental oblique horizon, to freely turn the spider until the last degree or end of the said sign or arc is precisely situated on the said oblique horizon. And this done, to again transfer the said rule to the said beginning or first degree of the said sign, and to mark on the limb the point on which it passes, for the quantity of degrees and minutes comprised between the said two marks will be the required oblique ascension.

[Another Oblique Ascension Method]
ALTERNATIVELY. We will also give here another method for practicing this proposition, as for knowing the oblique ascension, both of some particular sign or arc taken in its entirety, as in the preceding manner, and of each degree or point in each sign and arc of which Jacquinot has not spoken; then, since the oblique ascension of some point or degree in the Zodiac is nothing other than the degree of the equinoctial co-ascending on the proposed oblique horizon; and the oblique ascension of some sign, the space comprised between the two degrees of the equinoctial co-ascending, one with the beginning, and the other with the end of the said sign: all the difficulty lies in finding the said co-ascending degrees. If, therefore, one wishes to know the oblique ascension of some point or degree of the Zodiac, that point or degree will be brought and fixed on the Oriental oblique horizon, marked on the plate appropriate for the proposed place; then the rule being transferred to the beginning and first degree of Aries, the number of degrees comprised on the limb between the right horizon of the same eastern side and the said rule, will be the oblique ascension of the proposed point or degree. That is to say, that the degree of the equinoctial co-ascending with the said proposed degree is as distant from the vernal section as the said rule is on the limb from the right horizon. This being practiced for the two extremities of some line or arc, the difference of the two products, that is to say the difference between the two distances from the said vernal section found for the co-ascending degrees of the equinoctial, or else the space between the said degrees, will be the required oblique ascension of the whole line or arc.

[Oblique Ascension of Taurus]
For example, if the first degree of Taurus is placed and fixed on the oblique Eastern horizon of the plate used for Paris, and the rule is moved and fixed on the first degree of Aries, you will see that from the direct horizon of the same Eastern side up to the said rule, there are 14 degrees 19 minutes of the equinoctial arc comprised on the limb: this is the oblique ascension for Paris of the first degree of Taurus. Similarly for the last degree of the same sign, having placed and fixed it on the said oblique Eastern horizon, and the rule being transferred and fixed on the first degree of Aries, you will see from the direct horizon up to the said rule 31 degrees 59 minutes of the equinoctial arc comprised on the limb, and this is the oblique ascension of the last degree, or end of the said sign of Taurus. Also, the difference between these two oblique ascensions, namely 18 degrees 30 minutes, will be the oblique ascension of the entire sign, that is to say, that with the entire sign and at the same time, it rises solely on the horizon of Paris for 18 degrees 30 minutes of the equinoctial arc.

[Jacquinot's Terms and Oblique Sphere]
We note two things regarding what Jacquinot says here. First, an impropriety in these terms of rising and setting directly or obliquely in an oblique sphere, for no signs or degrees can rise directly on an oblique horizon, but some rise more slowly and with more time than others, so that according to this more or less time, it seems ridiculous to say they rise directly or obliquely, given that time changes nothing of their ascensional obliquity which is natural to them in the oblique sphere; it would be more acceptable if he said more or less directly or obliquely. Also, what he says, that in an oblique sphere 6 signs rise directly and the other 6 obliquely and descend conversely, is not absolutely true in all oblique spheres, for up to an obliquity of 19 degrees the contrary will be found, but above 20 degrees Jacquinot's statement will hold, which everyone can examine by the tables of oblique ascensions by Montroyal and others.

[Universal Planisphere and Oblique Ascension]
This proposition can be easily practiced on the universal planisphere, by means of direct ascensions known with the ascensional differences, for either their difference in one case, or their sum in the other, gives the required oblique ascension, inasmuch as the ascensional difference is nothing other than the difference between the parts of the equinoctial arc, with which a sign or degree rises in the direct sphere, and those with which the same sign or degree rises in the oblique sphere; and in short, it is the difference between the direct ascension of a sign or degree, and its oblique ascension. We will therefore propose first.

[Finding Ascensional Difference on Planisphere]
PROPOSITION. Given a degree in the Zodiac, to find its ascensional difference by the universal planisphere. Let the horizontal rule be placed in such a position that it represents the horizon of the proposed place, and let the point on it of the rising of the said degree be marked, that is to say, where the parallel of declination of the proposed degree cuts it, for the number of meridians comprised between the said point and the direct meridian will be the required ascensional difference. EXAMPLE. Let the 1st degree of Taurus be proposed. The rule being situated on the 48th degree 45 minutes of polar elevation for Paris, the parallel of declination of the first degree of Taurus will be found to cut it at a point distant from the direct meridian by 13 degrees 15 minutes, and such is the ascensional difference of the said degree on the horizon of Paris. SECOND EXAMPLE. The end of Taurus or the beginning of the 1st degree of Gemini in the same situation of the horizontal rule will be found to rise, that is to say, its parallel will cut the said rule at a point distant from the said direct meridian by 24 degrees, or minutes and 49 minutes: and thus will be the ascensional difference of the said last degree of the.

[Finding Oblique Ascension on Planisphere]
PROPOSITION. Given a degree in the Zodiac, to find its oblique ascension by the universal planisphere. Let the direct ascension of the proposed degree be found by the 35th proposition, and its ascensional difference by the last preceding one. If the said degree is of a northern sign, the difference of these two; or if it is of a southern sign, the sum of the two will be its required oblique ascension. EXAMPLE. Let the beginning and end of the sign of Taurus be proposed. The beginning has a direct ascension of 27 degrees 54 minutes, and an ascensional difference of 13 degrees 25 minutes; inasmuch as it is a northern sign, the difference will be its oblique ascension; that is to say, subtracting 13 degrees 25 minutes from 27 degrees 54 minutes, the remainder 14 degrees 29 minutes will be its required oblique ascension. Similarly for the end, whose direct ascension is 57 degrees 48 minutes and ascensional difference 24 degrees 49 minutes, the difference 32 degrees 59 minutes will be its oblique ascension.

[Corollary: Equidistant Degrees and Oblique Ascension]
COROLLARY. Since two degrees in the Zodiac equidistant from the same equinox rise at two points equidistant from the equinoctial sections, and mark on the planisphere two points equally distant from the direct meridian, one necessarily being northern, and the other southern; it will also necessarily happen that the oblique ascension of one will decrease as much from its direct ascension as the other increases. For example, the 1st of Scorpio, having 207 degrees 54 minutes of direct ascension, and the same ascensional difference as the 1st of Taurus, namely 13 degrees 25 minutes, inasmuch as it is southern, it will increase its said direct ascension by the said 13 degrees 25 minutes, by which the 1st of Taurus decreases it: and therefore its oblique ascension will be 221 degrees 19 minutes: Thus, if a degree rises more directly, its opposite will rise more obliquely.

[Descent of Signs in Direct and Oblique Sphere]
XXXVIII. PROPOSITION. On the descent of signs, both in the direct and oblique sphere. For the descent, there is no need to give a rule, because in the direct sphere the ascension and descent of a sign are always the same. And in the oblique sphere, the descent of a sign is found by the ascension of its opposite, to which it is always equal. EXAMPLE. The descent of the sign of Taurus will be 41 degrees, because the ascension of the sign of Scorpio, which is its opposite, is found to have that many degrees: also the descent of Scorpio will be 19 degrees, which is the ascension of Taurus; and so for the others: by dividing these degrees by 15, you will have the hours and minutes in which these signs rise and set. This means during which, or in the space of which, the said signs rise and set.

[Descent on the Universal Planisphere]
On the Universal Planisphere. This proposition receives no difference on our universal planisphere from what was said in the preceding one, whether one uses the same proposed degree for its required oblique descent, or whether for the same subject one takes the opposite degree, to know the oblique descent of the other by the oblique ascension of the one.

[Finding the Four Angles of the Sky]
XXXIX. PROPOSITION. To find the four angles, or centers of the sky, namely the four principal houses. It must be understood that the entire sky is divided in several and diverse ways.

[House Calculation Principles]
Some are for measuring hours and other periods of time, which are reduced to the equinoctial, according to the movement of the first mobile. The others are for the distinction of the proper movements of the stars, which refer to the 12 celestial signs of the ecliptic of the first mobile. And because, in addition to the time and movements mentioned above, one considers the influences of the stars according to both movements and their diverse situation, the ancient philosophers divided the sky into twelve equal parts, which are commonly called the 12 houses. This is done by 12 permanent circles (as we said in the declaration of the parts), of which the 2 principal ones are the oblique horizon and the meridian, which always distinguish the four cardinal parts of the world, namely East, North, West, and South: which are called the first, 4th, 7th, and tenth houses. To find and know their beginning, one must place the degree of the Sun between the Almicantaraths, at the same altitude as you will have found by the back of the Astrolabe, and the degree of the zodiac that falls in the eastern part of the oblique horizon will be the Ascendant angle.

[Horoscope and House Angles]
which we call the horoscope, or first house, of which the opposite degree, which falls on the said horizon in the West, is the western angle, otherwise called the seventh house. Consequently, the degree that is directly on the meridian line will be the angle of the Midheaven, called the 10th house, and its nadir, which touches the midnight line, is the angle of the Earth, called the fourth house.

[House Calculation Example]
EXAMPLE: Wanting to erect a figure for someone born on April 26th, the Sun being in the 7th degree of Taurus, I take the altitude of the Sun on the back of the Astrolabe, which I find to be forty-six degrees after noon. Then, the degree of the Sun being placed and positioned at the same altitude between the Almicantaraths, I find the twenty-second degree of Virgo falling on the oblique horizon in the East, which will be the horoscope of that nativity; and the twenty-second degree of Pisces in the West, which is its opposite. Similarly, on the meridian line, I find the 10th degree of Gemini; and on the midnight line, the 26th degree of Sagittarius, which is its nadir. These we have arranged in this Geometric figure, as is customary in Astronomy.

[Altitude and Time Considerations]
This should be understood in cases where the hour is not known, for otherwise it would not be necessary to take the said altitude; also, the Sun does not always shine, nor at all hours that one would want to practice this proportion, nor even most often in the place where one would want. In which case, the rule placed on the known hour, and the degree of the Sun also known, being moved, one will have what one desires, following Jacquinot's doctrine. Nevertheless, it will always be safest, if the sun is shining, to take the altitude.

[Critique of Jacquinot's Method]
There is something to criticize in what Jacquinot says here, which the authors of the second and third impressions should have rather noted, than to entirely change Jacquinot's example and commit a greater impertinence in theirs. What concerns Jacquinot is of little importance, yet it deserves to be noted. To erect, he says, a figure for someone born on April 26th, one must take the altitude of the Sun, etc. Now, at the instant of birth, the altitude of the Sun is no longer useful; one must otherwise have the hour and moment of the said birth known to erect the said figure, and place the degree of the Sun on the known hour and do the rest of what Jacquinot teaches. It is another thing if, at the instant and moment of the said birth, one wants to practice this proportion and know the hour otherwise unknown; thus, what Jacquinot says serves to erect a celestial figure for the instant of someone's birth or for some other present subject, and not for a person already born, or another past subject. The greater impertinence noted in later impressions is to have added the known hour; for to erect a figure for a person born on June 2nd, 34 hours after noon, the Sun being in the 3rd degree of Cancer, they began by taking the altitude of the Sun on the back of the Astrolabe, which they found, they say, to be 34 degrees, and they place the said degree of the Sun on a similar Almicantarath. A very ridiculous thing, even if it were not impossible, having knowledge of the hour at which a person was born, to nevertheless afterwards take the altitude of the Sun to know the said past hour. Such observations only serve for the knowledge of the present hour and the momentary instant of observation, and not for the past. They therefore teach to seek and know something notorious and already known by other means, by a method that would be useless if it were unknown.

[Corrected House Calculation Example]
We will therefore revert to their example as follows, and as it can be practiced and known on our small Astrolabes. Let the birth in Paris of someone on June 24th, 1611, at 6 hours 45 minutes and 34 seconds after noon, the Sun being precisely in the 3rd degree of Cancer, for which one wishes to erect a figure of the constitution of the Sky. Let the Alidade or rule be placed on the proposed hour, namely 6 hours 45 minutes 34 seconds after noon, and the said degree of the Sun, 3rd of Cancer, be guided and stopped under the said rule, so that it corresponds precisely under the celestial line of the Alidade. Then one will see the 17th degree of Aries fall on the eastern oblique horizon, which will be the horoscope of the said birth. The 17th degree of Libra on the other side falls on the western oblique horizon, on the meridian line, the 15th degree of Capricorn, and at the Nadir or midnight, the 19th degree of Cancer, about 5 minutes of Virgo: and these are the 4 principal houses required for the instant of the said birth, which the following figure represents.

[Altitude for Unknown Time]
It will be another matter if, at the instant of the said birth, one wishes to know the hour by the sun, for it will be necessary to take the altitude, and to erect the said figure, place its degree on a similar Almicantarath, or opposite the hour known by observation.

[General Planisphere Usage]
All these following propositions, up to the end of this treatise, are made to be practiced on the universal planisphere without any other aid; and therefore, we will content ourselves with having only reported those whose construction and manner of practice have been found simple and easy.

[Erecting the Twelve Houses]
XL. PROPOSITION. To know how to erect the twelve houses of the sky at all hours and at all times. There are two principal ways to erect the houses of the sky, one of which is given by the ancients, and the other by modern astrologers, namely by Regiomontanus and Peurbach. And although they differ little among themselves, the modern method has some advantage in convenience, and is that which is commonly used today. Nevertheless, we will teach the practice of both. Some add a third method according to Campanus, which we do not find to have yet been treated in the use of the Astrolabe.

[Regiomontanus House Method]
To erect a figure according to the method of Regiomontanus, which among others is the best and most reasonable, it is necessary to know the hour precisely by the Sun, star, or clock: and to place the small rule, with the degree of the sun, on that hour (as we have demonstrated above) keeping the zodiac firm in place.

[House Division by Angles]
Then you will find the four angles by the preceding doctrine, which will be for the beginning of the first, fourth, seventh, and tenth houses. For the other eight, you will look at the eight degrees of the ecliptic that fall upon one and the other part of the four great circles described both above and below the horizon, in the tables of regions: namely, for the second house, the degree of the zodiac that is on the next arc of the oblique horizon, extending to the midnight line, and the third in a similar following arc. Consequently, for the fifth and sixth, you will consider the two arcs between the angle of the earth and the West. And after having established these first six without looking at the parts of these arcs, you will be able to find by similar degrees of the opposite signs the other six houses: for the first is opposite to the seventh (as we have already said), the second to the eighth, the third to the ninth, the angle of the earth to the tenth, the fifth to the eleventh, and similarly the sixth to the twelfth.

[Figure for Example]
Figure for example.

[Jacquinot's Astrological Methods]
This proportion, with the three following, concerns judicial Astrology, where Jacquinot, in conformity with previous Astrolabographers, treats methods for erecting celestial figures, distinguishing the twelve houses of the Sky, figuring nativities, finding horoscopes or ascendants, recognizing the diverse aspects, and finally representing all the revolutions of the aforesaid things, of which, besides that they require another place, we will speak here only indifferently, it not being our goal either to lead the reader to it or to dissuade him from it; we will only content ourselves with saying that what Jacquinot notes in these last propositions, which are vulgar, common, and easy, is but a means between two very difficult, rare, and little known things: the first being a great precision and certainty in observations, the other a long and circumspect experience. The first can give us the calculation and the figures, and the latter will assure us a more certain judgment. And we will say furthermore that it is always very pleasing and agreeable to be able at all times to figure and represent, as in a painting, the apparent constitution of the Sky and the arrangement of its phenomena, not only for the present hour, but also for any other time, past or future: as, besides Jacquinot's figure, which is passable for his time, it can be seen in the one we add here.

[Second Method of House Division]
The second method for dividing the houses according to the ancients. You can furthermore (according to the ancients) find the twelve houses of the sky by the doctrine that follows. First, you must know, as above, the four angles, then direct the degree of the ascendant to the arc of the eighth unequal hour, and the degree that falls on the midnight line is the beginning of the second house. Afterwards, bring back the said ascendant to the end of the tenth unequal hour, and the degree you find on the said midnight line is the beginning of the third house. This done, take the degree of the seventh, and direct it to the second unequal hour, then the degree of the sign that falls on the midnight line will be the beginning of the fifth house. Similarly, you will turn the said degree of the seventh to the fourth unequal hour, and the beginning of the sixth house will fall on the said midnight line, so that you will have the first six houses: And by their opposite degrees, without an Astrolabe, you will be able to find the beginnings of the other six, which will be the twelve houses of the sky, according to whose disposition we can judge the events of the world, by placing in them, by ephemerides or other tables, the planets and fixed stars.

[Alternative House Division Method]
You can also by another calculation (according to this second method) find the aforesaid twelve houses, by placing the rule on the degree of the ascendant, and marking the point of the limb that it touches. After this, divide into three equal parts the arc that is from the said point to the meridian line, and the rule, placed at the first division which is on the horizon, will divide and distinguish the beginning of the twelfth house by the degrees of the ecliptic. Also, this rule, moved to the second division, extending to the meridian line, will show the eleventh house. Similarly, the arc that is from the point marked at the ascendant to the midnight line, divided into three equal portions, will teach us the beginning of the other houses: namely the second by the first division marked below the horizon, and the third by the second division, extending to the midnight line, by directing the rule to their beginning as already said, and by the opposite degrees of these, you will know the beginning of the other houses as before.

[Understanding Aspects of Stars and Planets]
The aspect of stars, both fixed and erratic, is nothing other than a certain habit they sometimes have together in the sky, according to which they communicate their lights and influences more sensibly. And although the said aspects can be known without the Astrolabe, nevertheless, because we have spoken of the twelve houses of the sky, concerning astrological judgments, in which aspects are required, it is appropriate to speak briefly of them according to the degrees of the ecliptic, which is the most universal method, for as for other ways of practicing the said aspects, tables are required, such as those of Regiomontanus, or others. It must therefore be understood that, including conjunction among the aspects, there are five types: namely, Conjunction, Opposition, Trine, Square, and Sextile.

[Calculating Aspects with the Astrolabe]
Therefore, if you wish to know the said aspects by the Astrolabe, you must understand that all degrees or stars distant by two signs, which is the sixth part of the whole circle, regard each other with a Sextile aspect, like the first of Aries to the first of Gemini. Furthermore, if you see three signs or ninety degrees between two stars, which is the fourth part of the said circle, it will be a Square aspect, like the first of Aries to the first of Cancer. Moreover, if you find a distance of four signs, which are one hundred twenty degrees, or the third part of the ecliptic, it is a Trine aspect, as would be the first of Aries to the first of Leo. Furthermore, if the said places are distant from each other by six signs, or 180 degrees, which is half of the circle, they will regard each other with an Opposition aspect, like the first of Aries to the beginning of Libra. Finally, when two stars are together in the same sign and degree, this is called a Conjunction. And it is necessary to know that of the said aspects there are three: namely, the Sextile, the Square, and the Trine, which are double, looking to the dexter and to the sinister: The sinister aspect is made according to the order of the twelve signs of the Zodiac: and the dexter, on the contrary, against the order and succession of the said signs.

[Dexter and Sinister Aspects]
EXAMPLE. The Moon being in the first of Aries regards Jupiter in the beginning of Aquarius with a dexter sextile aspect: and on the other side regards Venus being in the first of Gemini with a sinister sextile aspect, and so for the others, of which you have the figure described hereafter. Furthermore, it must be understood that of the said aspects some are good and temperate, and others bad, according to the repugnance or similitude of the places where they are made, or according to the quality of the stars found in the said places. As the Sextile is made of two signs agreeing in one quality, such as Aries and Gemini, similar in heat, which is an aspect of moderate friendship.

[Trine Aspect and Enmity]
And the Trine is made of two signs of the same nature and quality, like Aries to Leo, which are hot and dry, wherefore it is an aspect of perfect friendship and agreement. But aspects of enmity are made in signs repugnant in one or two qualities, thus the Square is an aspect of medium enmity, and the Opposition of complete hatred. This is esteemed malefic, both for the distance from one place to another, and for their contrariety.

[To Know the Horoscope of Revolutions]
To know the horoscope and ascending degree of the revolutions of the world, or of nativities, or other commencements.

[Revolution of the World's Years]
The revolution of the years of the world is when the Sun returns to the first degree and minute of the sign of Aries, which in our time is around the tenth of March, where we have the spring equinox.

[Revolution of Nativities and Beginnings]
The revolution of nativities, or of other things, such as a building, an election, or other commencements: is when the Sun re-enters the same degree and minute as it was at the hour when these things began.

[Calculating Revolutions with the Astrolabe]
Therefore, to know the horoscope of the said revolutions each year by the Astrolabe, you must have a root and a certain time of the commencement of that thing, or of one of the preceding revolutions, and note that time by the hours and minutes on the limb of the Astrolabe for your root. Afterwards, for each subsequent year, you must count from the point of that root on the Astrolabe 87 degrees and 19 minutes, or five hours and about 49 minutes, taking 15 degrees for one hour, and four minutes for one degree. Then set the rule over the end of that number, under which you will bring the degree where the Sun was at the time of the root of that revolution. This done, you will have the horoscope with the other angles, according to which you can find the other 12 houses of the sky, as has been said above, and erect the Astronomical figure to judge the disposition of that revolution.

[Example: World Revolution 1544]
EXAMPLE. Wanting to know the revolution of the world for the year 1544, inasmuch as in the year 1541 the revolution occurred in Paris on March 10th at 10 hours 44 minutes after noon, which I had marked on the limb of the Astrolabe as a root for subsequent years, I find that from the time of that root until the year 1544 there are two years. Therefore, I count on the limb of the Astrolabe from that root twice five hours and 49 minutes, or twice 87 degrees and nineteen minutes, which are one hundred seventy-four degrees and thirty-eight minutes. And I find at the end of these degrees that the revolution falls on ten o'clock in the morning, and about five degrees and a half, which are worth twenty-two minutes, opposite which I set the first of Aries with the rule, and I see the fourth of Cancer fall on the oblique horizon, which will be the horoscope for the said year.

[Revolution of the Year 1544]
Revolution of the year 1544, March 10th at 10 hours 21 minutes before noon, for latitude 48.

[Table for Solar Revolutions]
But because it would be a laborious thing to always count from the root as many times 87 degrees and 49 minutes on the limb of the instrument as there are years passed, we have added here a table, by which we can easily find the said revolutions, by looking at the years from the root up to that of your revolution, by entering with the number of these described below this title, the number of years, on the left side of the table, opposite which on the right hand you will find the number of degrees that you must count on the limb of the Astrolabe from the mark of the root until the end of these degrees: thus you will have the hour when the revolution occurred, and you will erect the 12 houses of the Sky, as has been said.

[Using the Table for Revolutions]
And if it happens that the years since your root are not found in any of the numbers on the left side, you must take the nearest below, and then enter with what is lacking from the total number, and add these degrees found together twice, always rejecting 360 if necessary, and count the remainder from the place of the root, according to the order of the description of the hours.

[Example: Revolution Calculation with Table]
EXAMPLE. Suppose there were 25 years from the root of a nativity until its revolution. I first enter the table with 20, which is the nearest lower number, opposite which I find 306 degrees and 19 minutes. And because there are still 5 years remaining, I go to the number 5 in the said table, where I find 76 degrees and 35 minutes. These numbers joined together result in 382 degrees and 54 minutes: therefore, by rejecting 360, which is the whole circle, there remain 22 degrees and 54 minutes, which must be counted from the place of the root, and there bring the place of the Sun with the rule. Thus you will see the hour when the said revolution begins, and its horoscope, as has been said.

[Jacquinot's Time and Equinox]
This refers to Jacquinot's time, for at this time, according to our small Astrolabes, we have, as has been said in several places, the spring equinox around March 11th.

[Recognizing Celestial Changes]
In this last proposition, Jacquinot proposes to us to recognize, by means of the Astrolabe, the various changes in the Sky and the new encounters of the stars that accompany the returns of the Sun to the same points, places, and degrees of the Zodiac in which it will have been calculated or observed and recognized when something remarkable has presented itself; which we still say is a sufficiently powerful and agreeable thing, provided it is practiced exactly, but which cannot be practiced very justly except with the aid of some ephemerides. It is true, however, that for the Astrolabe one can without sensible error

[Using Tables for Astrolabe]
use some table proper to this subject, like the one our Author has placed here. This table of solar or annual revolutions, placed here by Jacquinot, is found in all Authors who have written on the use of the Astrolabe, even in the Catholicon or universal of Gemma Frisius, but none discovers its origin, and does not give the reason, although the thing is easy to construct. Nevertheless, its utility being great, it should not have been despised and passed over in silence. Now its utility is such that if once the place of the Sun has been examined and recognized for some subject that has presented itself, one can by means of the said table, know and recognize promptly for several past years, and as many others soon to come, the passages and returns of the Sun to the same place, and this quite precisely without the aid of any Ephemerides. Adrian Metius, having come across this subject in his Astronomical institutions, in book 2, chapter 10, where he speaks of celestial figures, although exact everywhere else, fell asleep in this place, for he who reformed all his tables and submitted his calculation to the observations of Tycho Brahe, as more truthful than any preceding ones, instead of this vulgar table of Stoeffler, Jacquinot and others, decided to copy a more vicious one found in the ephemerides of Stadius, without giving any reason for its construction. We therefore say that as the surest and most exact practice on this proposition depends

[Solar Year Calculation]
absolutely from the knowledge of the exact quantity of the Solar year, which can only be obtained by two means: either by observation, or by Ephemerides well and accurately calculated. Likewise, the construction of the aforementioned table must be derived from this, which is nothing other than the excess of the quantity of the Solar year over the quantity of the common Civil year, reduced to hours and minutes, converted into degrees of the Equinoctial, and accumulated upon itself by continuous addition for one year, always rejecting both the entire circle of 360 degrees and the entire day of 24 hours.

[Tycho Brahe's Solar Year Observation]
This excess is recognized by the observation of the Sun's entry into the same point of the Zodiac for two consecutive years. Thus, Tycho Brahe noted that the Sun always entered later into the same point the following year by 5 hours, nearly 49 minutes. Therefore, the Solar year, which is the return of the Sun to the same point after the complete revolution of the entire Zodiac, is found to be 365 days, 5 hours, nearly 49 minutes.

[Solar vs. Civil Year Accumulation]
Now, just as all years follow and are counted by continuous addition and accumulation of 365 days, which is the quantity of the common Civil year, so too are Solar years (what Jacquinot and others call Solar revolutions) counted by continuous additions and accumulations of the 5 hours 49 minutes beyond the said 365 days of the common Civil year.

[Ephemerides and Equinoctial Degrees]
This excess is also recognized by the Ephemerides, and is nothing other than the difference in hours and minutes of the Sun's entry into the same point of the Zodiac. This difference, as in the said Ephemerides, for whatever quantity of years one may propose, is always found to be less than 24 hours. Also, reduced as it is, into degrees and minutes of the Equinoctial, it is always found in the aforesaid table to be less than 360 degrees, due to the rejection of the entire circle when the sum of the added differences exceeds it. And since by experience and observations this difference is found to be almost always equal from one year to another, at least in these times, so too by continuous addition of the same number, either in hours or in Equinoctial degrees, one can construct a table to serve for several hundred years without sensible error.

[Table Reduction to Hours and Minutes]
But we deem it more appropriate to reduce this table to hours and minutes of hours, and for greater ease in the practice of this proposition, even on the Astrolabe, inasmuch as our root being known to us in hours and minutes, the addition or subtraction will be made much more easily from the said differences placed in hours and minutes, than in degrees and minutes, without changing anything of the method of practice prescribed by Jacquinot, except that it will always be more prompt and more appropriate to join together the hours of the root with those of the difference, so that, counting from midday, one turns the rule for as many hours, and stops it at the end of the whole number of hours and minutes. For then the first degree (as for our example above) or other degree of the Zodiac of the Scorpion known as the root, being brought and stopped under the said rule, one will know the horoscope or ascending degree on the oblique Eastern horizon, with the three other principal points: under the meridian, on the West, and under the North or Imum Coeli, which are the four principal angular and cardinal houses of the figure of the said sought revolution; and similarly for the other houses according to the degrees of the Zodiac that will be found stopped under their circles.

[Leap Year Correction for Revolutions]
We will add here this remark, that it is necessary and absolutely essential to make and observe in the practice of this proposition and in the use of the aforesaid table or another, such as the one we will put hereafter calculated in hours and minutes. This is that one must differentiate between leap years and common years: For example, if the proposed year in which one wishes to know the revolution of the Sun is a leap year, the number of years since or before the known root, being marked in the aforesaid tables, will give directly the true number of hours at the end of which the revolution must take place, or has previously taken place in the proposed year; but for the true day, it can only be obtained by decreasing the total sum or remainder (after the addition or subtraction of the hours found in the said tables) by one entire day, or 24 hours.

[New Revolution Table and Usage]
We will therefore conclude this last proposition of Jacquinot's Astrolabe with a new table of Revolutions, by a new exposition and example of its use, and by a new figure, all in conformity with our small Astrolabes.

[Table Construction and Precision]
This table that we propose has been calculated by all in hours and minutes of hours by a continuous addition of the excess of the Solar year over the common Civil year; and although Tycho Brahe constructed particular ones for each of the 12 signs of the Zodiac, nevertheless we have considered that a single one could suffice for all, both because of the small difference between them of only a few minutes, and because this scrupulous precision, for which all the said tables were particularly made, is neither absolutely necessary nor in any way recognizable on the Astrolabe.

[Revolution Table]
Years, H. M. | Years, H. M. 1: 5.48. 2: 11.49. 3: 17.24. 4: 23.12. 5: 0. 6: 10.48. 7: 16.36. 8: 21.24. 9: 4.12. 10: 10.0. 20: 10.1. 30: 6.1. 40: 16.2. 50: 2.2. 60: 12.2. 70: 18.3. 80: 4.4. 90: 14.4. 100: 0.5. 200: 10.10.

[Table Usage for Solar Revolutions]
For the most certain use of both this table and Jacquinot's, it should be noted that for the proposed years, before or after some known root, for which one desires to know the revolution of the Sun, the nearest preceding leap year will always be taken. And by its difference from the root noted in the aforesaid tables, the number of hours and minutes found opposite will be taken. This being subtracted from the root for preceding years, or added to it for subsequent years, and from the remainder or sum, after subtracting one day, one will have the day, hour, and minutes of the Revolution in the said leap year. This being set as a new root, then by the difference of the said first proposed year, one re-enters the said table, and the number found opposite, added to the said new root, will give the time and moment very close to the true Revolution of the proposed year.

[Example: 1614 to 1625 Revolution]
We will take as an example the Figure we added above on the 40th proposition, in which we have as a certain root, the place of the Sun at the 10th degree, 54 minutes, 50 seconds of Cancer, at the time of a certain birth, on the 1st day of June 1614, at 6 hours 45 minutes after midday, and for the horoscope or ascending degree the 28th degree, 55 minutes of Virgo. If then one desires to know the revolution of this birth, that is to say, both the hour of the Sun's return to the same 10th degree, 54 minutes, 50 seconds of Cancer, and the new constitution of the entire Zodiac accompanying the said return in the next year 1625, taking the next lesser leap year 1624, with its difference from the root 1614, namely 10 years, I enter the aforesaid table, and find opposite 10 hours 0 minutes, which added to the root 6 hours 45 minutes gives the 1st of June, 16 hours 45 minutes; from which, having subtracted one day because the year 1614 is a leap year, there remains the 31st day of May, 16 hours 45 minutes for a new root, in the said year 1624, which differs from the proposed year 1625 by only one year, with this difference entering into the table, the number found opposite...

[Revolution Calculation Example]
to know 5 hours 48 minutes, added to the number of the new moon, May 1st, 16 hours 45 minutes, gives May 31st, 21 hours 35 minutes, that is to say, the first day of June at 10 hours 55 minutes in the morning or before noon. The revolution will be from the Sun's entry into the 10th degree, 54 minutes, 50 seconds, from which degree of the Sun, being guided and stopped under the rule placed on the 10 hours, 55 minutes of the morning marked on the limb, one will see on the Eastern horizon the Ascendant degree, or the horoscope, to be the 18th degree, 51 minutes, 56 seconds of Cancer. At the Midheaven, the 20th degree, 00 minutes, 15 seconds of Aries. At the setting, which is the angle of the 7th house, the 18th degree, 51 minutes, 56 seconds of Capricorn; and under the Imum Coeli, the 20th degree, 00 minutes, 15 seconds of Libra, and so for the others as they are found under the other circles, which must then be arranged in a Figure, as has been said before, and as the following clearly shows.

[Figure of Revolution 1619]
Figure of Revolution 1619. Before noon. We have here changed the common practice of the Tuscan tables, because for lack of distinguishing bissextile years from common ones, it will often happen that one will be in doubt after the necessary addition or subtraction of hours and minutes, to which day the compound or residue should be attributed, to be the true day of the revolution: Thus in the example above, having taken 15 hours 48 minutes for 11 years, and these added to the 8 hours 45 minutes of the root, the whole will be June 1st, 11 hours 45 minutes, as Jacquinot and others teach: And yet it must be said the last day of May with 21 hours 53 minutes, that is to say June 1st, at 10 hours 55 minutes before noon.

[End of Astronomical Propositions]
End of Jacquinot's Astronomical Propositions.

[Addition of Gnomonic Propositions]
ADDITION. We have also found it appropriate to add here some Gnomonic propositions, which can be easily practiced on the Astrolabe, and are useful for the speculation and construction of an infinity of various quadrants or sciotheric clocks, both horizontal and vertical, for any sphere position or polar elevation, even on declining or inclined planes.

[PROPOSITION I: Horizontal Hour Arcs]
PROPOSITION I. Given the height of the Pole of any place, to find the horizontal hour arcs. This proposition serves for the description of horizontal quadrants or clocks. Let the rule be drawn and stopped on the inner side or limb of the Astrolabe, on each 15 degrees of the limb, counting from the meridian line on one side or the other, that is to say on the points of 1, 2, 3 hours, etc., or on those of 11, 10, 9 hours, etc., and let it be counted on the oblique horizon of the proposed place (for which we assume that a suitable and particular plate will have been prepared beforehand) how many Azimuths are comprised between the said rule and the said meridian line, for the number of said Azimuths or vertical circles will be the number of horizontal degrees due to the arc of the hour, before or after noon, on which the said rule will be stopped, taking care however, if the rule does not precisely cut the said oblique horizon at a point where one of the said Azimuths terminates (as rarely happens, especially in small and medium Astrolabes where the said Azimuths are only traced by 3s or by 5s) to estimate as accurately as possible in degrees and minutes the space comprised by the rule on the said oblique horizon, beyond the last of the said Azimuths.

[Example: Horizontal Hour Arc for Paris]
EXAMPLE. For example, to find the horizontal hour arc of one hour for Paris. Having drawn and stopped the rule on the point of 1 hour on the limb, one sees on the oblique horizon that from the point where the meridian line cuts it, to the said rule, there are about 11 Azimuths and 13 parts, which give 11 degrees and 13 minutes for the horizontal arc of one hour before or after noon. Thus, the rule placed on 2 hours after noon or 10 in the morning, the horizontal arc of 2 hours will be found to be about 15 degrees 16 minutes. NOTE. To know the number of hours inscribable on a horizontal quadrant or clock for any proposed place, one must place the rule on the point where the oblique horizon of the plate appropriate for the said place terminates with the circle or tropic of Capricorn, which is the largest and outermost circle of the said plate, and see on the limb at what hour the said rule is stopped, for that hour on one side towards the West shows the hour or part of the sunrise on the longest day of the year, and therefore the first inscribable on the said horizontal clock, and on the other side towards the East, the hour of sunset on the same longest day will be known, and therefore the last inscribable on the said clock.

[General Planisphere Method]
On the General Planisphere. Let the rule or movable horizon be drawn and stopped on the center of the given height or polar elevation, so that it represents the horizon of the proposed place; and let the points through which the hour circles pass be marked on it, and this counting from the largest circle or exterior meridian up to the point where the said rule cuts the Tropic of Winter; for the said rule being transferred to the equinoctial or right horizon, will show between the said hour circles, counting from point to point, beginning from the extremity tending towards the center, the required horizontal hour arcs. For example, for Paris, having drawn and stopped the rule on the 48th degree 45 minutes, counting from one or the other of the poles, etc., and having marked on it the points of common section with the said hour circles, the said rule transferred to the right horizon shows between the meridian circle and the first point 11 circles and about 13, that is to say 11 degrees and about 13 minutes for the hour arc of 1 hour after noon, or 11 hours before noon (because two hours equally distant from noon comprise equal and similar hour arcs); between the same meridian and the second point will likewise be found 15 degrees about 16 minutes, by as many circles or meridians comprised by the horizontal arc of 2 hours after noon or 10 hours in the morning, and so for the others.

[Note on Hour Arcs]
NOTE I. It is to be noted that we only need to mark the said points from the exterior circle to the meridian up to the intersection of the rule with the Tropic of Winter, inasmuch as for any proposed place, this space measures the semidiurnal arc of the longest day, and doubled determines the quantity of the said entire longest day. That is to say, taken in one way, it gives the morning hours, and returning gives those of the afternoon, so that one can recognize, by this space, the number of hours inscribable in a horizontal clock for the said proposed place.

[Note on Hour Symmetry]
NOTE II. It is also to be noted that for convenience one can be content with the arcs of the 6 hours comprised from the meridian up to the line which represents the circle of 6 hours, inasmuch as two hours equidistant both from noon and from the circle of 6 hours, always comprise similar and equal horizontal hour arcs, thus the space from 5 hours to 6 will serve for that from 6 to 7, that from 4 to 5 will give that between 7 and 8, and so for the others, without going further than to the Tropic of Cancer or Summer, so as not to include more than the useful and necessary hours.

[PROPOSITION II: Vertical Hour Arcs]
PROPOSITION II. Given the height of the pole of any place, to find the vertical hour arcs. This proposition will serve for the description of vertical quadrants or clocks.

[Measuring Vertical Hourly Arcs]
Let the rule be moved as stated in the preceding proposition, and successively stopped on all the hours of the limb, and note how many Almicantaraths or circles and degrees of altitude will be comprised between the said rule and the Zenith or vertical point of the particular and proper plate for the proposed place, cutting the first and principal vertical circle, namely the Azimuth passing through the said Zenith and terminating at the two vernal sections or points of common section in the East and West of the equinoctial circle with the oblique horizon: for the number of said Almicantaraths comprised between the said rule and the said vertical point or zenith will be the number of degrees due to the vertical hourly arc of the hour, on which the rule will have been stopped.

[Example: Vertical Arc for Paris]
For example, for the vertical arc of a space of 2 hours before or after midday at the elevation of Paris, the rule being placed on 10 hours or 11 hours on the limb, that is to say, on 30 degrees of distance from the arc or from the other side of the line or point of midday, it is found that between the said rule and the vertical point or zenith, there are 2 Almicantaraths and about 3/4, which cut the said vertical circle, and therefore it will be said that the vertical hourly arc of 1 hour, before or after midday, is 20 degrees and about 51 minutes, and so for the others up to the hour of the equinoctial sunrise or sunset, where the said vertical circle terminates on both sides, which marks and shows that in the quadrant or vertical dial only the 6 equinoctial hours should be described.

[On the General Planisphere]
Inasmuch as just as the horizontal hourly arcs are parts or portions of the horizontal circle comprised between the hourly circles, and as the lines of the horizontal hours are the common sections of said hourly circles with the horizon: So also the vertical hourly arcs are portions of the vertical circle comprised between the said hourly circles, and the lines of the vertical hours are common sections of said circles with the said vertical. Therefore, the rule being situated and stopped, as prescribed in the preceding proposition to represent the horizon of a proposed place, it is necessary to imagine another line which, from the middle of the said rule or center of the said horizon, rising perpendicularly or at right angles, goes to meet the zenith of the said place, which represents the first or principal vertical circle passing through the said zenith and through the points of common section of the Equinoctial circle with the said horizon, on which vertical circle are, as stated, comprised and counted the vertical hourly arcs marked by the points of their common sections with the hourly circles. And as the point of each zenith is distant from the pole by the complement of the polar elevation on the horizon, which complement is equal to the Equinoctial elevation on the same horizon. Therefore, it is one and the same thing, or at least equal, that the Equinoctial elevation on the horizon and the polar elevation on the first and principal vertical circle; which the movable rule can easily represent, successively: Thus, counting from one or the other of the poles, their elevation on the said vertical circle, and transferring and stopping the rule there, it will represent the said vertical circle for the practice of the present proposition.

[Marking Hourly Circles on the Rule]
This being premised and practiced, or at least understood and supposed, the points through which the said hourly circles pass will be marked on the said rule, as in the preceding, and this counting from the meridian or outer circle, drawing towards and up to the center of the rule only, where the line representing the 6-hour circle passes (inasmuch as the plane of the said quadrant or vertical dial is only susceptible of 6 hours before and as many after midday). The rule thus marked, being transferred to the Equinoctial or direct horizon, will show between the said hourly circles the value of the required vertical hourly arcs.

[Example: Equinoctial Elevation]
Let, for example, the Equinoctial elevation for Paris be counted as 41 degrees 15 minutes from one or the other of the poles, and at the end of the numeration, the rule is guided and stopped, and on it is marked the point through which the 15th meridian or 1st hour circle before or after midday passes below the cut, and so for the other hours and their parts: then the rule being transferred to the Equinoctial will show between the same circles, between the extremity of the rule and the first point, 10 meridians and about 1/2 comprised, which give 10 degrees and about 10 minutes for the vertical hourly arc of 1 hour or 2 hours: and so for the others.

[III. PROPOSITION: Drawing Dials]
Given the horizontal or vertical hourly arcs, to draw a horizontal or vertical dial.

[Constructing a Dial]
Let two lines be drawn, cutting each other at right angles, one of which will represent the midday line, and the other the 6-hour line, and a compass point being placed and stopped on the point of their common section, let a circumference or portion of a circle be described with the other point, on which, with the aid of some divided quadrant or a proportional compass, the said known hourly arcs will be marked, and this counting on both sides of the midday line, then from the same point or center of the said circumference to the points of the arcs marked thereon, straight lines being drawn, these will be the required hourly lines, on which the shadow of the gnomon or style of the dial should mark the hours exactly; which needs no other example than the two following Figures, of which the first represents the said gnomon, style or banner, and the other the two dials, one vertical above, and the other horizontal below.

[Figure of the Gnomon]
Figure of the gnomon.

[Note I: Dial Placement]
It is to be noted that besides the construction of said dials as above, it is also necessary to know and understand their correct situation, and therefore for the horizontal dial, a very flat and level surface or parallel to the horizon will be chosen, on which, having drawn a meridian line, the said dial will be placed and affixed in such a way that its midday line corresponds precisely to the said meridian line. And for the vertical, an equal and flat surface will be chosen or erected, such as a perpendicular wall on the horizon, which faces directly South or North.

[Note II: Gnomon Construction]
It is also necessary, in addition to the constructions and positions above, to prepare a gnomon, or style indicating the hours by its shadow, which can be obtained by preparing a triangle similar to that which is made on the general planisphere by the rule with the 6-hour line in the constitution of the said rule, as it is prescribed in the first of these gnomonic proportions for the horizontal dial, and in the second, for the vertical, imagining a third line, which falls from the extremity of the said 6-hour line (that is to say, from the pole) perpendicularly onto the said rule; for having made a Gnomon or banner of iron, wood or other similar material to the said triangle, such as the one we have just represented above, and this gnomon being attached perpendicularly to the plane of the said dial, so that the angle which holds and is made at the center of the horizon or middle of the rule, is applied to the center of the dial: In this state, the shining Sun will mark by the shadow of the said gnomon (that is to say, by the shadow of its side which relates to the said triangle, to the 6-hour line, and represents both on the planisphere and on the said dial the axis of the world) the common equal hours required.

[IV. PROPOSITION: Vertical Plane Declination]
Given the declination of a vertical plane, to find the elevation of the pole on it, and the meridian line.

[Vertical Plane Declination]
We call the declination of a vertical plane, the inclination that a vertical plane, like a straight wall erected upon the horizon, has to the circle properly called vertical, that is to say, to the circle which, passing through the Zenith of some proposed place, terminates at the horizon at the points of the true equinoctial East and West; or else the indifference that there may be, that the proposed wall does not face directly South or North: and this inclination or difference can be known by means of a meridian line drawn near the wall, and continued up to it, of which mention was made in the 14th proposition above, or by means of a small compass and magnetized needle, whose declination is known and marked for the proposed place. And by the meridian line of the plane, we mean the line of common section of the meridian of the plane with the plane itself; and just as the meridian of the place, cutting the horizon at right angles, generates upon it a straight line called the meridian line of the place, so the meridian of the plane (which is a great circle passing through the poles of the world and cutting the said plane at right angles) generates upon it, by its common section, a line called the particular meridian line of the plane or the line of the style, in which, and upon which, the style, gnomon, triangle, or banner of the describable sundial must be erected and fixed.

[Calculating Pole Elevation on a Plane]
Suppose then that the declination of a wall is known; let it be counted among the azimuths from the point of common section of the oblique horizon with the Equinoctial, drawing towards the Northern part of the plate for a meridional plane, and towards the Southern for a septentrional plane, on the East side if the wall declines towards the East, or on the West side if the wall declines towards the West: and let that azimuth be marked, among the said azimuths, which meets at the end of the numeration; then let the rule be turned so that it is recognized to touch one of the Almucantaraths at the same point where it cuts the said marked azimuth, and let a point or line be made on the said rule precisely corresponding to the line of intersection; this done, let the rule be transferred to the meridian line, and count how many Almucantaraths will pass and be comprised from the center of the said rule, up to the said marked point on it, for this number will be the number of degrees of the height, or elevation of the pole on the said proposed plane. Likewise, the number of Almucantaraths, comprised from the one that was found to touch the rule up to the Zenith, will give the arc of the difference of the two meridian lines, of which one (namely, the true one of the place, always marked and represented by a line on the proposed plane falling perpendicularly on the horizon) being always supposed given and known, the other will be likewise.

[Example: Wall Declination in Paris]
For example, a wall presents itself in Paris, exposed to the North, but declining from the properly vertical by an angle of 71 degrees and 15 minutes towards the West. Counting then the said 71 degrees and 15 minutes among the azimuths, from the equinoctial point of the oblique horizon on the West side towards the South, or on the East side towards the North: The azimuth which meets at the end of this numeration represents the proposed plane, on which, moving the rule until it touches one of the Almucantaraths at the point of their common section (that is to say, at the same point where the said azimuth cuts both the rule and the said Almucantarath), this done, a point or line must be marked on the said rule precisely at the said point of tangency or common section, and count from the Zenith up to the said touched Almucantarath; doing this, 39 degrees and 50 minutes will be found, and such is the number of degrees for the arc of the difference of the two meridian lines, namely 39 degrees and 50 minutes. Then, turning and transferring the said rule to the meridian line, it is found that between its center and the said point marked on it, nearly 11 Almucantaraths pass, namely 11 and a half, which show that the arc of the Polar elevation on the said proposed plane is 11 degrees and 55 minutes, and such will be the height of the style or quantity of the angle of the gnomon, triangle, or banner that must be placed perpendicularly on the said meridian line of the plane.

[General Planisphere Calculation]
Let the declination of the plane be counted from the straight meridian, or 6 o'clock line which passes through the center, towards the exterior meridian (it does not matter from which side); and from the pole, let the elevation of the equinoctial be counted between the parallels, that is to say, the complement of the latitude, or polar elevation of the place where the said proposed plane is situated; and on the common intersection of the two, let the rule be drawn and fixed for the movable horizon, and on it let a point or mark be made at the place of the said intersection; then let the number of parallels comprised be counted on the limb from the same pole up to the extremity of the rule, and such will be the number of degrees for the arc of the difference of the two meridian lines; similarly, transferring the rule to the equinoctial or straight horizon, the number of meridians comprised from the said mark up to the nearest limb, or end and extremity of the rule, will give the number of degrees for the arc of the elevation of the pole on the said proposed plane.

[Example: Wall Declination in Paris (Planisphere)]
For example, another wall presents itself in Paris, whose declination is known to be 55 degrees and 45 minutes: On which, to describe a sundial, one wants to know the height of the pole and its meridian line, or the line of the style. Having therefore counted from the straight meridian, or 6 o'clock line, towards the exterior meridian, the proposed declination of 55 degrees and 45 minutes, and from the pole towards the equinoctial, the equinoctial elevation on the horizon of Paris of 41 degrees and 25 minutes: On the common meeting or intersection of the two, let the rule be drawn, and on it at the same place let a point or mark be made: then counting from the same pole up to the extremity of the said rule, one will find on the limb 55 degrees and 30 minutes: for the arc of the difference of the two meridian lines, and transferring the rule to the equinoctial or straight horizon, one will count from the said point marked on it up to its extremity 21 meridians and a half comprised, and consequently the arc of the elevation of the pole on the said proposed plane will be given as 11 degrees and 50 minutes.

[Finding Hour Arcs on a Vertical Plane]
Given the declination of a vertical plane, to find the arcs of the hours describable on it. This proposition is practiced in as many different ways as there can be different declinations presented, of which we will note a good part as follows. Having recognized, as said in the preceding proposition, among the Azimuths, the one that represents the proposed plane. Let the rule be drawn and fixed on the point where the said Azimuth cuts the Tropic of Cancer: for in this case it will show on the limb the last or first hour inscribable on the proposed plane, namely the last, if the said plane is Meridional, or Septentrional Oriental; and the first, if it is Meridional or Septentrional Occidental, indeed even both when the plane is Meridional, slightly declining towards East or West: which will be known when the Azimuth representing the proposed plane cuts the said Tropic of Cancer at two points above the horizon, and to have the first hour inscribable on a Meridional, or Septentrional Oriental plane, whose Azimuth is cut by the said Tropic of Cancer at only one point above the horizon (as are all Azimuths and vertical planes exceeding in declination the amplitude of the said Tropic or of the Sun in it), one must place the rule on the point of the oblique horizon, on the East side, where the said Tropic of Cancer cuts it, and it will mark on the limb the first hour or part of an hour inscribable on the said plane, that is to say, the hour or part when the Sun, on the longest day of the year, will begin to shine on it.

[Calculating Hour Arcs on a Plan]
on the said plane. Conversely, to find the last inscribable hour on a similar Meridional or Septentrional Occidental plane, one must place the rule on the oblique horizon on the Western side at the point where the said Tropic intersects it. And to find the hour arcs, that is to say, the quantity of degrees comprised by the arcs of the inscribable hours on the said plane, one must successively move and stop the rule on each hour of the limb, and note, as stated in the preceding propositions, on which Almicantarath it cuts the Azimuth representing the proposed plane, counting from the Zenith: the number of Almicantaraths comprised between the Zenith and the point where the rule cuts the proposed Azimuth will be the number of degrees of which each hour arc will be composed, always counting from the meridian line and from its top downwards (that is, from the Zenith towards the Nadir, since the Almicantaraths have been counted from the Zenith of the plate, extending towards the horizon) and this by means of a circle which will be drawn on the said proposed plane, whose center will be taken on the meridian line. With this caution, however, that when the rule, being situated on the hour whose arc is sought, does not cut the proposed Azimuth at two points above the horizon, but only at one towards midday, in this case it will be necessary to take the number of Almicantaraths comprised on the midday side, or towards the Armilla, between the point where the rule cuts the said Azimuth and the oblique horizon, and this number added to the 90 Almicantaraths comprised on the other side between the Zenith and the same oblique horizon, to compose the entire arc of the said hour, whose term will always end in the said case below the horizontal line of the dial, that is to say, the line of the said hour drawn from the center of the dial through the point on the said circle terminating its hour arc, will always in the said case be drawn below the horizontal line of the said dial: We will demonstrate all this with an example.

[Example: North-West Wall in Paris]
EXAMPLE. Let a North-West wall in Paris be proposed, that is to say, a wall or other surface perpendicular to the horizon exposed to the North, but declining towards the West by 71 degrees 15 minutes. Having counted this declination among the Azimuths from the equinoctial Azimuth on the West side, up to the 71st, and towards midday, the said 71st will represent the proposed wall plane. To find and know the inscribable hour arcs on the said plane, let the rule be placed on the point of the Tropic of Cancer where the found Azimuth passes, and it will show on the limb about 3/4 of an hour, that is to say, about 36 minutes of an hour after midday, and at this hour the Sun on the longest day will begin to shine on the face of the said proposed plane; of which the arc to be taken from the top of the meridian line downwards, will be composed of 65 and 3/4 Almicantaraths comprised from the said point up to the oblique horizon, and of the 90 Almicantaraths comprised on the other side from the said oblique horizon up to the Zenith of the plate, and therefore it will be 155 and 3/4 degrees. And the said hour line will be depressed below the horizontal line of the describable dial, by an angle of as many degrees as the Sun, being in the said Tropic, and beginning to shine on the said plane, is found elevated above the horizon, namely 63 and 3/4 degrees. The arcs of the other hours will be found in the same way, by successively transferring the rule to the hours of the limb, marking the points through which it passes on the said Azimuth, and counting the Almicantaraths comprised between the said points and the Zenith of the plate, thus for one hour with 90 degrees above the horizontal line, there will still be found about 33 and 3/4 degrees below, which will compose the arc of 123 and 3/4 degrees for the line of 1 hour after midday; for 2 hours, the rule will show among the Almicantaraths about 74 and 1/2, and between the Zenith of the plate and the point where the aforesaid Azimuth passes; therefore the arc of 2 hours after midday, will be about 74 degrees 41 minutes. Thus that of 3 will be found to be about 58 degrees 16 minutes, that of 4 about 50 degrees 55 minutes, that of 5 about 46 degrees 5 minutes, that of 6 about 42 degrees 20 minutes, that of 7 about 39 degrees 18 minutes; and finally the rule being on the intersection of the Tropic of Cancer with the oblique horizon will show 8 hours for the last inscribable hour, whose arc will be known to be about 36 degrees by as many Almicantaraths as will be found comprised on the said Azimuth between the rule and the Zenith of the plate.

[General Planisphere Difficulties]
Concerning the General Planisphere. Because the practice of this proposition on the general Planisphere is enveloped in difficulty, and moreover still requires knowledge of the angle comprised by the meridian of the plane with that of the place, that is to say, the hourly difference of the proper meridian of the plane with the meridian of the place, which serves as a common and necessary prosthaphaeresis to arrive at the knowledge of the hour arcs, we will refrain from presenting its exposition here so as not to become tedious in the deduction of the particular means that must be employed. Also, by means of the preceding propositions alone, well understood, everyone will be able to easily construct and understand the reason for the construction of a sundial on any proposed vertical plane, whatever its declination, as well as on any plane inclined to the meridian and to the horizon, but straight to the circle properly called vertical, considering that what the declination of the plane is to verticals, horizontal inclination is to horizontals, and thus, by merely permuting one for the other, the rest of the practice and procedure will be entirely similar.

[Constructing a Sundial on a Given Plane]
PROPOSITION VI. Given a plane whose declination is known, together with the height of the Pole upon it, and its meridian line, with the hour arcs, to describe a sundial upon it. Although it is not our intention in this addition of propositions concerning Gnomonics or the theory of Sundials, to treat the methods of constructing them, which is a subject that well deserves to be reserved and destined for another place than this one: but only to make known and understood that just as in the solid and material Sphere all the theory of gnomonics is considered, so also in the Astrolabe, which is the projection of the Sphere onto a plane, the same thing can be considered and known. Nevertheless, we have added this last proposition to provide the method of putting into practice and use the knowledge of these last two propositions, as in the third we did for the first two. Let there be then on a line A. B, drawn to represent the meridian line, at a point C. on it as a center, described a circumference K. B. L. on which divided (either by means of a divided quadrant, or with a compass).

[Drawing Hour Lines on Declining Dials]
of proportion) are taken and marked on both sides, if necessary, counting from line A.B. the hourly arcs found by the preceding proportion, as at points D. E. F. G. etc. From the center C, lines C D. C E. C F. etc. will be drawn to these points, which will be the hour lines or common intersections of the hourly circles with the proposed plane. Then, on the same circumference, counting again from the same line A.B., the arc of the difference between the meridian line of the plane and that of the place is taken and marked, and at the end R. from the same center C. a line C R. is drawn, which will be the meridian line of the plane, or the style line. From this line, taking again on the same circumference (on either side, it does not matter) the arc of the elevation of the pole above the said plane, as R. S. and at the end S. drawing from the center C. a line C. S. this line, elevated above the said plane, according to the angle R.CS. will represent the axis of the world, and will give the height of the style or gnomon, which must be elevated perpendicularly above the said meridian line of the plane C. R. and whose shadow will mark the equal hours of the day on the aforesaid lines: which the following Figure, conforming to the example of the preceding proposition, will make known.

[Declining Horologe]
We do not speak here of horologes describable on inclined and declining planes, as this is a matter of too high speculation for this treatise and because it is necessary to consider in imagination many circles not described on Astrolabes.
