Also, after a strong speculation and clear knowledge of the preceding propositions, one can by oneself proceed to the research of the diverse and different encounters that are made of the circles and considerable lines in gnomonics on whatever kinds of planes they may be, however bizarre they may be. End of Gnomonic Propositions.

[Second Treatise of the Astrolabe]
THE SECOND TREATISE OF THE ASTROLABE. Containing the Use of Geometric Dimensions by the Altimetric Scale, or Geometric Quadrant described on its back, Astrolabe. After having sufficiently declared the use of the Astrolabe, insofar as it concerns cosmographical speculation: it remains to deduce the use of the Altimetric Scale or Geometric Quadrant described on the back of the said Astrolabe.

[Use and Utility of the Scale]
This Scale has two equal sides elevated perpendicularly one above the other, and is placed double, namely one in each quadrant of the back below the horizon, of which the lower part, crossing the line of midnight, is called shadow or straight scale, which is made by bodies elevated straight above the earth, such as a tower, and other similar things: And the other, which descends from the horizon on this straight scale equidistant from the line of midnight, is called inverse or inverted shadow, which is made by a body equidistant from the surface of the earth, as would be a pole fixed in a wall on which the Sun shines. And the said scales have 12 points (or rather degrees or divisions) by the proportion of which we understand the things we want to measure to be divided into similar numbers and proportions. Furthermore, the rule on the back takes the place of the visual line, that is to say, that which extends from the center of the eye to the summit of the terminated thing, or rather represents the ray of the Sun which touches the upper end of the thing one wants to measure, ending on the surface of the horizon. Similarly, the line of midnight which descends through the center of the Astrolabe, represents to us the things that are measured, and is placed in the place of heights and depths.

[Quantity and Proportion of Measures]
Before descending to the practice of measurements, it will be convenient to declare the quantity and proportion of those commonly used. And before that, it is necessary to understand that the dimension of things is found in three differences. The first is called Altimeter, by which all simple lengths are measured, such as the height of a tower, the length of a field, the distance between cities and other longitudes. The other is Planimeter, which is done in length and width, such as surveying land, and all other superficial measures. The third is Stereometer, or Solidimeter, which is in width, length, and depth: which belongs to solid bodies, vessels containing oils, wines, grains, and other similar things. Of these differences, in the use of the Astrolabe, only the first, which is simple, is commonly treated, by which the height of towers, trees, columns, the length of a field, the width of a river, the depth of a well, and other similar things are measured.

[Three Types of Dimensions]
These three types of dimension and measure, of which Jacquinot speaks, consider the three sorts of quantities that can be presented for measurement, namely lines, surfaces, and bodies. The manner of measuring surfaces is quite appropriately named Planimeter, from a name composed of Latin and Greek, and consists in measuring all terminated quantities that have length or width, such as all kinds of areas, spaces and superficial capacities, acres, perches and feet of land, plans of some cities and buildings, or other surfaces. The measure of bodies or solids is commonly called Stereometer or Solidimeter (not Solimeter, as Jacquinot and Robert too barbarously call it) and consists in measuring all solid quantities, and which have the three dimensions, namely length, width, and height or depth: such as all solid bodies, capacities of vessels, thickness of walls, and others of whatever material they may be. The measure of lines is called by Jacquinot Altimeter, as if to say measure of height (of which the Geometric Quadrant added to the back of Astrolabes, is vulgarly called altimetric scale) but inasmuch as the measure of lines consists as much in measuring those which are found extended in length on a horizontal or inclined plane to the horizon, as those which are elevated in perpendicular height: We therefore say that this kind of dimension requires a more significant name than that of Altimeter, and perhaps it would not be out of place to call it Mecometer, that is to say, consisting in the measure of all kinds of terminated lengths, and it is this Greek name derived from μήκος, that is to say length, which is the only dimension of lines such as the height of a tower, the length of a wall, and a simple distance from one city to another.

[Common Measures and Their Divisions]
To measure in this manner is therefore to know how many famous measures the line between the extremities of this length contains. We call famous measures those which have less inequality between them, and are more known to man, such as fingers, feet, paces, and other measures composed of them. What is to be understood by famous measures are all vulgar and commonly used measures, as being the most notorious, ordinary and current, as in Paris the toise is renowned for buildings and places: for the countryside, the perch: for merchandise, the ell: and for common measure, as a rule and justification of others, the King's foot: such will be in Paris, and in the surroundings, the famous measures. Elsewhere there may be others which will be so called, as the most notorious and used. It is very true that often in the absence of such certain and determined measures, recourse is had to others less certain, but more prompt and easy, such as paces, common and vulgar feet, arms, and others that one willingly equates to some of the aforesaid famous and certain measures. Therefore, it must be understood that a finger is the interval of four grains of barley laid out in width, and not in length. The Palm is composed of 4 fingers. The Foot of 4 Palms, or 16 fingers. The Cubit of one and a half Feet. The Toise of 6 Feet.

[Units of Measurement]
The rod or pole of 2 paces, or of 10 feet. The stadium of 125 paces. The Italian mile of 8 stadia, or 1000 paces. The French league is made of 2 miles. And although men's feet are found to be unequal, nevertheless it is customary to choose a proper one, by the proportion of which lands, buildings, and other public measures of a country are measured. This Foot, of which Jacquinot speaks here, is the Foot called the King's foot, or Parisian foot: but the division he gives of it into palms and inches is not currently in use. That is why we have added here another King's foot divided into three, with its common divisions of a cubit, by which one will know that the said King's foot, or Parisian foot (the common rule for all other measures of Paris and almost all of France) is composed of 12 inches, and each inch of 12 lines, each of which is estimated to have the length in diameter of a grain of barley taken according to its width divisible into other 12 parts, and so continuing always by continual subdivision in duodecimal progression: And even if such subdivision is not sensible nor cited below the said lines, it is nevertheless considerable and often necessary for encountering fractions. On the other side, opposite this division, we have placed that of the decimal, by which in continuous decimal progression the foot will be known to be divided into 10 first parts, and each first into 10 seconds, and each second again further divided into 10 thirds, and so continuously. And this last division is more prompt and easy for calculation than any other, as will be seen noted hereafter in its place on the use and practice of the geometric square marked on our small astrolabes that we have had divided in similar decimal progression. And no small advantage will be found in this conformity of division between the measured measure and the square, by means of which all the parts of one are always equally proportioned to the parts of the other. For in a word, it is to divide the measure by a division similar to that of the geometric square or altimeter scale.

[Geometric Square Proposition]
Regarding the Geometric Square. PROPOSITION Of the Geometric Square. To find the height of a tower, or other thing by the shadow of the Sun. Know that when the sun is elevated by 45 degrees, by the 5th proposition of the first treatise (which is only done in our region when the sun is in one of the first six signs of the Zodiac), then the sun renders the shadows exactly equal to their bodies, and the rule touches the 12th point of the scale between the direct and inverted shadow.

[Jacquinot's Statement on Solar Altitude]
What Jacquinot says here is true, that the height of the sun above the horizon of that region (like Paris and other places situated under the same parallel) cannot rise to 45 degrees, unless the sun is in one of the first signs of the Zodiac, that is to say, in one of the northern signs. Even elsewhere than in these signs, the sun cannot reach such a meridian height: because with the equinoctial height for Paris of 48 degrees 15 minutes, it still needs 3 degrees 45 minutes of northern declination of the sun to compose a meridian height of 45 degrees. Now this declination only corresponds to about 9 degrees 30 minutes of Aries, and about 10 degrees 30 minutes of Virgo; and therefore the sun cannot have 45 degrees of height, neither meridian, nor (for stronger reason) at any other hours of the day, unless it is in some degree of the Zodiac between the said 9°30' Aries and 10°30' Virgo. This occurs from the end of March until about the 12th of September. We note here first the great impertinence of Robert and others who have improperly falsified and changed Jacquinot's original text, having substituted a vicious restriction instead of a true consequence after the conclusion. For Jacquinot having said that the Sun, being elevated by 45 degrees, makes the shadows equal to their bodies, instead of what follows, that in this case the rule touches the 12th point of the scale between the direct and inverted shadow (that is to say, divides the geometric square into two equally): these good doctors have changed and substituted that they understand these things in countries that have the same latitude as Paris, as if this conclusion of Jacquinot was not absolutely true and universal, that the Sun elevated above any horizon by 45 degrees makes the shadows equal to their bodies.

[Shadows and Solar Altitude]
It is indeed true, however, that Jacquinot drew as a consequence what should have been considered first. For taking the height of the Sun with the Astrolabe, when it reaches 45 degrees, we first know that the rule falls exactly between the two scales of direct and inverted shadow, that is to say, it divides the geometric square into two equally; and from there we draw a true consequence, that the sun's ray represented by the rule, will cut, or rather will comprise in the same ratio the body and its shadow, as the rule comprises the lower side of the square representing the shadow, and the side that falls perpendicularly from the center of the said rule, which represents the body; the two triangles being similar, and therefore having their sides proportional. But in the small triangle on the Astrolabe, the lower side parallel and analogous to the shadow, is equal to the side that falls from the center of the rule parallel to the body: therefore in the large triangle the shadow will be equal to its body, in the said case of the sun's elevation of 45 degrees. The sun then being found by the Astrolabe, or otherwise, at the said height of 45 degrees above the horizon, if at the same instant one marks the extremity of the shadow of some upright elevated body, the measure, in length, of the shadow will be approximately the measure, in height, of the body. But for the less advanced, it is still necessary to say why, at such a sun height of 45 degrees, the rule divides the geometric square exactly in two, and is found stopped on the middle line or of the mean shadow. This is because the geometric square being inscribed in one of the quadrants of the back of the Astrolabe, and consequently in a quarter circle composed of 90 equal parts, the two sides of the said square being by its construction made equal and equally distant from the center of the Astrolabe, that is to say, from the center of the said quarter circle, it necessarily follows that they will meet on the said quarter circle each a point equally distant from their original lines, that is to say, that the side of the direct shadow, elevated on the line falling perpendicularly from the center to the horizon, will meet a point on the said quarter circle as distant from the said perpendicular line, as the point that the other side of the inverted shadow, elevated on the horizontal line, will meet on the said quarter circle, will be from the same horizontal line: and therefore the line drawn from the center through the concurrence of the said two sides of the square, that is to say, the diagonal line of the square, or line of the mean shadow: will also meet the said quarter circle at a point equally distant from the said two perpendicular and horizontal lines: and consequently it will cut the said quarter circle into two equal parts each of 45 degrees. Therefore, by contrary reason, whenever the sun or other luminous body is found elevated by 45 degrees, and therefore the rule is found stopped on the middle point of the said quarter circle, it will divide the said geometric square equally in two, making two right-angled triangles, similar and equal, whose sides will also be equal: and therefore the side of the direct shadow, equal to the side of the inverted shadow; but in the observation the side touching the geometric square.

[Shadows and their Bodies]
the side of the right shadow is parallel and analogous to the shadow of a body, and the side of the inverse shadow is parallel and analogous to the body, just as the rule is to the ray: therefore, the two sides of the shadow being made equal, it necessarily follows that the shadows also be made equal to their bodies.

[Measuring Heights with the Astrolabe]
It still remains, it seems, to say that just as, to justly observe the line which, forming one of the sides of the geometric square, falls from the center of the Astrolabe downwards, must be perpendicular to the horizon; and that which crosses it at the said center, forming the other side of the said square, must be well parallel to the horizon; (which happens when the said lines are well perpendicular one upon the other, and the Astrolabe is suspended in equilibrium) so also, to measure well, it is presupposed that the height one wishes to measure is perpendicular or elevated straight above the horizon: and the surface, upon which its shadow is projected and extended, is equal, well unified, and level, that is to say, chosen parallel to the horizon. And the more exactly these precautions are observed in the practice of all the proportions of this treatise, the more justly one will operate, and the more precise and exact measurements one will obtain.

[Note on Shadow Equality]
NOTE. We have said here that the shadow made by the sun elevated 45 degrees above the horizon is only approximately equal to its body; which is true regarding the apparent diameter of the luminous body, such as the sun and the moon. It would be otherwise if the light proceeded as from a single point. And therefore, in the practice of these propositions by means of the sun, it would be necessary, to have a just and precise measurement, to find at the extremity of the shadow the point that corresponds to the midpoint of the sun's body, that is to say, the extremity in the shadow where the ray that proceeds from it terminates, the sun being elevated 45 degrees, remaining extended with respect to the said midpoint. Because at the same time, and on the same plane, the upper part of the sun's body being more elevated, causes a shorter right shadow, and the lower part causes a longer inverse shadow, which this figure that we have added will easily make understood by the distinction we have made there of the three considerable rays.

[Geometric Square and Shadow Types]
Regarding the Geometric square, Jacquinot, speaking throughout this geometric treatise of the right shadow and the inverse shadow, was content to have simply said at the beginning which side of the square is called the right shadow, and which the inverse shadow, without giving the reason. To better explain the matter, we say that of the two sides of the geometric square divided into equal parts, one falling perpendicular to the horizon is called the scale of the inverse or reverted shadow, and the other parallel to the horizon is called the scale of the right shadow, for which the common reason given is that the right shadow is that which proceeds from a body, whether it be a pillar or other thing elevated straight above the horizon: and the inverse shadow is that which proceeds from a body, whether it be a pillar or other thing inverted and lying parallel to the horizon: but it seems to us that a better reason can be given for these two appellations of shadows; moreover, often the shadow of a body perpendicular to the horizon is projected in the form of an inverse shadow on a plane also perpendicular; and conversely, that of a body lying on the horizon is projected and received on a horizontal plane in the form of a right shadow: and therefore we say that this diverse appellation of shadow does not depend so much on the opaque body and the plane on which its shadow is projected, as on the luminous body and its ray; so that the ray being direct, the shadow will be called direct, and the ray being inclined, inverted, and bent, the shadow will be called inverse or reverted. Now, as the light, which we assume to be from the Sun, being perpendicular to a straight elevated body, the shadow does not appear, and is entirely straight like the body; and conversely, the light being at its greatest inclination, and as it were entirely inverted, its ray is entirely bent and inverted, and the shadow appears as infinite and entirely inverted; also, there is a midpoint between these two extremities where the light being constituted, its ray will be said to be moderately inclined, and the shadow also called a mean shadow. Such a ray being represented by the diagonal of the geometric square, it is vulgarly called the line of the mean shadow, which, when produced, divides the quadrant equally. Such a shadow is called a shadow of 45 degrees, equal in length to the height of its body, so that the light rising further, and its ray consequently straightening little by little until it becomes perpendicular, the shadow, from being mean, is made straighter, and consequently called right; or on the other side, the light inclining further, and inverting towards the horizon, its ray is more bent and inverted, and consequently the shadow is called inverted, notwithstanding that being received on a plane perpendicular to the horizon it shortens as the ray inclines or inverts further; for the change of plane is only for convenience and to retain within the capacity of some place or instrument, the shadows which otherwise, caused by much inclined and inverted rays, would produce and continue to infinity.

[Geometric Square and Shadow Analogy]
Another reason for this diverse appellation of shadow, drawn from the very practice of the geometric square, can still be noted here. This is that the light being elevated more than 45 degrees above the horizon, and the alidade always cutting, in this case, the lower part of the geometric square, the shadow of a perpendicular body is always analogous to the parts cut by the alidade, as the body is to the whole number of the altimeter scale, that is to say, to the side of the square that descends from the center of the Astrolabe. And this by reason of the conformity of the two triangles made, in this case, similar and similarly positioned. But the light being more inclined and inverted over the horizon than 45 degrees, and consequently the shadows being made longer than the height of their bodies, in this case, there is an inversion of the position of the small triangle on the geometric square, and consequently of the analogy: for the shadow, in this said case, is made analogous and proportional to the entire side of the square, that is to say, to the entire number of the scale, as the body is to the parts cut by the alidade. Above 45 degrees, by conformity and similar position of the two triangles, the direct analogy of the body to the entire side of the square, and of the shadow to the cut parts, is instituted; this shadow is called a right shadow: but below 45 degrees, because of the inversion that occurs in the position of the small triangle, and of the analogy and proportion of the body to the cut parts and of the shadow to the entire side, this shadow is called inverse or reverted.

[Measuring Heights at 45 Degrees]
II. PROPOSITION. To know the height. This proposition is that when the Sun is not at 45 degrees, or at other times, whether morning or other, you wish to measure heights, you must place the back of the rule on the elevation of 45 degrees, or on the 11th point of the scale, between the right and inverse shadow; then, holding your Astrolabe justly suspended by its ring with your hand, you will approach or move away from the thing you are measuring, until through the sight of the rule you see its summit. Having done this, you will see that the space between you and its base is equal to its height, adding that of your eye, because it is not on the surface of the earth.

[Geometric Square: Visual Ray and Shadow]
It is true that this proposition is more universal and more reliable in operation than the preceding one, because it can be practiced at all times, in all places, and at all hours; and besides, it is not subject to the inconveniences noted in the remark on the preceding one, supposing always that the Astrolabe is always well rectified, and the place chosen for such observation is perfectly level and parallel to the horizon. And in this second method of measurement, the visual ray passing through the two pinnules of the Alidade, and terminating from one end at the eye of the measurer, and from the other at the extremity of the height to be measured, represents the sun's ray in the preceding proposition: and the distance from the measurer to the thing to be measured takes the place of the shadow, shortened nevertheless in this proposition by the height of the measurer. Furthermore, the Astrolabe or other instrument applied in this observation does not vary in any way; it remains in the same state for one and the other of these two propositions; or just as the fiducial line is midway between two equal sides of the geometric square, so the visual ray is midway between two equal quantities, one of which is the distance from the eye of the measurer, and the other the height of the thing to be measured above the measurer.

[Eye Height Correction in Measurement]
What Jacquinot says here, that one must add the height of the eye, because it is not on the surface of the earth, is also true and necessary in almost all the following propositions, where mention will be made of the measurement of some height, which can be easily understood by the figure above, where it is seen that the eye of the measurer prevents the diagonal ray between the height and the distance from being prolonged to the surface of the earth, on which the measurement is made and taken, and not in the air the height of the eye: also, instead of forming a triangle similar and proportional to that which is made on the Astrolabe by the fiducial line, with two sides of the geometric square, only a part is formed which represents a trapezoid. But inasmuch as the distance from the eye of the measurer to the thing to be measured is or should always be equal to the distance of his feet (supposing that the measurer must always stand upright, making the observation), if one imagines a straight line drawn from the eye of the measurer to the thing to be measured which is parallel to the surface chosen for the distance, that line will form with the visual ray, and a part of the height to be measured, a triangle similar to that which is formed on the geometric square of the Astrolabe, of which all the sides will be known in similar and equal measures to that by which the distance will have been measured; just as all the sides of the small triangle formed on the Astrolabe are known in measures of the degrees of the square or altimeter scale. And then, since the line of the eye's distance is parallel to that of the feet's distance, and since the eye's height and the height to be measured are perpendicular to the horizon, and therefore parallel to each other, the line of the eye's distance will cut off from the thing to be measured a height equal to that of the eye, and therefore known, since that of the eye is always supposed known; which, added to the rest of the said height comprised within the said triangle, and known by observation, will give the entire height known in measures of the distance and of the eye's height. Note: We have said here, supposing that the measurer always stands upright during the observation. That is to say, that the height of his eye is perpendicular to the ground of the distance of his feet.

[Precision in Measurement]
Inasmuch as it is impossible for the measurement to be taken accurately if this precaution is not observed. Therefore, to avoid this inconvenience, two things are absolutely necessary: either that the distance is always taken from the point where the perpendicular line from the eye falls on the surface where the distance is measured; or (and this is the most reliable) that the height of the eye is always taken at the center of the Astrolabe or geometric square, and the distance from the point where the perpendicular from the said center falls on the same surface: for in this case, the measurer can take convenience for the observation, and it will not matter where his eye is, provided that it is on the visual line of the Alidade, that is to say, the one which, passing through the two apertures of the pinnules, will meet and join the extremity of the height to be measured.

[Measuring Height with Two Rods]
III. PROPOSITION. To find the height of a tower without an Astrolabe, using two rules or rods. Although this proposition and some others following it are not for the use of the Astrolabe, we have added them here to measure heights, and to use them in default of the said instrument. You will therefore take two rules, one half the size of the other, and you will raise them on a flat place, placing the small one as far from the other as its own size, which can be measured by a third rule that will join them at right angles: then you will look at the tower through the top of the said rules, approaching or moving away until you see its summit: and the distance between the said small rule and the foot of the tower will be the measure of its height, by only adding the quantity of the said small rule.

[Notes on Measuring with Two Rods]
This proposition and the following one, added here by Jacquinot outside the use of the Astrolabe, can serve just as well for measuring distances in length as for heights: and for the practice of this one, it will first be noted that the two rules or rods must be raised perfectly straight and perpendicular on a very even place and parallel to the horizon: but as for their length, it is not absolutely necessary that one be half the other; this subjection would be more troublesome than useful, as David Robert's annotations on this passage, where he took great pains to make us understand the design and arrangement of Jacquinot's trivial, common, and easy figure, instead of giving us any reason for it, or noting the defect found in Jacquinot's text, which we will show needs to be reformed and clarified for those who are newly initiated or little practiced and understood in this practice.

[Rod Lengths for Measurement]
We say then, first, concerning the lengths, that it does not matter whether one of the rules is more or less than double the length of the other: inasmuch as it will rarely happen that two rules or rods are found such that one is double the other, that they can be erected and fixed on a plane without some aid or support from elsewhere; for in this case, to take their distance from the length of the small one as Jacquinot and Robert wish, in which case two rules, one less or more than double the length of the other, could also serve promptly and easily, there being no difference except in their distance, which would be taken equal to the excess of the larger over the smaller.

[Measuring Height with Two Unequal Rods]
Therefore, whenever one wishes to practice this proposition, it will suffice that the two rods are simply unequal, one being larger than the other. And whether one wishes to fix them in the ground or simply erect and lean them against a plane, care must be taken that the smaller one is fixed and distanced from the larger one such that their distance is equal to the excess of the larger over the smaller. For in this case, if the eye is placed on the line connecting the extremities of the said two rods, and one sees the summit of some elevated body, such as a pyramid, tower, or other thing, one can say that the distance between the small rod or staff and the tower will be equal to the height of the tower. But if one does not precisely perceive the summit of the said tower at the first attempt, it will be useless to do what Jacquinot teaches, which is to approach or move away until one can see it through the extremities of the said rods, unless at the same time one approaches or moves away the two elevated and erected rods as said, without changing anything of their distance or constitution. For it is the line connecting the extremities of the two rods that must be directed to the summit of the tower. This being a lengthy process if the two rods are not attached together or elevated and erected on a movable plane: to avoid this inconvenience, we will give another method to practice this proposition, which will be general, quicker, and easier.

[Alternative Method for Height Measurement]
Let two staffs (rods or staves) be taken, one longer than the other. And having fixed and erected the longer one perpendicularly on a very even piece of ground, let the smaller one also be erected and fixed at whatever distance one wishes, so that by the extremities of the two staffs one can perceive the extremity of the height to be measured, such as the summit of a tower or other thing. Then the distance between the tower and the smaller staff will have such a ratio and proportion to the height of the tower above the said smaller staff, as the distance between the two staffs has to the excess of the longer over the smaller. For example, if the said distance between the two staffs is found to be double, triple, half, or a quarter of the said excess, the measured distance between the tower and the smaller staff will be double, triple, half, or a quarter of the height of the said tower above the said smaller staff. And when the said distance between the staffs is found to be equal to the excess, the distance from the tower to the smaller staff will be equal to the height of the tower above the said smaller staff. Therefore, by always adding the height of the said smaller staff, one will have the entire height of the tower measured quite precisely and promptly.

[Measuring Length with Two Rods]
NOTE. As in the figure above, the eye being applied on the side of the smaller staff, the visual ray extends upwards to measure some height. Similarly, the eye applied on the side of the longer staff, the visual ray will extend downwards, terminating a length on the horizontal surface. Thus, when one wishes to measure some length and distance, the two staffs being fixed at whatever distance one wishes, the longer one will be advanced or withdrawn from the ground, so that through the two extremities of the two staffs the eye can precisely see the target of the length or distance to be measured. Then, whatever proportion the excess of the longer staff has to the distance or separation of the two, such will be the entire height of the said longer staff to its distance from the proposed target, that is to say, to the distance and length to be measured. For example, if the said excess is only a third, a quarter, a sixth, or a twelfth of the separation of the two staffs, the height of the longer one will be a third, a quarter, a sixth, or a twelfth of the length to be measured.

[Jacquinot's Method with a Single Rod]
NOTE II. To aid and make easier the method given by Jacquinot for practicing this proposition, we will also say that a single staff will suffice, of whatever length one desires. Having pierced it near one end, so that a small rod, as long as the part of the larger one from the hole to its extremity, can be passed through the hole or at least fixed at right angles. For to measure some height, one will approach or move back the said staff, fixing it upright in the ground, until the eye can perceive the summit of the thing to be measured through the two extremities. Then the distance between it and the staff will be equal to its height above the said staff. Therefore, by adding the height of the said staff, one will have the entire height of the proposed thing to be measured. This can also be practiced for measuring a length.

[Measuring Height with a Mirror]
III. PROPOSITION. Otherwise with a mirror. Place your mirror on the ground, straight and level, as far from your feet as the height of your eyes. And if it pleases you, attach it to the end of a rod, then look diligently into the said mirror, approaching or moving away (always maintaining the distance) until you see the summit of the tower in the mirror. After that, measure the space between the mirror and the base of the tower, and thus you will have its height justly, without adding anything to it.

[Mirror Method: Proportionality]
Generally, when looking at a tower in a mirror, the proportion that the space between your foot and the mirror bears to the height of the eye, will be the same proportion that the distance between the mirror and the base of the tower bears to its height. The same things are to be observed here as in the preceding method: namely, that this approaching or moving away of the thing to be measured must be understood as applying to both the mirror and the measurer together, as if the said mirror were attached to some staff or rod, so that the measurer, by approaching, can make the said mirror approach or recede without changing the equality of the distance, that is to say, always observing that the distance between the measurer's feet and the mirror is equal to the height of his eye. And this is to find a distance from the mirror equal to the height of the thing to be measured, assuming that the mirror is very flat and even, without waves, bumps, or cavities, and is laid on a very even plane parallel to the horizon. For with a convex mirror, there would be a great difference, if it were not handled by a measurer as expert in the knowledge of mirrors as in the art of measuring. We note this so that Jacquinot's figure, which represents a convex mirror, does not mislead those less versed in these exercises, making them believe that it is indifferent whether one uses a flat or a convex mirror; as also to warn that absolutely all wavy and uneven mirrors are unsuitable for such exercises.

[Mirror Method: Point of Observation]
NOTE. It must be noted here that according to the size of the mirror, there can be a difference in the measurement. Therefore, to avoid this inconvenience, one must always observe that the same point of the mirror taken for the distance, either of the thing to be measured or of the measurer, is the same point through which one sees, that is to say, the one that reflects the extremity of the thing to be measured to the eye. And therefore, for more certainty, one should willingly use a marked point in the middle of the mirror.

[Measuring Horizontal Distances with a Mirror]
We have said that this proposition can serve to measure certain horizontal lengths, because if one attaches a flat mirror to a staff fixed upright in the ground, such that the plane of the mirror is perpendicular to the plane of the horizon, and one looks at some target for a proposed distance or length, the eye being as elevated above the point of the mirror that reflects the said target (that is to say, above the horizontal line that passes through and traverses the mirror at the point of reflection) as it is distant from the plane of the mirror: the height of the said point on the mirror above the plane of the horizon will be equal to the distance of the said target marked on the same horizontal plane. Likewise, there will always be the same proportion of the height of the eye above the said point of reflection to its distance from the mirror, as there is of the height of the said point on the horizon to the distance the mirror has from the target marked as the end of some proposed distance and length.

[Practical Use of the Flat Mirror]
We have also deemed it worthy of remark here, concerning the subject and in favor of the flat mirror, that most, indeed all, of the propositions in this present treatise can be very easily, promptly, and accurately practiced with it: provided that it is handled with extreme care, which we can demonstrate in the method of measuring, with a single placement of the measurer and a single position of the mirror, the distance and height of some tower surrounded by water or otherwise inaccessible. To this tower, at whatever distance it may be, having opposed a flat mirror attached to a staff, such that its plane is plumb and perpendicular to the horizon, the measurer seeing the foot of the said tower, by the preceding Note, will know the distance, always proportional to the height of the mirror, as the height of his eye above the point of reflection is to its distance from the mirror. Then, the gaze being lowered a little until the measurer perceives the summit of the tower, whatever proportion he finds of his distance from the mirror to the lowering of his eye below the horizontal line of the point of reflection, such will he judge and know to be the distance he found between the mirror and the tower, to the height of the tower above the same point of reflection; therefore, by adding the height of the said point of reflection, he will have the exact total height of the tower, and this from a single station and a single position of the mirror. This we do not find to have been remarked by anyone yet, not even by Father Clavius in his compendium of Altilongimetric methods, although the thing is easy to practice and of quite notable effect. See the following figure.

[Finding Heights with Shadows]
To know daily the height of bodies elevated above the horizon, place yourself in a flat area, and on a table, stool, or other object, fix a rod very straight. Then wait until the shadow is as long as the body of the said rod. Then mark the end of the shadow you measure, and the object to be measured will have as much height as there are measures between its foot and the point marked at the extremity of its shadow.

[Accurate Shadow Measurement]
The surest and quickest practice of this is to draw a circle on a flat plane, level and parallel to the horizon, and at the center, perpendicularly erect a gnomon, staff, or rod of the length of the semidiameter of the circle, that is to say, of the compass opening that traced the circle: for when the shadow of the said gnomon terminates at the said circle, it will infallibly be as long as the gnomon is high. And therefore, marking at the same time a point at the extremity of the shadow of that whose height one wishes to know, the measure of the said shadow will give the height of its body. If, dividing the height of the gnomon or rod into several equal parts, one traces several circles both inside and outside the aforesaid, distant from each other by the quantity of one of the said parts, by the known proportion of their semidiameters to the height of the gnomon, when the sun's shadow terminates in any of these circles, one will have at the same time the proportion of all shadows to the height of their bodies.

[Heights When Sun is Below 45 Degrees]
For this, it must be noted that whenever the height of the Sun is taken and it is found to be less than 45 degrees, the lower part of the rule will always fall on the inverse scale: and then the shadows of bodies perpendicularly elevated above the horizon are longer than their height, in such a proportion that 12 exceeds the number of points touched by the rule.

[Example: Shadow Length Calculation]
EXAMPLE. If the rule falls on the sixth point of the inverse scale, the shadow is double the height of its body, just as 12 is double 6. If on the fourth, which is the third part of 12, the height will be only the third part of the shadow. And thus one must judge other proportions.

[Geometric Quadrant Divisions]
This agrees with the ancient and common division of the geometric quadrant or altimeter scale into 12 equal parts in small astrolabes and quadrants, or into 60 or another multiple of 12 in larger and more spacious ones: which, in truth, seemed convenient and prompt, both because of the multitude of its aliquot parts, which are encountered quite often in practice and shorten a division, as when only 1/2, 1/3, 1/4, or 1/6 of a number is needed; and because of the ordinary division of our common measures, such as the toise into 6 feet, the foot into 12 inches, and the inch into 12 lines; so that after the analogy is made, the quotient is always known in these measures without new evaluation.

[Modern Astrolabe Scale Divisions]
But nevertheless, our small astrolabes are found to be divided differently in their quadrants or altimeter scales, namely into 10 or 100 equal parts, or another number, multiple of 10, according to the capacity of the instrument. And although this division, considering the reasons above, seems less prompt and easy than the preceding one: it is recognized in practice to be infinitely easier and more convenient, first, because of the two absolutely necessary operations in analogy, namely multiplication and division, it always saves one, in whatever way the said analogy is established; and therefore it gains almost half the time. Secondly, because the division by 10 is found to be easier to subdivide, to take fractions and smaller parts more precisely, and to subject them to a just calculation. Thirdly, because one can be helped in large subdivisions by tables and numbers of tangents or sines; by means of which one can have, with the number of parts of the scale, the number of degrees and minutes of the circle corresponding to it, and conversely, by the known height in degrees and minutes, estimate the parts of the scale more precisely.

[Example: Altimetric Observation]
As for example, let the alidade in some altimetric observation be found on...

[Shadow Scale Calculation Example]
the 45th degree of the umbra versa on the square of the astrolabe divided into 100 parts, or on the 45th degree, the division being only 10. And let the distance of some elevated body directly above the horizon be measured. If the said degree comes from the observation of its height, or else the length of the shadow, if the observation is made towards the sun, let the said distance or the said shadow be found to be 35 fathoms. Then, because as the entire side of the square is to the said parts of the umbra versa, so the length of the shadow or measured distance is to the height of the body: the analogy will thus be instituted as 100 is to 45, so 35 fathoms of shadow or distance will be to 15 and 3/4 fathoms of height. Now, in the operation of this analogy, it is seen that there is no need to perform any division, but only to truncate the last two figures of the number to be divided, to serve as the numerator in the fraction whose denominator is always the same number of 100, the divisor, and the number remaining after the said truncation is the number of whole fathoms. For multiplying 35 fathoms by 45 degrees of shadow makes 1575, which divided by 100, by truncating as many of the last figures as there are zeros in the divisor number 100, gives 15 whole fathoms, and the truncated figures placed above the line with the number 100 as the divisor below, give a fraction of 75/100; that is to say, 75 parts of a fathom which would be 100: or else by reduction to smaller terms, 3/4 of a fathom. Or if this observation was made with a square divided into 12 or 60 parts, the alidade would be found on the 27th degree of the umbra versa divided into 60, or on the 5 and 1/4 degree, so that in the operation it would be necessary not only to multiply 35 fathoms of shadow or measured distance by 27 or 5 and 1/4, but also to divide their product, namely 945 or 189, by 60 or by 12 to obtain 15 fathoms and 3/4 or 1/4, that is to say 3/4 of a fathom.

[Sun's Height and Tangent Tables]
And furthermore, if from the said observation one desires to know the height of the sun in degrees, or else the angle of the measured height: besides the sufficiently just and precise ratio found on our astrolabes of the degrees of the square or geometric scale with the degrees of the circle, we also have the same thing by the tables of tangents, taking only the first figures, and leaving as many of the last as the division of the square or scale of those tangents will be smaller. Thus, opposite 45, I find 24 degrees and about 14 minutes: and such was the height of the sun or the angle of the measured height. As, on the contrary, if in the observation one can estimate more precisely the degrees of the circle cut by the alidade than those of the square, the said tables will give us precisely the parts of the square or scale corresponding to the degrees of the circle. And by this means, a more accurate calculation and a more precise measurement will be made.

[Height Calculation Above 45 Degrees]
Regarding the Geometric Square. 297. PROPOSITION. To know the said heights when the sun is elevated more than 45 degrees. It is convenient to practice as we have said by the preceding Proposition, except that it must be understood that the heights of bodies exceed the length of the umbra recta, just as the shadow exceeded the bodies by the aforesaid proposition, when the rule touched the points of the umbra versa scale: and one must only consider the proportion of the points that the rule touches to 12.

[Umbra Recta Example]
EXAMPLE. If the rule falls on the sixth point of the umbra recta scale, this will demonstrate that the shadow of the body is half its height, because 6 is half of 12. And thus one must judge the others.

[Simplified Calculation for Umbra Recta]
In the operation and practice of this proposition on our small astrolabes, there is no need for a tedious multiplication, because as the parts of the umbra recta touched by the alidade are to the entire side of the square; so the shadows are to their bodies, or the distance to the heights: always assuming the bodies are perpendicular, and the planes on which the shadows and distances are measured are united and parallel to the horizon. Therefore, multiplying the entire side of the scale by the number of measures of the shadow or distance, and dividing the product by the number of parts touched by the alidade on the side of the umbra recta, the quotient, or fourth proportional term, will give the required height. Now, this multiplication, necessary in the division of the scale by 12 or 60, is saved in that by 10, 100, or 1000, there being only a need to add to the number to be multiplied 1, 2, 3, or as many ciphers and zeros as there are in the whole number of the division of the scale.

[Umbra Recta Calculation Example]
Regarding the Geometric Square. 299. EXAMPLE. Let it be that in the observation the alidade touches the 45th degree of the umbra recta and the shadow or the distance is measured at 35 fathoms. Therefore, one will say as 45 parts of umbra recta are to 100, the entire side of the square: so 35 fathoms of shadow or measured distance will be to 77 and 7/9 or 7/9 of a fathom of height. Now the multiplication of 35 by 100 is done by simply adding two ciphers, 3500, which divided by 45, give as quotient the said 77 and 7/9: and thus the operation is seen to be shortened by almost half.

[Finding Height by Visual Line]
300. Use and Utility of the Astrolabe. VIII. PROPOSITION. To find the height by the visual line. To find in general the height of an elevated body perpendicularly above the horizon by the visual line, it must be noted that when looking at the summit of a tower or other object, the rule will fall between the two scales, or on one of them. Therefore, if it happens that it falls between the two, the space will be equal to the height of the tower, by adding the distance from your eye to the ground, as demonstrated above. But if the said rule cut the umbra recta scale, the space with the height of the eye will be as much less than the size of the tower, as the points touched by the rule will be less than 12. On the contrary, if it touches the umbra versa scale, the said space will be, with the height of the eye, as much greater than the size of the tower, as 12 points will exceed the points of the said scale where the movable rule is found.

[Visual Line Height Example]
Regarding the Geometric Square. 301. EXAMPLE. If, by looking through the sights of the rule at the top and summit of a tower, the rule cuts the sixth point of the umbra recta, I will say that the space between my feet and the tower contains half the height that this tower has above my eye. Therefore, by measuring this distance, and taking it twice, you will have the universal height of that tower, by adding the distance from my eye to the ground. Similarly, one must judge the points of the umbra versa scale.

[Editor's Note on Text Correction]
Use and Utility of the Astrolabe. This passage needed to be re-established by Robert and others, but they passed over it without recognizing the defect, although the passage is one of the most important in this whole little treatise. Robert having made the first error here, he was followed by Jacquinot, as in many other places that we have noted in the treatise on the Astrolabe; in which appears the difference between what is given to us by proper and particular science, experience, and demonstration.

[Critique of Previous Altimetry Methods]
demonstration, and what we have simply reported by imitation of others. We say then that it is absurd, in the case where the rule cuts the straight scale, that the space, that is to say the distance with the height of the eye, is proportional to the height of the tower, just as the points of the scale touched by the said rule are to the whole number of the scale: in such a way that just as the said points are below 100, so also the said space with the height of the eye is as much less than the height of the tower: for the height of the eye can only in a case of mean shadow, or rather should never enter or be comprised in the proportion of the distance to the height; but it is a quantity always known, or supposed as such, which must be added to the quotient of the height found by analogy, to compose the entire height, which otherwise is only found above the point in the measured object corresponding to the height of the eye, or better, to the height of the center of the Astrolabe.

[Three Cases for Perpendicular Heights]
To re-establish therefore this proportion in suitable terms on our small Astrolabes: We say that to measure any perpendicular heights above the horizon, our visual ray (represented by the fiducial line of the alhidade) can be considered in three ways; Firstly, when the Alhidade meets the line of mean shadow, which Jacquinot calls between the two scales. Secondly, when it meets the scale on the side of the straight shadow. And thirdly on the scale or side of the versatile shadow. For the first case, it is true that the space with the height of the eye are equal to the entire height, and that the height of the eye in this case can be added to the distance, because the visual ray, being continued, will infallibly comprise on the ground a space from the point of the distance equal to the height of the eye, forming an isosceles right triangle: which would not happen in the two other following cases. For the second case, when the rule is found on the side of the straight shadow, the distance will be as much less than the height of the perpendicular body, such as a tower, pyramid, or other above the eye or the center of the Astrolabe, as the points touched by the rule are less than 100. For example, if the rule touches the 20th degree of straight shadow, the distance between the measurer, or the center of the Astrolabe and the proposed tower to be measured, will be only the fifth part of its height above the said center or eye of the measurer, as 20 degrees are only the fifth part of 100. Therefore, if the distance is measured as 6 fathoms, the height above the center of the Astrolabe will be 30 fathoms: to which adding the height of 5 feet or other measure, one will have the entire height of 30 fathoms 5 feet. But if in the observation the rule touched 10 degrees of versatile shadow, then the distance would be as much greater, that is to say five times greater than the said height, as 100 degrees or parts, the whole number

[Geometric Square: Versatile Shadow Calculation]
of the division of the scale, are five times more than the 10 degrees covered by the rule; and therefore at 5 fathoms distance, the height of a tower would be above the eye or center of the Astrolabe only 3 fathoms, and the entire height 3 fathoms 5 feet.

[Measuring Inaccessible Tower Height]
To find the height of a tower that cannot be approached.

[Method for Inaccessible Tower Height]
In such an occurrence, it is necessary to observe the summit of a tower one wishes to measure twice, by directing the rule each of the said times on the versatile scale and on some of its quotient points, such as 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6. Then one must note the points at each time and their quotient numbers. After this, subtract the smaller from the larger, and the difference will remain, by which one must divide the space between the two places where such inspections were made; and the quotient number of this division with the height of the eye will show the height of the tower.

[Example: Inaccessible Tower Height]
Let us suppose that I see the top of a tower by the rule being at the sixth point of versatile shadow, and I mark the place where I saw the top of the said tower in such a situation of the rule. I retain the quotient number of the points that touch the rule, namely 2. This done, I place the rule on the fourth point, and move away from this tower in a straight line until I can see the top again in such a situation, noting likewise the place where this will happen, and also looking at the quotient of four points, which I find to be 3. Afterwards I subtract the first quotient, inasmuch as it is smaller, and 1 remains, by which dividing the space between the two marked places which I find to be 10 paces, to which I add the height of my eye which is 5 feet, and from the whole results 11 paces which is the height of that tower.

[Special Case: Quotient Difference of 1]
And it must be understood that whenever the difference of the quotient numbers is 1, then the distance between the two marks plus the height of the eye comprises the height of the tower, which happens when one of the sightings is made at the 5th point of the versatile scale, and the other at the 6th, or when one is made at the 6th and the other at the 4th. Also when one of the inspections is made at the 4th point, and the other at the 5th.

[Critique of Jacquinot's Altimetry]
As this proposition is the noblest, most general, and seemingly most difficult of all those in this altimetric treatise: so it seems to us that it was rather poorly understood by Jacquinot, or at least his discourse would give more to guess for the less skilled, than it would instruct them if it were not re-established, so obscure, confused, and ill-suited to the nature of the proposed matter it is. This is what moved the authors of the second impression to entirely cut out all of Jacquinot's discourse, and substitute another which they cobbled together from Stoeffler as best they could, to disguise their borrowing; and in truth, they disguised it so much that in the example they took from the said Stoeffler, they only propose to us one and the same observation encounter in two different places. But furthermore, David Robert himself, who by his third impression should have re-established and clarified the whole, was not content to follow and report the same impertinences: for besides this, by his annotations on this passage, composed of a jargon entirely boorish and ill-reasoned, it seems that he deliberately wanted to confuse the mind of the curious learner, to divert him from the path of intelligence, and prevent him from understanding it any more than he did. Such were previously the annotations and demonstrations of De Mesmes on Stoeffler in this place, who instead of supplying what was omitted by Stoeffler, got entangled himself in methods of practice and demonstrations that he did not understand, or else there was a great lack in all these books on the part of the Printers.

[Facilitating Geometric Square Understanding]
We will therefore consider, to further facilitate the understanding and practice of this proposition regarding the Geometric Square,

[Three Principal Cases for Measurement]
position to the least advanced, separately all the encounters that can be made in practice, according to how much more or less one can approach the proposed thing to measure, and we will reduce everything into three principal encounters: 1. If it happens that the two observations are made within a space or distance from the object that is less than its proposed height to be measured, the rule or alidade will be found in both observations stopped on the parts of the shadow direct. 2. If the two observations are made outside the distance of the object equal to its height, the rule in this case will always be found in both on the side of the shadow inverse. 3. If one of the two observations is made within the said distance, and the other outside, it will happen that the rule in this one will be found on the parts of the shadow inverse, and in the other on the parts of the shadow direct. As it can also happen that one of the observations is made at an equal distance to the height: Which happens when in the said observation, the rule is found stopped on the line of the mean shadow, in this case if the other observation falls on the shadow direct, they will both be considered on the shadow direct, and will follow the laws of the first encounter; but if the other falls on the shadow inverse, the two can be considered differently, either one on the shadow inverse, and the other on the shadow direct, and therefore under the laws of the 3rd encounter; or both on the shadow inverse and under the laws of the 2nd encounter.

[First Encounter & First Rule]
When the two observations are made within a distance less than the height to be measured, and therefore the rule in both falls on the side of the shadow direct, to obtain the exact height of the object, one will proceed in two principal, general, and easy ways. Let each number of parts cut by the rule in one and the other observation be divided by the entire side of the square, that is to say by the whole number of the division of the scale, and having taken the difference of the two quotients, by subtracting the smaller from the larger, let the measure found for the distance and space between the two observations be divided by that difference, and the quotient of this last division will be the exact height of the object above the center of the Astrolabe, which we will clarify more particularly with an example. EXAMPLE. Let a pyramid be proposed, not far off, yet separated by some wall, river, or ditch, or otherwise inaccessible, whose point is visible and remarkable, of which one wishes to know the height above some chosen plane to make the measurement. Having raised the Astrolabe or geometric square to some height, and turned the rule so that the measurer can, through the sights of the pinnules, see the point or summit of the said pyramid, one must note the number of parts that the rule covers on the side of the shadow direct: as well as the point on the plane corresponding to the center of the said square, and let this number be 5 of the shadow direct. Then, having moved back in a straight line to some distance on the same plane, let the Astrolabe or square be raised again to the same height as the first observation, and let the point of the said pyramid be observed by the measurer through the pinnules of the rule, noting again the number of parts cut by the said rule, which are found to be 9 of the shadow direct. This done, and having marked the place of this last observation, one must divide both the 5 of the first observation and the 9 of the second by the whole number 10 of the square to obtain 1/2 and 9/10 as quotients: whose difference is 4/10, by which one must divide the distance between the two observations: let it be measured as 20 fathoms; therefore, dividing 8 by 4/10, one will have 20 fathoms for the exact height of the said pyramid above the center of the Astrolabe or square.

[Second Method for First Encounter]
The second way to practice this first encounter will always be to take the difference of the two numbers of the parts of the shadow direct cut by the rule in the two observations, and say, as this difference is to the entire side of the square, so let the distance between the two observations be to another: The fourth proportional term will always be the exact height of the object to be measured above the center of the Astrolabe or square. As in the proposed example, the two numbers of shadow direct being 5 and 9, their difference is 4. Therefore, if one says as 4 parts of difference of shadow direct are to 10, the whole number of the scale, so let 20 fathoms of distance between the two observations be to the required height: the fourth proportional term being 20 fathoms, will be, as above, the exact height of the said pyramid above the center of the Astrolabe.

[Second Encounter & Second Rule]
When the two observations are each made at a distance further from the object to be measured than its height, and therefore the rule in both observations falls on the side of the shadow inverse. One will again proceed in these two ways. Let the whole number of the scale be divided by each number of the parts of the shadow inverse cut by the rule, and let the difference of the quotients proceeding from each division be taken, subtracting the smaller from the larger: and let the distance found between the two observations be divided by this difference: the quotient proceeding from this last division will be the exact required height.

[Example of Second Encounter]
EXAMPLE. Let for example be proposed the point of a bell tower, from the foot of which one cannot approach, and of which one wishes to know the height. And in the first observation of it, through the sights of the pinnules, the rule is found on 5 points of the shadow inverse: then moving back in a straight line, let the said point be observed again, and the rule found on 2 points of the same shadow. This done, and having measured the distance between the two observations as 15 fathoms, let the whole number 10 of the square or scale be divided by each of the said numbers 5 and 2 of the shadow inverse, and from the two quotients 2 and 5, let the difference 3 be taken; by which, having divided the measured distance between the two observations, namely 15 fathoms, one will have for quotient 5 fathoms, and that will be the exact height of the said point of the bell tower above the center of the Astrolabe.

[Converting Inverse to Direct Shadow]
The other way will be to convert the points of inverse shadow cut by the rule into points of direct shadow, and with these to do the same thing that we said in the second way for the first encounter; For this way differs in nothing from that one, except in the conversion and mutation of shadow where this one must begin. Now this conversion of points of inverse shadow into points of direct shadow is done in two ways: by one, dividing the square of the whole number of the scale by each of the points found of inverse shadow, the quotients will be points of direct shadow, which will have the same proportion to the entire side of the square, as the same entire side of the square to the points cut by the rule. Therefore, making it so that as each of the points of inverse shadow cut by the rule in one and the other observation is to the entire side of the square, that is to say, to the whole number of the scale: so let the same whole number be to another: one will have two third terms in continual proportion which will be points of direct shadow, with the difference of which one will proceed as stated above. By the other way, dividing the entire side of the square by the same points found of inverse shadow, then multiplying the quotients by the same entire side of the square, the products will be the same required points of direct shadow, whose difference will be proportional to the distance between the two observations, as the entire side of the square is to the proposed height to be measured, always to be taken above the center of the Astrolabe.

[Example: Steeple Height Calculation]
As in the example proposed above, concerning a steeple, in the two observations of which the rule was found at 5 and 2 points of umbra versa, making as 1 is to 10, so the same 10 to another: and as 5 is to 10, so the same 10 to another: That is to say, dividing 100, the square or product of the multiplication of 10 by itself, by 5 and by 2, one will have 20 and 50, which will be points of umbra recta in the same proportion to 10, the entire side of the square, as the same 10 to 5 and 2. These points will also be obtained by dividing 10 by 2 and by 5, and multiplying the quotients 5 and 2 by the same 10. Thus, taking 20 from 50 to find their difference 30, as the said 30 of difference of umbra recta are to 10, the whole number of the scale: so the 15 toises of distance between the two observations are to 5 toises of the exact height of the said steeple above the Astrolabe or square.

[Third Case and Rule]
When one of the observations is at a lesser distance than the height, and the other at a greater: And consequently, the rule cuts the scale of umbra recta in one, and the scale of umbra versa in the other. One will proceed in these two ways.

[Method for Third Case]
Let the number of parts of the umbra recta cut by the rule in the first observation be divided by the side or whole number of the square, and by the number of parts of the umbra versa cut in the second observation, let the said side or whole number of the square also be divided, and by the difference of the two quotients of the said divisions, let the number of measures found between the two observations be divided: for the new quotient resulting from this new division will be the exact height of the thing to be measured above the center of the Astrolabe.

[Example: Castle Wall Height]
Let it be proposed to measure and know how high and elevated a walled Castle is above the plain or countryside that surrounds it. The Astrolabe being elevated to its ordinary height (for we suppose that one has a suitable staff of a known length, to which the Astrolabe is attached to always know its height, and this for more security and promptness, although it is not absolutely necessary) (seeing through the pinhole sights the top of the wall of the said Castle, the rule cuts 9 parts of the umbra recta: then having marked the place, and moved back in a straight line, seeing again the same point of the top of the wall, the rule cuts 8 parts of the umbra versa: after having measured the space between the two observations, found perhaps to be 5 toises, if one divides 9 parts of umbra recta by 10, the whole side of the square, one will have 9/10 as quotient: and dividing the said whole side 10 by 8 of umbra versa, one will have 10/8: then subtracting one from the other, namely 9/10 from 10/8, one will have 14/40 as difference, by which if one divides 5, the number of toises found between the two observations, one will have 14 toises and 2/7 of a toise for the exact height of the said wall above the Astrolabe. If the Astrolabe is elevated by 5 feet, one will have for the entire height or elevation of the said wall above the plain 15 toises 8 inches and very nearly 7 lines.

[Second Method for Castle Height]
This same measurement will also be found by this second method, which will be to convert only the points of the umbra versa cut by the rule in the second observation into points of umbra recta, and by the difference of these to those of the first observation, divide the product of the multiplication of the measured distance by the whole side of the square: for the quotient will be the same as above for the required height. As in the proposed example, the 8 points of umbra versa of the second observation being converted into 12 and 1/2 of umbra recta (which is done either by dividing 100, the square of the whole number of the scale, by 8 parts of umbra versa, or by dividing 10, the whole side of the square, by the said 8, and multiplying the product 1 and 1/4 by the same 10.) As 3 and 1/2 of difference between 9 and 12 and 1/2 points of umbra recta are to the 10 points of the whole scale: so the 5 toises of measured distance between the two observations will be to the same 14 toises and 2/7 of desired height for the elevation of the said wall above the Astrolabe.

[Generalizing Measurement Techniques]
These three kinds of principal cases in the practice of this 4th proportion being well examined and understood, it will be easy to reduce all others to them, as if in one observation the rule is found on the line of the mean shadow, and in the other on the umbra recta, these two will be considered as umbra recta by which, dividing the whole side of the square, and by the difference of their quotients the space between the observations, one will have for a new quotient the required height; and this by the first method. And by the second, as their difference will be to the whole side of the square. Thus the space between the observations will be the desired height. But if in the first observation the rule falls on the line of the mean shadow, and in the second on the umbra versa, the two observations will be considered either as in the 1st or as in the 3rd case, by dividing the two points touched by the rule by the whole side of the square, or by dividing only the points of umbra versa for the first method; and for the second, it will only be necessary to convert the points of umbra versa of the second observation into points of umbra recta, and do the rest as in the said 1st or 3rd cases.

[Two Ways to Use the Square]
There are two ways to use, in all this treatise on the Geometric Square described on the Astrolabe. One by which the measurer subjects the said Astrolabe to his convenience, and the other by which he subjects himself to his Astrolabe. By the first, the measurer makes the Astrolabe mobile, both for its situation and for the change and variation of its rule: by the other, the measurer having set the rule of his Astrolabe on some point of the square, he subjects himself to moving back or forward, raising or lowering until his eye meets and aligns with the fiducial line of the rule to be able to make his observation. And as the first method is more general and prompt for experts in these exercises, so the second is easier and more suitable for those who are less experienced, as it is derived and drawn from the clear knowledge and long experience of the first to accommodate the capacity of those curious to learn. Now Jacquinot confuses these two methods throughout this proposition: for at the beginning he seems to subject the Astrolabe, by saying that one must observe on which points of umbra versa the rule falls in the two observations; and then here in the example he supposes the rule to have been set and fixed on the 6, then on the 4 points of umbra versa, and that the measurer, by moving back or forward, had to search for the place to be able to observe in such a configuration of the rule to obtain a distance between his two observations equal to the height to be measured above his eye. Nevertheless, he seems to attach himself more particularly to the latter method, and gives at the end the means to be able to practice it, determining some points on which, the observations being made, one can have between them a distance equal to the height to be measured. But since according to the first method of practice, which we have clarified above concerning the cases of this proposition, there is always a similar proportion.

[Measuring Heights with Astrolabe Quotients]
portion of the difference of the quotients to Unity, as of the distance between the observations to the height to be measured above the center of the Astrolabe or square: We say that it is just as easy to give a distance double, triple, quadruple, etc., or half, or in another proportion to the height, as equal, by noting on the square the points whose difference of their quotient is 1, 5, 4, etc., or 4, 7, and others. As on our division by 10 in our small Astrolabes, if one observation is made on 1 point of umbra versa, and the other on 2, the distance will be five times greater than the height. One being on 4 and the other on 5 of the same umbra versa, the distance will be half. One on 1 and the other on 5, the distance will be triple: on 1 and on 5, the distance would be 5 times greater than the height to be measured, always taken above the center of the square.

[Notes for Less Experienced Practitioners]
NOTE. As we are not working here for experts more advanced in these exercises, but for the less experienced, and who need the path to be made easier for them, who need to be guided as by the hand to turn them away from bad passages, and to make them aware of what they should fear and avoid. Thus we deem it necessary to reveal to them here the difficulties that might stop them, and to make them aware of the precautions they should take in the practice of this proposition. For the difficulties concerning the Geometric Square, we advise them to first exercise themselves in the 4 parts of Arithmetic, both in whole and broken numbers, in order to know how to reduce to the same denomination, add, subtract, multiply, and divide all sorts of fractions that are often encountered in the practice of this proposition. Otherwise, it would be to give oneself a lot of trouble in vain, or at least for little profit. For the precautions (besides those we have noted above concerning the balance of the Astrolabe, the equality of the chosen plane for observation, and the same height or elevation of the Astrolabe, by means of the same proper and chosen staff for this effect), it is still necessary, to operate more justly, to note very precisely the places of the two observations in order to measure and have the distance more justly: which will be done easily enough with a plumb bob (stone, or other thing found as needed) hung on a string, which falling freely to the ground, when the string touches the center of the Astrolabe or the square, will give on the ground the true places of the observations. And to measure a proposed height more justly, care will be taken after the first observation to move back or forward to make the second, so that the two observations correspond in a straight line with the line that must be imagined to fall from the point noted at the summit of the proposed height on the same plane on which the measurer makes his observations: that is to say, in a word, that the two observations are made in the same plane perpendicular to the horizon, and passing through the same point noted at the summit of the thing to be measured in both observations. This will happen when, after having made the first observation, and having marked its place, one will stick a rod or staff there, from which one will move away by receding so that, from the place where one wants to make the second observation, seeing and looking at the same point noted in the proposed height, the said rod or staff marks and recognizes it precisely between the two. And this manner of receding will be more secure for the less experienced than advancing: which practice will make known enough.

[Distance of Inaccessible Heights]
ADDITION. Concerning the distance of the same inaccessible heights. It will perhaps not be found out of place to add here that there is a way in the practice of this 9th proposition to derive from the aforesaid two observations the distances of the same measured heights; and this by the second methods that we have reported on all encounters, by reduction as needed of umbra versa to umbra recta: to make known how useful and general the said method is. Concerning the Geometric Square, suppose then that one has made the two observations above to know some proposed height, and that by the second method, reducing everything to umbra recta, one has found the just measure of the said height, if one desires to know the distance between the first or last station and the thing to be measured: Generally, what proportion there will be of the difference of the two umbra recta to the smaller, or to the larger: such will always be that of the distance between the two observations to the distance from the closest or from the furthest of them. Therefore, whenever by two observations one desires to know the distance of some inaccessible thing, one must first always reduce, if necessary, the points of umbra versa of one or the other, or of both observations, if it so happens, to points of umbra recta: then having taken their difference; as this difference will be to the smaller umbra recta, or to the larger: so the distance measured between the two stations will be to the distance between the closest, or the furthest of them, and the proposed thing.

[Example: Castle Wall Measurement]
EXAMPLE. We will use here the example reported by us in the third encounter on the subject of the wall of a Castle surrounded by moats, where at the first observation the rule cut 5 parts of umbra recta, and at the second 8 parts of umbra versa.
