If then one asks how far each station, or each place of the two observations, is from the said wall: Having reduced the said 8 parts of umbra versa to 12 and 1/2 of umbra recta, and taken the difference of the said two numbers 9 and 12 and 1/2, namely 3 and 1/2, as the said 3 and 1/2 of difference are to 9 parts of umbra recta of the first and closest observation, or to 12 and 1/2 of the second and furthest, so the distance measured between the two observations of 5 toises, will be to the distance of the closest 11 toises and 1/2 of a toise, or to that of the second and furthest 17 toises and 1/2. But inasmuch as the distance of the second observation can be obtained by adding to that of the first the distance between the two observations, it will suffice to find by analogy that of the said first or closest, which will always be by multiplying the distance measured between the two observations by the points of the first, and dividing the product by the difference of the points of the one and the other.

[Inaccessible Heights from a Single Station]
ANOTHER ADDITION. Concerning the distances and heights of inaccessible things, whose base is not remarkable, and this by a single station. David Robert having in his Annotations on this 5th proposition made this question.

[Measuring Inaccessible Heights with One Station]
If, due to some inconvenience of the location or otherwise, one cannot make two observations in two different places, one might ask if there is a way to measure a proposed height that cannot be approached. It has been well answered that yes, but he has not given us much clarification, although, departing from all previous propositions, he has again called upon the following for his aid and succor. If he had done this, or if he had not formed the question, he would have relieved us of this trouble; for as it has not been our intention to report and pursue in this treatise all the methods that can be invented in the practice and handling of the geometric quadrant, of which we would, if necessary, swell a large volume; so our principal aim has been to clarify everything in this treatise that might cause difficulty or curiosity for the less advanced. So, Robert having formed the aforesaid question, we deem it appropriate to clarify it for anyone who might be curious to know its solution; which we will also give concerning distances, even in cases where the base of the object, such as a tower, wall, or other perpendicular body, is neither visible nor remarkable.

[Measuring Inaccessible Perpendicular Bodies]
We therefore say that there is a way with the Astrolabe or simple geometric quadrant, from a single place, to measure and know both the height of any inaccessible perpendicular body and its distance, even if its base is not remarkable. To achieve this more easily, the surveyor, if not yet very expert (for otherwise the thing is not absolutely necessary), should only provide himself with a stick of a certain length, to attach his Astrolabe or geometric quadrant in such a way that at two places on the said stick, the said Astrolabe or quadrant can be attached, sometimes higher, sometimes lower.

[Method for Two Observations]
Suppose then that on the said stick, the Astrolabe or quadrant can be raised to a height of 6 feet, and to another of 2 feet. The said stick being fixed in the ground, and the Astrolabe being elevated thereon by 6 feet, some remarkable point at the summit of the proposed object to be measured will be observed as before; and it will be marked on which points of the direct or inverse shadow the rule is stopped. Then, lowering the Astrolabe to a height of 2 feet, to have 4 feet of distance or difference between the two observations, the same point will again be observed through the pinnules, and it will be noted on which other points of the direct or inverse shadow the rule is found. This will always hold true, if necessary, to convert the points of direct shadow into points of inverse shadow (and this in the same manner as we have shown above, the conversion of inverse shadows into direct shadows being one and the same thing, but which must here be taken in reverse); then, by their difference, the product of the multiplication of the space between the two observations by the largest number of the inverse shadow will be divided, that is to say, by that of the lower observation; for the quotient of this division will be the exact height of the proposed object to be measured, to be taken above the lower observation.

[Example: Measuring a Gable and Wall]
For example, a house gable is presented, and a wall between the two; and one wishes to know the height of the said gable. The Astrolabe being elevated to 6 feet in height (by means of the aforesaid stick or other perpendicular stake on the plane of the horizon), if, by looking through the pinnules at the top of the said gable, the rule falls on 8 points of inverse shadow; and having lowered the Astrolabe to a height of 2 feet, if, by a second observation of the said gable, the rule falls on 8 points of the same shadow; as their difference, namely 4, is to 8 points of inverse shadow touched in the second, and lower observation: thus the 4 feet of difference in Astrolabe height between the two observations will be 34 feet above the center of the Astrolabe in the said second observation: which being in the said observation elevated by 2 feet, as said, one will have 36 feet or 6 fathoms for the entire height of the said gable above the plane on which the said observations were made.

[Calculating Distance with Inverse Shadows]
But to know the distance, one will practice the two methods that we have reported above for knowing heights by two stations, having no other difference except that here we will apply to inverse shadows what we said there about direct shadows, and to direct shadows what was said there about inverse shadows; and for the distance between the two stations, we will here consider the difference in the height of the Astrolabe in the two observations. Furthermore, the same rules will be practiced without changing anything, and in any encounters according to the different positions of its rule and observations, which encounters can also all be reduced to three main ones.

[Example: Distance to a Gable]
As in the proposed example of a house gable, whose base, due to a wall between the two, cannot be discovered or noticed: Since in the first observation of the top of the said gable, the rule was found on 8 points of inverse shadow, and in the second, 4 feet lower, on 8 and a half of the same shadow; By the first method, dividing the entire side of the square, namely the number 10, by each of the points of inverse shadow 8 and 8 and a half, one will have two quotients 10/8 and 10/8.5, whose difference is 1/17; by which dividing the 4 feet of difference between the two heights of the Astrolabe in the two observations, one will have 80 feet, or 13 fathoms 2 feet of distance or remoteness to the base of the said gable. And by the second method, the 8 and 8 and a half points of the two observations being on the inverse shadow, only their difference will be taken, namely 1/2. And as the said difference will be to the entire side of the square 10: thus the 4 feet of difference in the height of the Astrolabe will be to the said 80 feet, or 13 fathoms 2 feet of remoteness as above; that is to say, multiplying the 4 feet of difference in the height of the Astrolabe by the 10 of the entire scale, and dividing the product 40 by 1/2, the difference between the points of inverse shadow, one will have a quotient or fourth proportional term of 80 feet, which will be the exact remoteness of the base of the said gable.

[Height of a Tower on a Mountain]
X. PROPOSITION To know the height of a tower situated on a mountain. If you wish to know the height of a tower or other object situated on a mountain, place yourself on a flat spot near it, then, according to the doctrine previously given for two stations, take the height of the tower and the mountain together: then the height of the mountain separately, which, subtracted from the first height, will leave that of the tower.

[Example: Measuring a Tower on a Mountain]
EXAMPLE. Wishing to measure a tower situated on a mountain, I first look at the height of this tower, as if it were on flat ground, which I find to be 100 paces. And because the mountain gives it an advantage, I observe its height separately, which I find to be 50 paces, which I subtract from the total height I previously found, and 50 paces remain, which is the particular height of this tower. By this means, you can measure the length of a window or the advantage of a house roof, and other similar things.

[Understanding Mountain and Tower Heights]
This 10th proposition should be understood as follows: if from some place on a plain, one discovers a tower, building, or other structure built on a mountain that is elevated above the same plain, with their base situated on the summit or another part of the said mountain: one might wish to know both the height of the said tower alone above the mountain, or of the mountain alone below the tower, as well as the height of both together.

[Measuring Tower Height on a Mountain]
above the plain. Otherwise, in the way Jacquinot proposed it to us, without determining or assuming anything, difficulties may arise. It is indeed true that the height of the court, for example, with the mountain above the plain, can be known by taking it along a line, which from the visible and remarkable point at the summit of the said court would fall perpendicularly onto the said plain; but to take the height from the summit of the mountain above the same plain would be of no use for obtaining the said height of the tower alone, if the foot or base of the said tower were not remarkable, and not sufficiently recognizable on the said summit of the mountain; and therefore it is more appropriate to say, to obtain the height of the tower alone, that after having measured the height of its summit above the plain, one must measure the height of its base above the same plain, in order to conclude the height of the tower alone from the difference of the two heights. Now, the method of practicing this proposition is no different from the preceding one, namely, according to Jacquinot, by two different stations, which we have shown can be done by a single one, of which we will repeat nothing here, inasmuch as this proposition is but an application of the precepts of the preceding one. We will only warn that although it is proposed here to know two heights in one, it is nevertheless not necessary, but rather useless, to do it according to Jacquinot, and thus as his figure shows in the Geometric Square. 355 represents it, four different stations, namely two for each of the said heights: it is enough to make only two for both, observing in each of the said stations the two points whose height one desires to know, always the same distance between the two stations will be proportional to each height above the Astrolabe, as the difference of the points of direct shadow converted or cut by the rule in the observation of each of the said points, is to the side or whole number of the square. We will also note on Jacquinot's figure something that the less advanced should be wary of, which is the inequality of the height of the Astrolabe, or of the observer's eye, figured in the four different stations. For absolutely in all this treatise, whether receding or advancing, whether rising or lowering, to make the observations, it must always be in straight lines, one parallel to the horizon, and the other perpendicular, otherwise there would be some disproportion in the distances between the stations to the required heights. For the distances, both of the said tower and of the mountain on which it is seated and elevated, we can still practice what we have said about the preceding proposition, either by two stations or by a single one. And supposing that the said tower is built precisely on the summit of the mountain, being able by means of a visible point at its top to know its distance, we will consequently have 334 The Use and Utility of the Astrolabe. that of the summit of the mountain, which distance of both should be taken, as said, from the point on the plain on which a perpendicular line would fall from the point marked at the top or extremity of the said tower; XL. PROPOSITION. To measure the length of a field, or other plains. One must consider that by the simple height of your eye you cannot justly measure any plains that are longer than the height of your eye, wherefore for longer measurements it would be necessary to be in an elevated window, or in another high place, by the known heights of which you will measure those fields. And to do this, you must hang the Astrolabe and direct the rule until you can see the extremity of the length you wish to measure, then you will find the rule on one or the other scale, or between the two. Firstly, if it falls on the direct scale, such proportion as 12 will have to the points of that scale that the said rule will touch, such proportion will have Concerning the Geometric Square. 355 will the height of your eye have to the distance: as if it falls on the sixth point of the said scale, the distance seen will be only half the height of your eye. But on the contrary, if the rule falls on the inverse scale at the same point, that distance will be twice as long as the height of your eye. And thus you can judge the other points: as if the rule fell on the 12th point of the inverse scale, the height of the eye would be only the 6th part of the distance, just as 1 is only the 6th part of 12. 336 The Use and Utility of the Astrolabe. This 11th proposition is proper and particular for measuring distances: whose extremity is visible and remarkable, and is, as it were, the converse of the 8th preceding one, in which, by a known distance or length, inasmuch as one can freely approach the foot of the thing to be measured, it is taught how to find its height. For here, by a known height, it is proposed to measure and know a distance or length. It is therefore necessary here to note that just as this proposition is only a simple conversion of the proportions of the said 8th, namely from the given to the required, from the known to the unknown, and from one ratio to the other: so all the encounters in this proposition must be similar to those of the said 8th. But in the aforesaid 8th proposition, the thing to be measured being supposed perpendicular to the horizon, and the distance parallel (by the continuation of the horizontal line of the Astrolabe up to the thing to be measured, on which line the said distance is measured) therefore in this proposition it is necessary to suppose and take care that the known height (which is the staff of which Jacquinot speaks) is perpendicular to the horizon, and that the distance to be measured is parallel, so that one is perpendicular to the other to relate to the small triangle that is formed in the observation on the Geometric Square of the Astrolabe. Otherwise, if there is some dissimilarity, and therefore disproportion, one would consequently have a false measurement. Now we have Concerning the Geometric Square. 337 noted this, inasmuch as it rarely happens that one can find a plain as even and parallel to the horizon as most of the heights proposed to be measured are straight and perpendicular: Or rather, inasmuch as it will happen even less often that any distance proposed to be measured is found to be such as to be able to obtain a just measurement by this proposition. What is stated above, nevertheless, being supposed possible: we say that Jacquinot, Stoeffler, and others too much restrict and confine the help and utility that can be drawn from the Astrolabe for the practice of this proposition concerning planimetry, saying that it is not possible with it to measure a length longer than 12 times the height of the eye of the measurer, or of his staff, that is to say, of the Astrolabe, if it is attached to the said staff, which would always be more secure than holding it in the hand to sight over the said staff as Jacquinot's figure represents. It is indeed true that in distances further than the height of the Astrolabe, the rule always cutting the side of the inverse shadow, and therefore the parts of the said shadow cut by the rule being proportional to the whole side of the square, as the height of the Astrolabe to the proposed distance: if one simply considers the number of the division of the square into 10, 12, or another number of parts, and that the rule cannot rise on the scale of the inverse shadow, diminish the number of cut parts only to unity, the smallest proportion will be of the height 358 The Use and Utility of the Astrolabe.

[Geometric Square Scale Divisions]
of the Astrolabe at the proposed distance, like from the unit to the side or whole number of the scale; thus, the side or whole number of the scale being 10 or 11, 60 or 100 equal parts, one could only measure a distance 10 or 11, 60 or 100 times longer than the said height of the Astrolabe. But as in physical things and continuous quantities (such are the degrees, points, or divisions of the Altimetric scale), the unit is not considered mathematically as a mathematical point, and therefore indivisible, but physically as an infinitely subdivisible quantity, which is therefore like a proportional mean between each of its parts up to an infinite division, and each of its corresponding multiples up to an infinite multiplication: Thus, our altimetric divisions, whether of 10 or 11, 60 or 100, both each of them and the first represented and named as the unit, are always understood to be divisible, at least into certain common and easily distinguishable and recognizable aliquot parts, such as 1, 2, 4, or others. This, which is actually done in large instruments that can be divided into 100, 1000, or another number of parts, must be understood in small instruments, such as in one of ours, in which each 10th degree can and must be estimated as subdivisible at least into 2, 3, 4, 5, or 10 other parts, according to what can be judged from observation to estimate more justly the

[Regarding the Geometric Square]
segments made by the rule on each of the said whole parts. Therefore, we conclude that with a simple division of 10, for example, as in our small Astrolabe, there will be a way to measure a length greater than 10 times the height of the Astrolabe, according to how, by a subdivision of each degree by 2, 3, or 4, the entire scale of the Geometric Square will be understood to be divided into 10, 30, or 40 equal parts, to be used as needed in case of a distance 20, 30, or 40 times greater than the said height of the Astrolabe.

[Example of Geometric Square Measurement]
EXAMPLE. For example, a length to be measured on a plain is proposed, the extremity of which is remarkable; if, observing the said extremity, the rule is found stopped on one quarter of the first division of the umbra versa, we will say that as 2.5 is to 10 (the whole number of the scale), so 5 feet of Astrolabe height will be to 100 feet or 33 toises 2 feet of the proposed length.

[Measuring Inaccessible Distances and Heights]
ADDITION. Regarding the distances and heights of inaccessible things, whose base is visible, and this by a single station. We will add this remark here: That, since by the 8th proposition, by means of a known distance or length, one knows a height, and by this, with a known height, one knows a length, there is a way with these two propositions, from a single station and the same height of Astrolabe or Geometric Square, to measure and know a proposed distance with a height that would be perpendicular to it, which one could not approach; provided, however, that the extremity of the said length or distance, or else the foot and base of the said height, be visible and remarkable. For the Astrolabe being hung on a stick, and elevated to a certain height, if observing through the pinnules the foot of a tower, for example, one can by the present proposition measure and know the distance of its foot, in the same proportion to the height of the Astrolabe, as the parts of the umbra recta to the whole side of the square, or the same whole side of the square to the parts of the umbra versa: One can then, afterwards, by the said 8th proposition, observing the summit of the said tower without varying anything, except the rule, know its height which will always be proportional to the said first found distance, as the parts of the umbra versa to the whole side of the square, or the same whole side of the square to the parts of the umbra recta.

[Example: Measuring a Tower's Height and River Width]
EXAMPLE. Let a tower be proposed, for example, which one cannot approach due to a river; the base, however, is uncovered, and one wishes to know the width of the said river that flows at the foot of the tower, together with the height of the said tower. The stick for hanging the Astrolabe being fixed at the edge of the river, in a place corresponding to the height of the foot of the said tower, and the Astrolabe attached at a height of 5 feet, let the foot of the said tower be observed through the pinnules and marked on what point of the scale the rule meets, and let the points found be 2.5 of umbra versa; then raising the rule, the rest remaining immobile, let the summit of the said tower be observed, and the rule found in this second observation on the 6th point, also of umbra versa. This being done, we will say that as 2.5 points of umbra versa in the first observation are to 10 (the whole number of the scale), so 5 feet of Astrolabe height will be to 35 feet 7 inches of river width, or of distance between the place of observation and the foot of the said tower. Then we will say again, as 10 (the whole number of the scale) is to 6 points of umbra versa of the second observation, so the 35 feet 7 inches found distance will be to 21 feet 4 inches of the height of the said tower.

[Measuring Depths of Wells and Cisterns]
XII AND LAST proposition. To measure wells, pits, cisterns, and other depressed places, of which one can see the bottom, and whose width is known. Just as we measure unknown heights by known distances: Similarly, we measure depths by the knowledge of their widths. To find therefore the depth of a well, one must look through the rule, directing the sight from the nearest part of the well's edge to the opposite and most distant part of the water. And then if the rule falls on the 10th point of one or the other of these scales, the width will be equal to the depth: but if it falls on the umbra recta scale, the said depth will be greater than the width, by as much as the points touched by that rule will be less than 10. On the contrary, when it falls on the points of the umbra versa

[Depth Measurement with Geometric Square]
the depth is smaller than the width according to the proportion of these points to 12. EXAMPLE. Wanting to measure the depth of a well, let it be A. B. C. D. by looking through the two opposite points of the top and bottom, like A. D. I find the rule of the back cutting the 10th point between the umbra recta and umbra versa scale. By this I know the depth of this well to be equal to its width, but if it fell on the 6th point of the umbra recta scale, then the width would be half of its depth, so that if the width of the said well is found to be 6 feet, I say the well is 12 feet deep. Similarly, if the rule touched on the first point of this scale, which is the 12th part of its width, then the diameter or width of the well will be the 12th part of the depth, which according to the given example will contain 72 feet, and thus one must judge the other proportions according to the points touched by the rule.

[Observing Depths with the Astrolabe]
This must be understood by observing with the Astrolabe, that is to say, that the measurer, making his observation, must hold his Astrolabe in hand or otherwise freely suspended in such a way that by raising or lowering the rule, he can, directing his sight through the holes of the pinnules, see both the nearest inner edge of the well and the extremity of the water's edge on the opposite part of the bottom. Thus, the eye of the measurer, the two holes of the pinnules, the two opposite points on the upper edge, and at the extremity of the water, must be in one and the same line: otherwise the observation will not be accurate, nor the measurement found precise.

[Measuring Depths: A Necessary Precaution]
But here it is still necessary to note a necessary precaution in the practice of this proposition, which, as Jacquinot says, teaches by the knowledge of widths to measure depths, just as by the preceding propositions, by means of some known distances or widths, heights have been measured. For just as in the preceding propositions it was always presupposed that the heights proposed to be measured were erected perpendicularly, both to the horizon and to the planes on which the observations were made and the measurements taken; so also in this one it must still be supposed that the things whose depth is proposed to be measured are perpendicular, both to the horizon and to the planes on which the widths that we suppose to be known are taken and measured: and this all the more so as few things of this quality are encountered. For although ordinarily the widths we wish to use, or rather the planes where we take them, are parallel to the horizon, such as the edges and copings of wells and cisterns, nevertheless the depth is not willingly found to be plumb due to the slope, the force of the foot, or the necessary counter-slope to strengthen the walls against the force and weight of the earth they support. Thus, a well will always be found to be wider at the top than at the bottom; and thus a ditch will always be narrower at the bottom than at the top. This being verifiable and noted, it is easy to judge that for want of having foreseen it, there would infallibly be more or less error in the measurement according to the greater or lesser difference in the said widths.

[Practical Application for Depth Measurement]
Therefore, to practice this proposition more surely: we will advise the apprentice to begin by recognizing the difference in widths if there is any between the bottom and the top of the proposed object. This will be done as follows: the balance of his Astrolabe being adjusted, he will precisely stop the rule on the meridian or perpendicular line of the back, and his said Astrolabe hanging freely, he will advance in such a way that through the holes of the pinnules he can see the foot of the wall, and will mark the place where he made this observation. And from the same place he will observe the opposite extremity of the bottom: and by the proportion of the points of the scale touched by the rule, to the shadow of the entire scale, or conversely (according to whether the depth is greater or lesser than the width), he will judge that which the width (diminished by double what he will have advanced, that is to say, reduced and made equal to the bottom width) will have to the depth: and he will surely practice all that is taught here by Jacquinot, without however stopping at his figure, which is little conformable to the nature of the thing proposed to be represented, and which represents to us more naturally the method of measuring the distance of some elevated mirror, than the depth of a well or cistern: in that both the surface of the water and the enclosure of the walls, figured like the glass of a mirror furnished with its frame, are represented to us perpendicularly on the plane of the horizon, as well as the measurer. And it seems that the measurer only wishes to know how far he is from the marked extremity D. Which, according to the proportion of the small triangle marked on the Astrolabe, would be as distant as the height of the measurer's eye: to which, however, the same appearance resists. In this appears the difference between the painter or other who simply imitates and follows his fantasies and imaginations to express them according to his capacity, or him who, considering the said fantasies and imaginations in nature, imitates nature by expressing them: which we call representing things naturally. This is why, instead of Jacquinot's aforesaid figure, we advise the apprentice to consider the one we have placed below at the end of the following addition.

[Addition: Measuring Depths Without Known Widths]
We will add here, as a final point to this last proposition, what we have given in the preceding years, that just as it does not always happen that the proposed objects, whose bottom is visible, can be measured in width to find their depth from there, so also there is a way in the said case to measure the said depth just as easily as by the preceding propositions we have made the heights without having known the distances: and this method should be all the more esteemed as it is general for knowing both the widths, however unequal from bottom to top, and the depths, and this by two observations, and nevertheless from a single station.

[Measuring Wells, Cisterns, and Ditches]
Therefore, a well, cistern, or ditch being proposed, of which one desires to know both the depth and the width. Let the Astrolabe be elevated on the coping of the well, the edge of the cistern, or the parapet of the ditch in such a way, as we have said before with a suitable stick chosen for this effect, that at two different heights the opposite edge of the water in the well or cistern, or the foot of the wall opposite the bottom of the ditch, can be perceived through the pinnules. For what proportion will the difference in the number of points of direct or inverse shadow touched by the rule in the two observations have to the smaller number, such will be the difference in the height of the Astrolabe in the said two observations to the depth below the center of the Astrolabe in the lowest of them. And therefore, by subtracting the elevation of the center of the Astrolabe in the lowest observation above the said coping or parapet, one will have the exact depth of the well below the coping, or of the ditch below its parapet. As also, what proportion will the difference in height of the Astrolabe in the two observations always have to the proposed width (to be taken between the center of the Astrolabe and the point at the same height, corresponding above to that which will have been observed below), that the difference in the direct shadows laid down or reduced will be found to have to the whole number of the scale.

[Combined Measurement Techniques]
So, adding to this what we have said above for the measurement of height by a single station, it will be easy to measure and know both the depth of a ditch and the height and distance of the wall of some castle or fortress, and this from a single and same station; but by four observations made at two different heights or situations of the Astrolabe or Geometric Square. We will content ourselves with having only noted and somewhat represented this by one of the figures we have added above.

[Use of the Universal Geometric Trellis]
The small squares, divided and distinguished by tens and hundreds, which we have had placed on the back of our small Astrolabes, is what we call the geometric Trellis, or universal Geometric Square. Its use is powerful, and as much, indeed more ample than that of the geometric Square, or common altimeter scale; but much quicker and easier, and can be adapted to all sorts of divisions, both ordinary such as of 11, 60, 144, and other multiples of 11: or of 10, 100, 1000, and other multiples of 10: as well as non-ordinary such as of 8, 16, 15, 30, and others at will. And moreover, it needs no knowledge of Arithmetic, except for simple numeration: because most of the propositions of Jacquinot's geometric treatise (I mean all those that can be practiced by a single observation) are practiced.

[Universal Geometric Grid Use]
on this Grid without any indication of analogy or rule of three: that is to say, without needing to perform any multiplication or division, one can immediately obtain what is required. And for propositions involving two observations, this small Grid has the additional advantage of facilitating the necessary analogies encountered therein, and this by exhibiting the unknown term without any multiplication or division, as will be more particularly noted hereafter in its place. Furthermore, it does not depend in any way on the geometric square, but can serve alone: provided only (I mean if any Grid were made alone and separate from the Astrolabe) that it is positioned in such a way, during observation, that one of its sides is perpendicular to the horizon, and the other parallel, as is seen on the back of our Astrolabes, where one of these sides is seated and corresponds to the straight horizon, and the other is perpendicular to it, corresponding with the perpendicular line of the Astrolabe. And the angle where the said two sides join and terminate corresponds precisely to the center of the Astrolabe: in a word, it has on the back of the Astrolabe such and similar a situation as one of the geometric squares described below. This is not to say that one cannot use the said Grid in the form that one sometimes uses the geometric square with a plumb bob suspended from a thread, the pinnules being attached to one of the sides of the said square or grid. We have said that the use of the said Grid can be accommodated to all sorts of divisions of squares or geometric scales. This is all the more true because the said Grid can alone represent an infinity of diverse geometric squares differently divided: which is justified by its very construction, inasmuch as all the spaces are square and equal, and two lines equally distant from the center also comprise a square space, whose sides are all divided by the lines of the interior squares in such a number as is the order of their distance from the said center, as the two fifteenths form a square divided into 15 equal parts, the two twentieths form one divided into 20, and the two thirtieths one divided into 30, and so on for others, according to any other number of squares comprised. And therefore it is true to say that the use of this Grid is more ample and more general than that of any other common geometric square, far from the distinction of straight or inverse shadow being considerable in the operation for the measurement of any height, length, or depth; inasmuch as on the said Grid one can always consider a small similar triangle, and similarly positioned to the large triangle whose sides are proposed to be measured. We will therefore come to the practice and more particular explanation of its use, which for more clarification we will apply to Jacquinot's propositions on the use of the Square.

[Finding Height by Sun's Shadow]
To find the height of a tower, or other thing, by the sun's shadow. If we fix the Alidade on the 45th degree of the circle on the side of the said Grid, that is to say, on the midpoint of the quadrant of the said circle where the said Grid is figured, we will see that the fiducial line of the said Alidade will precisely cut all the large and small squares of the said Grid through their angles, and will divide each into two equal right-angled and isosceles triangles. Now the Alidade, or its fiducial line, always representing the ray or solar, or visual, being the diagonal of the said squares or triangles, one of the sides comprising the right angle also always represents the shadow, or the distance of some body, and the other side the said body; consequently, the said sides being equal, by the definition of isosceles triangles, it follows that the said shadows or distances, in this case, must be equal in length to the height of their body. When therefore the sun happens to be elevated 45 degrees above the horizon, the Alidade positioned, as said, on the Astrolabe hanging freely, will be disposed to receive and give free passage to the sun's rays through its sights, and consequently, when this happens, marking at the same time the extremity of the shadow of some perpendicular body, such as a tower or pyramid, without doubt the length of the shadow will be equal to the height of the said tower or pyramid, except for what we have noted above in the note on this first proposition.

[Determining Height by Visual Line]
To know the height by the fiducial line. This proposition does not differ in anything from the preceding one for understanding: but instead of the sun's ray, one uses the visual ray. The Alidade therefore being placed and fixed, as in the preceding, on the 45th degree or midpoint of the quadrant of the circle on the side of the said Grid, and the Astrolabe hanging freely by its handle, if on a very level plain one moves back or forward so that through the sights of the said Alidade one can perceive the extremity of some body elevated straight above the said plain, the distance measured between the measurer (or the center of the Astrolabe, that is to say of the said Grid, supposing it were alone and separate) and the said body will infallibly be equal to the height of the said body above the eye or the said center: and consequently, by adding the height of the eye or of the center of the Astrolabe or Grid above the said plain, one will have the entire height of the body to be measured.

[Example: Measuring Pyramid Height]
EXAMPLE, to begin to make known the use by practice: the Alidade being fixed as above on the 45th degree, if by moving back or forward one finally perceives through the pinnules the extremity or point of a pyramid, and by measurement one finds the distance from the midpoint of the foot of the said pyramid to be 15 fathoms: Counting on the horizontal line of the said Grid, from the center, the said number of 15: one will see that the line which falls perpendicularly on the said number is cut by the Alidade at a similar number of 15: consequently the height of the said pyramid will also be 15 fathoms above the eye of the measurer or the center of the Grid: For, as said, all the lines of the said Grid parallel to the horizontal line are analogous and represent the plains on which the observations are made, and the lines perpendicular to the said horizontal line are analogous and represent the bodies elevated straight above the said plains.

[Heights when Sun is More or Less Elevated]
To know the heights when the sun is more or less elevated than 45 degrees. These two propositions are practiced with the sun's ray, which, according to whether it is more or less inclined, makes the shadows of bodies more or less long than the said bodies. Therefore, at whatever height of the sun, the Astrolabe hanging freely, and the Alidade being raised or lowered until the ray passes freely through the sights of the pinnules, if at the same time one makes a mark at the extremity of some shadow proceeding from some body elevated straight, and after having measured the length of the shadow one counts on the horizontal line of the said Grid, from the center, the number of measures, always the other line which is perpendicular to it at the end of the numeration will be cut by the Alidade at the same number of measures as that of the proposed height to be measured from which the said shadow proceeds.

[Example: Measuring Wall Height]
EXAMPLE. Although the matter is clear, nevertheless to help the less experienced, we will guide their hand in these practices with examples. A wall presents itself whose height you wish to know by its shadow: Hang your Astrolabe by its handle and raise or lower the Alidade little by little until the sun's ray passes freely through the sights of the pinnules (which you will recognize when, approaching something against the pinnules, the light appears well rounded.) At the same time, stick a stick at the extremity of the shadow of the said wall, and measure that shadow straight towards the sun: if you find it

[Using the Grid or Square]
of 15 toises, you will count from the center of the Grid, that is to say, from the Astrolabe, along the horizontal line, the said number of 13. And considering the perpendicular raised on the end of the said number of 13, you will count the parallels comprised on the said perpendicular; that is to say, the number of divisions of the said perpendicular comprised between the Alidade and the said horizontal line; and the number of the said parallels or divisions will be the height of the wall. As if the sun were only elevated by 31 degrees, the number of these parallels comprised between the Alidade and the horizontal line would give 7 toises and 3/4 of a toise, or rather 7 toises, 4 feet, 9 inches, and 1/2 of an inch.

[Note 1: Measuring the Shadow]
We have said in this example that one must measure the shadow extending directly towards the sun, to note in passing an inconvenience that can often occur and could mislead the less experienced, which is that if the elevated body, whose height one wishes to know, has some width, and the sun's ray is even slightly oblique, it is important to know and recognize the point at its foot or base that corresponds to that of the summit from which the shadow was cast. This is often difficult to find on the expanse of walls, unless there is some mark, sign, or other thing at the top of the wall that can be easily noticed and distinguished in the length of the shadow. Therefore, for greater precaution, when marking a point at the end of the shadow, one must at the same time note or recognize at the base of the body, wall, tower, or other wide object, some line that corresponds, with respect to the sun, to the point marked in the shadow; or else, by immediately measuring the shadow, draw it directly towards the sun.

[Note 2: Grid Divisions]
It is also to be noted here that one can assign to each division of the said Grid any number one wishes, such as 2, 3, 4, 6, 10, 12, and others; and thus no number of measurements will ever be presented that cannot be accommodated and counted on the said Grid to obtain the required estimation. This is especially necessary to consider and observe when the perpendiculars are cut by the Alidade between two parallel lines. As in our example, the perpendicular on the number 13 is cut by the Alidade at a height of 31 degrees between the 7th and 8th parallel, so that one must estimate and judge the value of the two segments, which we have said is 4, and so for the others.

[Proposition 8: Height by Visual Line]
This proposition is nothing other than a repetition of the two preceding ones, but differently applied. In the two preceding ones, a lesser or greater inclination of the sun's ray than 45 degrees is considered; in this one, a lesser or greater inclination of the visual ray is considered. But as we have noted above that in operations with this instrument, the difference between direct or inverse shadows is not considerable, so also this diverse inclination of the visual ray above or below 45 degrees brings no diversity to the operation of this proportion, any more than in the two preceding ones; there being always only one and the same method of practice. And only one and the same position of triangles being observed, all this diversity only serves to diversify the inequality between the bases and the perpendiculars of the said triangles. We will therefore understand the practice of this proposition, as of the two preceding ones, under a single method of operation, which will be as follows.

[Measuring Height of a Plumb Body]
To measure the height of any plumb-elevated body, whose foot or base is freely accessible: Choose a flat and even place, and at whatever distance it may be, holding the Astrolabe, or suspending it by its handle from something, raise or lower the Alidade until the summit of the elevated body is perceived through the sights of the pinnules. Then measure the distance. And on the said grid, mark the perpendicular as distant from the center as the said measured distance contains measures. For the number of parallels comprised on the said perpendicular between the Alidade and the horizontal line will give the height of the said body in a similar number of said measures, always to be taken above the center of the said Grid.

[Example: Pyramid Height]
A pyramid presents itself, of which, to know its height, you look and observe its point from some place through the pinnules of the Alidade, perhaps stopped at the 50th degree of height on the limb. If then, having found by measuring the space between the place of observation and the middle of the base of the said pyramid to be 25 toises, you count how many parallels are comprised between the point where the Alidade cuts the 25th perpendicular and the horizontal line: You will have the number of toises that the height of the said pyramid contains, 41 toises, or 4 feet above the center of the said grid.

[Propositions 9 & 10: Tower Height]
These two propositions are still practiced on this instrument without any distinction of direct or inverse shadow, and we have also coupled them, inasmuch as the operation of one differs in nothing from that of the other; except that in the 10th proposition it is necessary to operate twice, because one seeks two different heights, each of which is found by the method of the 9th. Therefore, when it is proposed to find the height of some tower that cannot be approached because of a ditch, river, or other impediment, it will be necessary to make two observations, as was said on the fourth Geometrical, and in a place chosen so level that the Grid or the Astrolabe is at the same height in both observations: so that the line of distance between them is parallel both to the plane of the place, that is to say, to the horizon, and to the parallel lines of the said Grid, that is to say, that it coincides with the horizontal line of the said Grid. And with this precaution, in the state of the rule or alidade at the time of the first observation of the summit of the said tower, the 10th or 100th, 20th, or any other parallel desired will be chosen among the parallels, and it will be seen and marked at what point of division, counting from the central perpendicular, the said Alidade cuts it. Then in the second observation, which will be made by moving back or forward in a straight line, it will again be seen and marked at what other point of division of the same parallel the said Alidade will meet. This being said, having measured the distance between the said two observations, what proportion the difference of the two points cut by the Alidade on the said parallel has to the distance of the said parallel from the horizontal line, such will the number of measures between the two stations have to the number of measures of the required height to be taken above the center of the said Grid. And this analogy can be easily resolved on the said Grid without any multiplication or division, by only counting the first term from the center of the Grid on the horizontal line, that is to say, for the number of the first term, taking the perpendicular as distant from the first central one, and on it counting the number of the second term from the horizontal line, and at the end of the numeration applying the Alidade, it will cut the parallel corresponding to the number of the third term at a point whose distance from the horizontal line will give the fourth term required for the height of the said tower above the said Grid.

[Example]
null

[Measuring Tower Height with Two Observations]
In the first observation of the summit of a tower surrounded by water, the rule is found on the 35th division point of the 60th parallel; and in the second observation, the rule is met on the 20th point of the same parallel, and the measured distance between the two observations is 9 toises. Therefore, as the difference between 35 and 20, which is 15, is to 60, so 9 toises of distance between the two observations must be to the height of the tower above the trellis. And to obtain this 4th term, you will count 60 divisions on the 15th perpendicular, and applying the rule to the 54th point of the Trellis or Square, you will find that it cuts the 9th perpendicular exactly on the 36th point of its divisions, and that will be the height of the said tower above the central parallel of the said Trellis.

[Measuring Tower Height on a Mountain]
In this way, having measured the height of a tower situated above a mountain, then the height of the said mountain, or rather of the foot of the said tower, by the difference between the two heights, one will have the height of the said tower alone: which is what the said 10th proposition proposes.

[Note on Simple Operation]
It is to be noted here that this is the simple operation and practice of this proposition; one could easily find what is required without any analogical instruction, there being, after having made the first observation, only to draw a line on the said trellis along the alhidade's line, and after having made the second observation and measured the distance between the two, the alhidade remaining in the same state, one will search among the parallels for the one whose divisions comprised between the alhidade and the said line first drawn (that is to say, between the two positions of the alhidade in the two observations) will be equal in number to the measures found between the two observations or stations: for the distance of such a parallel from the first central one will give the exact height required above the said Trellis or Astrolabe.

[Measuring Lengths of Fields and Plains]
As in the preceding propositions we have sought and found the measure of heights on the perpendicular lines of the said Trellis which are analogous to them, so for the practice of this proposition we will seek and find the measure of the lengths of plains and fields on the parallels analogous to them. Therefore, to know the length of some field or the width of some river, the Astrolabe or Trellis being elevated to some known height, the alhidade being disposed in such a way that by its pinnules one can observe the extremity of the proposed length, or the opposite bank of the river, it will cut the parallel corresponding to the number of measures of the height of the Astrolabe at a point as distant in number of divisions as there are similar measures to that of the said height of the Astrolabe between the measurer's staff, or his foot, and the observed extremity.

[Measuring Depths of Wells and Pits]
Since depths are lines perpendicular to the horizon (for it is of such depths that this proposition intends to speak), we will therefore again seek and find their measure on the perpendiculars of the said Trellis, and this by a method as simple as the preceding ones. When, therefore, it is proposed to find the depth of a well, whose width we can freely measure, the Astrolabe being held in hand, or otherwise in such a way that by the pinnules both the inner upper edge and the extremity of the water at the opposite part of the bottom can be sighted and perceived along the same line, the alhidade, in this state, will cut the perpendicular corresponding to the number of measures of the interior width of the well (which we assume to be equal from top to bottom) at a point as distant in number of divisions as there are in the said depth similar measures to that of the width.

[Addition: Measuring Hypotenuses and Ladders]
This is not all; this hanging Trellis goes even further. We have already had the length of the shadow, or the known distance, and the height of the wall: a little more effort will give us the length of a ladder which, placed on the extremity of the shadow or the distance, will touch the top of the wall. In a word, we already have the two sides of a right-angled triangle; we need to find the diagonal or hypotenuse. For this small Trellis can and should also serve for the measurement of all right-angled triangles, and if necessary, of all kinds of rectilinear triangles, just as for the measurement of some simple lengths, heights, or depths. If, therefore, in our example, we want to know the length of the said ladder, that is to say, of the line which would touch at one end the extremity of the shadow, or distance, and at the other the height of the wall, by making a point, or a small mark, easy to erase on the alhidade at the place where it cuts the perpendiculars which give the heights, and this mark being transferred to the first parallel, or to the first perpendicular, it will show between their divisions, from the center to the said point or mark, the length of the said line or ladder.

[Example: Ladder Length Calculation]
For example, if by a known distance of 9 feet one had found the height of a wall to be 30 feet, and one wanted to know the length of a ladder suitable for climbing it, the alhidade remaining in the state of observation of the said height, and a mark being made on the point where it cuts the 16th perpendicular, the said alhidade being transferred to the horizontal line, or to the first and central perpendicular, it will be found to comprise between its center and the said marked point on it 34 divisions, and of so many measures will be the length of the ladder which will join the extremities of the distance and the height of the said wall.

[End of Propositions on the Geometric Square]
End of Propositions on the Geometric Square or Trellis. We have also found it appropriate to add here some brief demonstrations which are found added in previous impressions to strengthen and secure the use of the Geometric Square, and this to satisfy those who, being pleased with them, might have desired them in this impression as well as in the preceding ones, and to correct what we have recognized as missing in some places.

[Demonstrations for the Gnomon]
DEMONSTRATIONS FOR THE PRACTICE AND USE OF THE GNOMON, OR THE ALTIMETRIC SCALE. And Firstly: Of the dimensions that are made by a single line.

[Gnomon Practice and Similar Triangles]
To better retain the practice of the Gnomon or Altimetric Scale, we have here placed a demonstration which serves generally for dimensions that are found by three known terms, of which the third can be measured to have the fourth which is unknown. This is done by two similar and orthogonal triangles, of which one is formed within the said Gnomon, and the other outside: inasmuch as the thing

[Measuring Height with the Gnomon]
5. 6. Demonstration for the practice and use of measuring whether something is high, deep, long, or wide, is always equidistant or parallel to one of the sides of the said Gnomon. Therefore, the fiducial or visual line that intersects them causes similar angles. Thus, by the similarity of triangles, the sides are proportional. For if the said visual line intersects the Scale of the direct shadow, then the height of the thing to be measured will have the same proportion to 12 as the distance of the thing to the number of points on the said Scale of the direct shadow. Similarly, the depth of a thing will have the same proportion to 12 as the width to the number of points of the direct shadow. As you can see in the first figure where the height of a tower is A, E, and your station at point C, where the fiducial line intersects the Scale of the direct shadow at point F. Now the line of station C G is parallel to A B, but because the visual line F C A intersects them at points C and A, angle C of triangle F C G will be equal to angle A of triangle C A B, by the second part of the 29th proposition of the 1st book of Euclid, by which also angle F of triangle C F G is equal to angle C of triangle A C B: therefore the two right-angled triangles F G C and C B A are equiangular, so the sides opposite similar angles are proportional, as Euclid demonstrates in the 4th Proposition of his 6th book. The proportion then of FG to GC is as CB to BA. Of these four, three are given, namely F G and C G, which are from the Gnomon, and C B by measurement. Therefore, by the rule of proportion, the fourth, B A, will be known.

[Measuring Depth with the Gnomon]
Similarly, it must be understood that when the said fiducial line intersects the scale of the inverse shadow, as at point H of the same figure, and the station is D. Then the height of the thing to be measured will have such a proportion to the number of points of the inverse shadow as the distance to 12. And the depth with respect to the said points of the direct shadow will be what the width is with respect to 12. For by the first part of the said 29th Proposition, angle H of triangle EHD is equal to angle A of triangle D A B: And the angles opposite at point D are equal by the 15th of the first; therefore the two right-angled triangles are similar, and for this reason the sides will be proportional: therefore, as there are three known terms, namely DE, EH, and DB by measurement, consequently the fourth, which is AB, will be known.

[Measuring Inaccessible Heights (Two Stations)]
Of Dimensions made by two stations. II. PROPOSITION. Now if the thing to be measured is inaccessible, then the 3rd and 4th terms will be unknown, so it is necessary to operate differently from above, the demonstration of which follows. Let A B in the first figure be the height of the tower which cannot be approached, and let it first be that the fiducial line at stations C and D intersects the Scale of the inverse shadow at points F and H. Therefore, in the two triangles F D E and A D B, due to their similarity, F E is contained as many times in E D as A B in B D; therefore E D (which in the instrument is 12 points) being divided by F E, yields a number demonstrating how many times A B is in B D. Similarly, due to the similarity of triangles H C G and A C B, it is found that G H is contained as many times in E C as A B in B C: therefore the number demonstrating the quotient or how many times A B is in B C will be known: consequently the quotient of A B in C D will be known. Therefore, C D being divided by that quotient number, A B results, to which the height must be added to obtain the desired height.

[Euclidean Proof for Inaccessible Heights]
This consequence is drawn from the 19th of the 5th Book of Euclid. For since D B is to A B as D E is to E F by the similarity of the two triangles D E F, D B A: the ratio of D E to E F being known, that of D B to B A will also be known: but by the similarity of the two triangles C G H, C B A, C B is also to B A as C G to G H: consequently since the ratio of C G to G H is known, that of C B to B A will also be known; the ratio therefore of each of the two D B and B C to the same B A is known: but D B is the whole, and B C is the part; therefore, by the said 19th, if the whole D B and its part C B have a given ratio to B A, then the other remaining part C D will also have a given and known ratio to B A: but C D is given and known by measurement, therefore B A will also be given and known. For by multiplying the number of measures of C D by the term of the known ratio corresponding to A B, and dividing the product by the term of the said ratio corresponding to C D, the value of A B will be obtained in the same measures as C D. As if the known ratio of C D to A B is 1 to 3, and C D is measured as 20 fathoms: multiplying 20 by 3, and dividing the product 60 by 1, we will have 60 fathoms for A B. And this is what the preceding demonstration means when it adds: Therefore C D being divided by the quotient number, A B results. And inasmuch as from the same observations above, and by the same method, we have obtained the knowledge of the distance as of B C. We will further add, since in the same figure by the similarity of triangles D E F, D B A, and of triangles C G H, and C B A, D B is to A B as D E is to E F, and C B is to B A as C G to G H, that the two rectangles G H in C B, and E F in D B are equal to each other, each of them being equal to a third, namely C G or D E in B A by the 16th of the 6th book of Euclid. But by the 14th of the same book, the two rectangles D B in E F, and C B in G H being equal, their sides will be proportional, and therefore as G H is to E F, so D B is to C B, and by dividing as G H minus E F, that is F H is to E F, so D B minus C B, that is C D is to C B: but the first two terms F, and F H, the difference between G H and E F, are known on the Gnomon or Scale of the Square, the third D C is also known by measurement, therefore the fourth B C will also be known, which was to be demonstrated. We will also add here the proper and particular demonstration for the second method that we have added above on the subject of inaccessible heights proposed to be measured, and this by reduction of inverse shadows to direct shadows, in order to divide the product of the multiplication of the entire side of the square by the number of measures of the interval between the observations, by the difference of the said direct shadows, in order to obtain a quotient equal in number to the measures of the proposed height above the center of the Astrolabe.

[Shadow Conversion and Astrolabe Height]
Let then, in the 3rd figure added by us, H be the height proposed to be measured A R, and the two observations at points C and D such that the two visual rays or fiducial lines A C, A D cut the side of the inverse shadow, in the first observation at point H, and in the second and further away at point F, the height of the Astrolabe in these two observations being C S or D T, that is B R. It is therefore necessary first to show the reason why in the aforesaid second general method, conversion from inverse shadow to direct shadow is made, and how this conversion brings no change or difference in the measurement. Inasmuch as, by the construction of the Astrolabe or Geometric square, the two lines C G, K I are parallel, as also the two C H, G H are parallel: The line C H, by the 19th of the first of Euclid, falling between them makes the angles H C G, C I K equal, as also I C K and C H Q equal. Therefore the triangle C K H is similar to the triangle H G C, and consequently as H G is to G C, so C K, that is G G, will be to K I: but H G is inverse shadow, and K I is direct shadow: therefore the proposed height B A is to the distance B C in the same proportion as C K to K I (direct shadow), as H G (inverse shadow) is to C G: that is to say, the same proportion is maintained, after the conversion of the entire side of the square to its parts.

[Measuring Height with Right Shadows]
of the right shadow, which previously from the parts of the versed shadow on the same entire side of the square, we will say the same of the two triangles FED, DLM. To demonstrate then that by means of the two known right shadows KI & LK, or rather by their difference, we can ascertain the measure of a proposed height. We say that in Figure 34, due to the parallels AB & CK, or DL, just as BC & KI, or LM, the triangle ABC is made similar to triangle CKI, and triangle ABD to triangle DLM. Therefore, as ML is to DB, so DB will be to BA, and similarly as IK is to KC, so CB will be to BA. And by permutation, as ML is to DB, so LD will be to BA, and as IK is to CB, so KC, that is, its equal LD, will be to the same BA. Therefore, by equal reason, as ML is to DB, so IK, that is, NL cut, its equal, is to CB. And by the 19th of the 5th book of Euclid, the remainder MN will be to the remainder DC as the whole ML is to the whole DB. But we have already found that ML is to DB as LD is to BA; therefore MN will also be to DC as LD is to BA. Now the first three terms are known, namely MN, the difference of the two numbers of the right shadow; DC, the interval of the observations; and LD, the whole number of the Scale or Geometric Square. Therefore, the fourth term BA will be known in the same measures as DC.

[Adding Astrolabe Height]
It remains to show, regarding the said 34th figure, why the height of the Astrolabe is added to the height found by analogy. This is because if we suppose the Gnomon or Altimetric Scale, assuming that the Astrolabe is elevated to the same height, as it should be, in two observations, the line passing through the center of the Astrolabe, such as DC, being horizontal, and therefore considered parallel to the horizontal plane RO, if it is extended to the proposed height, it will cut off a height, such as BR, equal to CS or DT, which are the two heights of the Astrolabe that we always assume to be equal. Therefore, BK being known by analogy, and BR by the elevation and height of the instrument, the entire known height AR will be obtained from the two combined and assembled parts, which was what was proposed to find.

[Measuring Inaccessible Height (Second Method)]
But if it happens in the two sections that the fiducial line intersects the scale of the right shadow, as at points M & L in the 4th figure, the operation will be different from what it has been described above. For one must multiply the distance between the two stations by 12, and divide the sum by the difference of the points, and the demanded height will result. For the demonstration of this, see the said 4th figure. For the similarity of triangles MCP and CAB, the proportion of MP to CB is as PC to AB. Similarly, the proportion of LP to DB is as PD to AB. And because the proportion of PC to BA is similar to the proportion of PD to BA, it follows that the proportion of MP to CB is as LP to DB. Therefore, by the 19th proposition of the 5th book of Euclid, the remainder LM to the remainder DC is as the total LP to the total DB. But LP to DB is as PD to BA, therefore LM to DC is as PD to BA. Of these four, the three LM, DC, and PD are known, therefore the fourth BA, which we seek, will be known.

[Easier Inaccessible Height Measurement]
We can even more easily obtain the height of an object that cannot be approached, provided that two stations can be made, in one of which the fiducial line is with the line of the middle shadow, and the other at will. For then one must multiply the distance of the stations by the number of points, and divide the sum by the difference that the said points have to 12, and the height of the object will result, which will always be equal to the distance of your first station.

[Example: Versed Shadow Measurement]
As in the 5th figure, if the distance of the stations CD was 10 paces, and the fiducial line intersecting the scale of the versed shadow at point F, 8 points. Let us therefore multiply CD, 10 paces, by GF, 8 points, and 80 will result. These being divided by FH, 4 points, 10 will result: so many paces does AB, which is the height, or BC, which is the distance of the first station, contain. The Demonstration. For the similarity of the triangles in the said 5th figure, the proportion of CG to GH is as CB to BA. And because CG is equal to GH, we therefore conclude that CD is equal to AB. Similarly, the proportion of DG to GF is as DB to BA. Now by the 16th proposition of the 6th book of Euclid, what is of CG in AB is equal to what is of GH in CB. Similarly, what is of DG in AB will be equal to what is of GF in DB. But because in each of the said similarities the first and the fourth are always the same and equal, it therefore follows that what is of GF in DB is equal to what is of GH in CB; therefore the proportion of GF to GH is as CB to DB by the second part of the said 16th proposition. The remainder FH to the remainder CD will therefore be as the total GH to the total BD, by the said 19th proposition of the 5th book. And because FH to CD, and GF to BG are each in the same proportion as the total to the total, the proportion therefore of FH to GD will be as GF to BC. Of these four, three are known, therefore the fourth CB, which is equal to AB, will be known.

[Single Station Inaccessible Height]
ADDITION. We will conclude this small treatise with a brief demonstration of the method we have given above, for finding from a single station the measures of a proposed inaccessible height. Let AR therefore be the proposed height in the 6th and last figure. And from a single station at point C, let the first observation be made with the Astrolabe elevated. Then, with the Astrolabe elevated at D, let the second be made. And let it be that in both, the rule or the two visual rays ACF and ADE cut the side of the versed shadow at points E and F. Let also from the imagined point C another visual ray be drawn parallel to ADE, namely CI, that is to say, making HI equal to GE, and drawing IC up to K. Now, because of the parallels AR, DS, GF, the angles BAC, CFH are said to be equal, as also BKC, CIH, that is DEG equal. And therefore, triangle BKC is similar to triangle CIH, or to its equal DEG, and triangle KAC to triangle CFH, and triangle KAG, that is ACD, to triangle CFI. Therefore, as CH is to HF, so CB is to BA, and as CH is to HI, so CB is to BK. And by permutation, as CH is to CB, so HF is to BA, and HI is to BK. Therefore, by the 19th of the 5th book, the remainder IF will be to the remainder AK, that is, its equal DC, as the total HF to the total AB. But the first three terms, IF (difference of the points of the versed shadow), CD (difference in height of the Astrolabe), and HF (points of the lowest observation), are known. And therefore the fourth proportional term AB will also be known in the same measures as those of the difference in height CD. To this fourth term, adding BK, the height of the Astrolabe in the said lowest observation, the entire proposed height AR will be obtained, which was what needed to be found.

[Note on Figures]
The Reader will be advised here that, for design and for greater convenience, we have had the figures of this present small treatise printed separately on a sheet, so that the said sheet, being unfolded, the figures can always be placed in view to facilitate understanding.

[End of Propositions]
I could have been briefer, but not clearer. End of the Spherical, Astronomical, & Geometrical Propositions, on the Use of the Astrolabe. D. A. L. G. Extract from the King's Privilege.

[Royal Privilege for Printing]
By grace and privilege of the King, Jean Moreau, bookseller in Paris, is permitted to print or have printed and engrave a Universal Astrolabe, and to sell and distribute a book entitled 'The Use of the Universal and Particular Astrolabe'. All booksellers, printers, engravers, and other persons of whatever quality or condition they may be, are forbidden to print, engrave, or have engraved, sell, or distribute the said book and instrument, unless they have been printed or engraved by the said Moreau, or with his consent, for a period of ten years, under the penalties stipulated by the said Privilege. Given in Paris this twenty-eighth of August 1621. Signed by the King in his Council, RENOUARD. Completed printing on April 8, 1625.
